Four Objections to the First Mood of Conditional Syllogism and Their Answers
Author presents and refutes four objections to the first mood of conditional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute common objections against the first mood of conditional syllogism using the author's logical responses.

Core Idea
The first mood's validity withstands four key objections through careful analysis of opposition, conversion, generality, and belief.

In this passage, the author defends the first mood of conditional syllogism against four sophisticated objections. Each objection targets a different aspect of the syllogism's validity, and each answer reveals a deeper logical principle. Understanding these responses strengthens your ability to use conditional reasoning in theological proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify each objection's core claim
Read each of the four objections (numbered [1], [ب], [ت], [ث]) and summarize the logical flaw each claims to find in the first mood.
Objection 1 concerns the unity of the middle term; objection 2 questions the necessity of the conclusion; objection 3 argues the conclusion is more general; objection 4 offers a counterexample.
2
Locate the author's answer and its logical basis
For each objection, find the corresponding answer (marked [1], [ب], [ت], [ث]) and identify the key logical principle the author uses to refute it.
For example, answer 1 relies on inference from incompatibility of concomitants; answer 2 on the convertibility of the negative; answer 3 on equality of conclusion and premises; answer 4 on the role of belief.
3
Assess whether the answer adequately addresses the objection
Consider each answer critically: does it fully resolve the objection, or does it assume something not proven? Compare with your own understanding of the first mood.
The author's answers are generally accepted in the tradition; they clarify the precise conditions under which the first mood operates.
4
Apply the same reasoning to a real kalam argument
Take a common theological proof that uses a conditional syllogism (e.g., the proof of the world's temporal origination) and consider how one of these objections might be raised and answered.
This step connects the abstract logical discussion to actual Islamic theological practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يورد عليها من الشبه أيضًا كما في الضرب الأول؛ وذلك بوجوه: [1] منها أن يقال إنه لا ينتج إلا وأن يكون الأوسط متحدا: وذلك في حيز المنع فإنه لا يكون متحدا إلا وأن يكون «ج د» في الصغرى في مقابلة ليس «ج د» في الكبرى؛ وليس كذلك فإن المطلق لا يكون في مقابلة المطلق. [ب] ومنها أن يقال إنه لا ينتج فإنه لا يلزم من صدق هاتين المقدمتين صدق النتيجة؛ وذلك لأنه إذا لزم فإما أن تكون النتيجة منعكسة، وذلك لا يمكن لما مر من قبل أن المطلقة تكون منعكسة أو لا تكون منعكسة، وذلك لا يمكن أيضًا فإنه يمكن أن يصدق نقيض ذلك العكس وهو قولنا قد يكون إذا كان «هـ ز» فـ «أ ب» فيصدق عكسه كذلك وهو قولنا قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «هز»، وإنه مما ينافي الصدق. [ت] ومنها أن يقال صدق النتيجة في هذه الصورة أعم من صدق المقدمتين لما أنه يلزم من الغير كما يلزم منه. ولو كان أعم منه فلا يفتقر إليه في التحقق؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن لا يفتقر ذلك إلى هذا أيضًا وإلا لكان الافتقار في أحد الجانبين منهما دون الآخر وذلك لا يمكن فإن الافتراق منهما في التحقق لا يخلو من أن يكون، أو لا يكون. [ث] ومنها أن يقال إنه لا يصح وإلا لكان منتجا في سائر الصور؛ وليس كذلك. فأما إذا قلنا كلما كان الإنسان فرسا كان متحركًا، وليس البتة إذا كان ساكنا كان متحركًا فإنه لا ينتج قولنا ليس البتة إذا كان فرسا كان ساكنا؛ وقد كان الفرس ساكناً في كثير من الأوقات.

أما الجواب فنقول: [1] في الأول منها الكلام في صورة يكون هذا في مقابلة ذلك لأنه هو الاستدلال بالتنافي بين اللازمين على التنافي بين الملزومين؛ وذلك لا يمكن إلا وأن يكون هذا في مقابلة ذلك. [ب] وفي الثاني نقول: قد مر من قبل أن السالبة في هذا الشكل لا تكون إلا وأن تكون منعكسة فالسالبة في الكبرى تكون منعكسة: وحينئذ يلزم أن تكون النتيجة كذلك. [ت] وفي الثالث نقول: لا يلزم من كونه لازما للعنوان أن يكون أعم من هذا في التحقق؛ وإنما يكون كذلك إن لو لم يكن ذلك الغير مساويا لهذا في التحقق؛ والحق فيما نحن فيه إنه يساويه فإن هذا لا يفارق ذلك في نفس الأمر. [ث] وفي الرابع نقول: قد مر من قبل أن مثل هذا القياس ينتج فإن من جمع بين هاتين المقدمتين على اعتقاد أن كل واحدة منهما حق يلزم أن يعتقد صدق النتيجة وحقيقتها كذلك كما إذا اعتقد كل واحدة منهما؛ وكان اعتقاده مطابقا للحقيقة.
Objections are also raised against it as in the first mood, in several aspects: [1] Among them is that it is said that it does not produce a conclusion unless the middle term is unified: but this is a point of denial, for it is not unified except if 'J-D' in the minor premise is in opposition to 'not J-D' in the major; but that is not so, because the absolute is not in opposition to the absolute. [ب] Among them is that it is said that it does not produce a conclusion because the truth of these two premises does not necessitate the truth of the conclusion; because if it did necessitate, then either the conclusion is convertible, and that is impossible due to what has passed, since the absolute proposition is either convertible or not convertible, and that is also impossible because the contradictory of that converse can be true, namely our statement 'it may be that if H-Z then A-B', so its converse is also true, namely our statement 'it may be that if A-B then H-Z', and that is contrary to truth. [ت] Among them is that it is said that the truth of the conclusion in this form is more general than the truth of the two premises, since it follows from something else as it follows from them. And if it is more general, it does not necessitate them in actuality; then it would follow that that does not necessitate this either, otherwise there would be necessitation in one side without the other, and that is impossible because the separation in actuality cannot be either existence or non-existence. [ث] Among them is that it is said that it is not valid, otherwise it would be productive in all forms; but it is not. For if we say 'whenever man is a horse, he is moving', and 'absolutely not if he is stationary, he is moving', it does not produce 'absolutely not if he is a horse, he is stationary'; and the horse is often stationary.

As for the answer, we say: [1] Regarding the first, the discussion is about the form in which this is in opposition to that, because it is inference from the incompatibility between two concomitants to the incompatibility between two things accompanied; and that is only possible if this is in opposition to that. [ب] Regarding the second, we say: it has passed before that the negative in this figure is only convertible, so the negative in the major is convertible; then it follows that the conclusion is likewise. [ت] Regarding the third, we say: it does not follow from its being a concomitant of the subject that it is more general than this in actuality; it would only be so if that other were not equal to this in actuality; and the truth in our case is that it equals it, for this does not separate from that in reality. [ث] Regarding the fourth, we say: it has passed before that such a syllogism produces a conclusion, for whoever combines these two premises with the belief that each of them is true, he must believe the truth of the conclusion and its realization likewise, just as if he believed each of them; and his belief corresponds to reality.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents four objections to the first mood of conditional syllogisms and their answers. The first objection concerns the unity of the middle term, arguing that the conclusion only follows if the middle term is unified, but here it is not because the absolute term in the minor is not opposed to the absolute in the major. The author denies the objection, stating that the inference relies on opposition of concomitants. The second objection questions the necessity of the conclusion, suggesting that the conclusion might be convertible and thus not necessary. The answer clarifies that the negative in this figure is convertible, so the conclusion is also convertible. The third objection argues that the conclusion is more general than the premises, so it does not depend on them. The answer explains that the conclusion is actually equal to the premises, not more general. The fourth objection provides a counterexample: 'whenever man is a horse, he is moving' and 'absolutely not if he is stationary, he is moving' should yield 'absolutely not if he is a horse, he is stationary,' but the horse is often stationary, so the conclusion fails. The answer states that such a syllogism does produce a conclusion, and if one believes the premises, one must believe the conclusion.

Checkpoint
What is the common thread in the author's responses to the four objections?
A) They all deny the premises.
B) They all affirm the necessity of the middle term.
C) They all clarify the conditions under which the first mood is valid.
D) They all reject the possibility of counterexamples.

Key Takeaway
The first mood of conditional syllogism is logically sound when its conditions are satisfied; objections often stem from misunderstanding these conditions, which the author resolves by clarifying the underlying logical principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the objection that a conditional syllogism of the first mood (modus tollens) only works if the consequent is denied correctly. Construct a valid conditional syllogism using an Islamic theological example (e.g., the proof of prophethood) and then formulate an objection similar to one of the four, along with the appropriate response using the author's reasoning.
?
In the fourth objection, the counterexample 'if man is a horse, he is moving' uses a false premise. Explain why this counterexample does not refute the syllogism's logical validity, referring to the author's answer about belief corresponding to reality.

Sophistry and Fallacies
intermediate

Unknowingly Combining Contradictory Premises
Author explains how one may unwittingly construct syllogisms from contradictory premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to detect when a syllogism is composed of contradictory premises without the reasoner's awareness, and trace how the contradictory premise arises from invalid syllogisms.

Core Idea
A reasoner can unwittingly deduce a contradictory premise from his own flawed syllogisms and combine it with a true premise.

In logic, it seems obvious that one would never use contradictory premises deliberately. However, classical logicians recognized that a person engaged in speculation may inadvertently construct syllogisms from contradictories. This happens when a true premise is combined with its negation, which the reasoner unknowingly derived from his own invalid syllogisms. In this lesson, we will analyze the mechanism of this error and learn how to detect it in our own reasoning and in dialectical debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the true premise
Find the premise that is actually true in reality. In the first example, the true premise is 'Some numbers are odd'.
2
Trace the source of the contradictory premise
Recognize that the contradictory premise (e.g., 'No number is odd') comes from the reasoner's invalid syllogisms. It is derived by combining the true premise with false premises from those invalid syllogisms.
3
Detect the contradictory pair
Confirm that the two premises are direct contradictories: one is 'Some S are P', the other is 'No S are P' (or equivalent). In the second example, 'All knowledge is opinion' contradicts 'Some knowledge is not opinion'.
4
Observe the resulting absurdity
From the two contradictories, any conclusion follows, including self-contradictory statements like 'Some numbers are not numbers' (second figure) or 'Some odd numbers are not odd' (third figure). This shows the syllogism is invalid.
5
Apply the check in dialectical contexts
Recognize that this error is common in jadal (dialectical debate) when the opponent concedes a false premise; the dialectician can combine it with a true premise to produce a refutation, but it may also occur unintentionally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم بأنه يمكن أن يعرض لأصحاب النظر الوقوف في استعمال القياسات المؤلفة من المتقابلات وهم لا يشعرون به ؛ وذلك حيث يكون عند إنسان ما قياسات فاسدة منتجة نتائج فاسدة ويكون في تلك القياسات أو تلك النتائج ما يلزم منه مقدمة فاسدة، ويمكن أن يساوي منهما أي إنتاج ضد ما يكون حق. وحينئذ ينتظم عنده من ذلك الحق ومن ضد ذلك القياس من متقابلتين من غير قصد منه إلى ذلك وشعور به مثلا إذا فرضت المقدّمة الصادقة وهي قولنا بعض الأعداد فرد ويكون عنده قياسات فاسدة لزم منها أو من نتائجها الفاسدة إن كل عدد منقسم بمتساويين؛ فإذا اكتسبت من هذ المقدمة بانضمام قولنا لا شيء من المنقسم بمتساويين بفرد قولنا لا شيء من الأعداد بفرد الذي هو نقيض تلك المقدمة الصادقة. وحينئذ يكون النظر واقعا على مقدمتين متناقضين إحديهما قولنا بعض الأعداد فرد، والثانية قولنا ولا شيء من الأعداد بفرد؛ وإنهما ينتجان من الشكل الثاني بعض الأعداد ليس بعدد، ومن الثالث بعض الأفراد ليس بفرد. ويمكن أن يكون وقوعه على القياس المؤلف من متقابلتين؛ وذلك بأن يكون في القياسات الفاسدة عنده أو في نتائجه مقدمة هي مقابلة لجنس المقدمة الصادقة أو لجزئي تحتها. فإذا خفي الحال كانت تلك المقدمة مقابلة للمقدِّمة الصادقة لما أنه يمكن أن يستنتج منها، ومن مقدمة أخرى مقابل المقدّمة الصادقة مثلا إذا فرضنا أن المقدمة الصادقة قولنا كل علم ظن ثمَّ لزم من أصول آخر كانت عنده أن الطب ليس بظن فإن هذه المقدمة مقابلة لجزئي من المقدّمة الصادقة ضرورة أن الطبّ علم ويلزم منه. ومن قولنا كل علم ظن قياس من الشكل الثاني منتج لقولنا بعض العلم ليس بظن الذي هو نقيض المقدّمة الصادقة: فقد وقع النظر أيضًا على قياس مؤلف من متقابلتين في الثاني منتج لقولنا بعض العلم ليس بعلم. وعلى هذا فإنه يمكن أن تكون المقدّمة الموضوعة باطلة والقياسات منتجة لما يقابلها أو تكون عنده قياسات صحيحة وقياسات فاسدة، فاكتسب من الصحيحة مقدّمة صادقة، ومن الفاسدة ما يقابلها. وكثيرًا ما يستعمل في الجدليات بهذا الطريق لما أن المقدمة المسلمة عند الخصم باطلة في كثير من الصور مؤلف منها ومن مقدّمة صادقة ما يلزم منه إلزام الخصم؛ وهذا ظاهر.
And know that it is possible for those engaged in speculation to inadvertently use syllogisms composed of contradictories without being aware of it. That is when a person has some invalid syllogisms producing invalid results, and in those syllogisms or their results there is something that entails a false premise, and it is possible that from them [the true premise and the false one] there equalizes any production of the opposite of what is true. Then there is organized for him from that truth and from the opposite of that syllogism from two contradictories without his intending that or being aware of it. For example, if the true premise is assumed to be 'Some numbers are odd' and he has invalid syllogisms from which it follows or from their invalid results that 'Every number is divisible into two equal halves', then if you acquire from this premise by adding 'No divisible into two equal halves is odd' the statement 'No number is odd' which is the contradictory of that true premise. Then the consideration falls upon two contradictory premises: one is 'Some numbers are odd', and the second is 'No number is odd'; and they produce from the second figure 'Some numbers are not numbers', and from the third 'Some odd numbers are not odd'. And it is possible that his falling upon the syllogism composed of two contradictories is such that in his invalid syllogisms or their results there is a premise that is the opposite of the genus of the true premise or of a particular under it. Then if the matter is hidden, that premise becomes the opposite of the true premise since it is possible to deduce from it and from another premise the opposite of the true premise. For example, if we suppose the true premise is 'All knowledge is opinion', then from other principles he has, it follows that 'Medicine is not opinion'. Then this premise is the opposite of a particular of the true premise, necessarily because medicine is knowledge and it follows from it. And from the statement 'All knowledge is opinion' a syllogism of the second figure produces 'Some knowledge is not opinion' which is the contradictory of the true premise. Then the consideration also falls upon a syllogism composed of two contradictories in the second figure producing 'Some knowledge is not knowledge'. And in this way, it is possible that the assumed premise is false and the syllogisms produce its opposite, or he has correct syllogisms and invalid syllogisms, so he acquires from the correct ones a true premise and from the invalid ones its opposite. And this is often used in dialectics (jadal) because the premise conceded by the opponent is false in many forms, and from it and a true premise there results what necessitates a refutation of the opponent. And this is clear.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage warns that a reasoner may unknowingly combine a true premise with its contradictory derived from his own flawed syllogisms, thereby falling into a syllogism composed of contradictory premises. The author illustrates with two examples. First, if the true premise is 'Some numbers are odd', and the reasoner's invalid syllogisms lead to 'Every number is divisible into equal halves', then adding 'No divisible into equal halves is odd' yields 'No number is odd'—the contradictory of the true premise. From these two contradictory premises, the second figure produces 'Some numbers are not numbers' and the third figure produces 'Some odd numbers are not odd', absurd conclusions. Second, if the true premise is 'All knowledge is opinion', and from other principles the reasoner deduces 'Medicine is not opinion' (which contradicts a particular instance), then combining 'All knowledge is opinion' with the contradictory of that particular yields a syllogism of the second figure producing 'Some knowledge is not opinion'—the contradictory of the original true premise. Thus the reasoner ends up with a syllogism composed of two contradictories, producing 'Some knowledge is not knowledge'. The author notes that this phenomenon often occurs in dialectical contexts where the opponent's conceded premise is false, and combining it with a true premise leads to a refutation. The key insight is that such errors can happen without the reasoner's awareness, due to the hiddenness of the relationships between premises.

Key Terms
المتقابلات
القياسات الفاسدة
المقدمة الصادقة

Checkpoint
In the first example, from the contradictory premises 'Some numbers are odd' and 'No number is odd', what conclusion does the author say is produced from the second figure?
A) Some numbers are not numbers
B) Some odd numbers are not odd
C) No number is odd
D) All numbers are odd

Key Takeaway
The essential insight is that contradictory premises can arise inadvertently from a mix of true and false premises, leading to invalid and absurd conclusions, and this must be checked in rigorous reasoning and dialectic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a debate where the true premise is 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (kullu muskiri ḥarām). The opponent concedes 'Nabidh is not forbidden' (nabidh is a date drink). But nabidh is an intoxicant. Trace how combining these could lead to a syllogism from contradictory premises and what absurd conclusion would result.
?
Analyze the following scenario: A person has the true premise 'All animals are living beings' and invalid syllogisms that lead to 'No horse is a living being'. Show how the contradictory premises 'Some animals are horses' (implicit) and 'No horse is a living being' combine to produce an invalid syllogism.

Sophistry and Fallacies

What is Begging the Question?
Author defines begging the question and contrasts it with a related fallacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of begging the question (muṣādara) and distinguish it from the related fallacy of placing a non-cause as a cause.

Core Idea
Begging the question uses the conclusion itself as a premise.

The author defines a critical fallacy: assuming what you are trying to prove. This lesson unpacks that definition and compares it with a nearby error. Understanding this distinction is essential for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Locate the term being defined
Find 'al-muṣādara ʿalā l-maṭlūb al-awwal' in the Arabic. Note that it is introduced with 'أما' (as for), signalling a new topic.
2
Extract the definition
The author says it is 'placing the desired conclusion itself as a premise to demonstrate itself.' Identify the phrase 'وضع المطلوب بنفسه مقدمة لبيان نفسه'.
The repetition of 'nafs' emphasizes self-reference.
3
Compare with the nearby fallacy
The author contrasts it with 'placing what is not a cause as a cause in the syllogism.' Note the similarity (both involve misplacing something) and the difference (the former uses the conclusion; the latter uses a non-cause).
The author says it is 'close but not exactly it.'
4
Test the definition
Consider an argument: 'The Quran is eternal because it is the speech of Allah, and the speech of Allah is eternal.' Does this commit begging the question? Check if the conclusion is used as a premise.
Islamic example: the conclusion 'the Quran is eternal' appears as a premise in the second statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المصادرة على المطلوب الأول فإنها عبارة عن وضع المطلوب بنفسه مقدمة لبيان نفسه، وأما وضع ما ليس بعلة علة في القياس فذلك قريب منه لا هو بعينه. وإنها أي المصادرة على المطلوب الأول داخلة في جنس ما لم يبرهن بما قيل؛ ولكن ذلك الجنس أعم منه لأن ما لم يبرهن بما قيل قد يكون بسبب أن القول غير منتج لشكله أو لمقدمة من مقدماته أو لما أن المقدمات أخفى من المطلوب، أو مساويه في المعرفة، أو مفتقرة في البيان إلى بيان المطلوب؛ وليس شيء منها مصادرة على المطلوب الأول في قياس منتج الشكل. ولا يجب أن تكون المقدمة أخفى من المطلوب، أو مساوية له حتى إذا كان كذلك كانت المصادرة على المطلوب. وذلك لأن الخفي والمساوي في الجهالة قد يكون غير المطلوب، ولا يكون مصادرة على المطلوب. فأما في المصادرة فلا يكون غير المطلوب بل إنما يكون القياس مصادرة على المطلوب إذا كان المطلوب نفسه مقدمة لبيان نفسه بأن يدل أحد جزأيه الذي يراد أن يجعله حدا
As for begging the question (al-muṣādara ʿalā l-maṭlūb al-awwal), it is the placing of the desired conclusion itself as a premise to demonstrate itself. As for placing what is not a cause as a cause in the syllogism, that is close to it but not exactly it. And indeed it (i.e., begging the question) falls under the genus of 'what is not demonstrated by what is said' (mā lam yubrahin bi-mā qīla). But that genus is more general than it, because 'what is not demonstrated by what is said' may be due to the statement not producing its valid form, or due to a premise among its premises, or because the premises are more hidden than the desired conclusion, or equal to it in knowledge, or in need in their demonstration of the demonstration of the desired conclusion. And none of these is begging the question in a syllogism that produces a valid form. Nor must the premise be more hidden than the desired conclusion, or equal to it, such that if it were so it would be begging the question. That is because the hidden and the equal in ignorance may be other than the desired conclusion, and it is not begging the question. As for begging the question, it is not other than the desired conclusion. Rather, the syllogism is begging the question only when the desired conclusion itself is a premise to demonstrate itself, by indicating one of its two parts which is intended to be made a limit (ḥadd).
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage defines the fallacy of begging the question (muṣādara ʿalā l-maṭlūb al-awwal). The author states it is: placing the very desired conclusion as a premise to demonstrate itself. He distinguishes it from a related fallacy: placing a non-cause as a cause in the syllogism, which is close but not identical. Begging the question is a species of the broader genus 'what is not demonstrated by what is said' (mā lam yubrahin bi-mā qīla). This genus also includes other defects: (1) the statement does not produce the correct form, (2) a premise is missing, (3) premises are more hidden than the conclusion, (4) premises are equal in obscurity to the conclusion, (5) premises require the conclusion for their own proof. None of these is begging the question if the syllogism has a valid mood. The author emphasizes that the mere fact that a premise is more hidden or equally obscure does not make it begging the question, because such a premise could be something other than the desired conclusion. True begging the question occurs only when the desired conclusion itself is used as a premise, specifically by taking one of its two parts (i.e., either the subject or predicate) as a term in the premise, essentially assuming what needs to be proved.

Key Terms
المصادرة على المطلوب الأول
وضع المطلوب بنفسه مقدمة
وضع ما ليس بعلة علة

Which of the following is an example of begging the question according to the passage?
A) Using a premise that is more hidden than the conclusion.
B) Using the desired conclusion itself as a premise.
C) Using a premise that is equal in obscurity.
D) Using a non-cause as a cause.

Key Takeaway
Begging the question is uniquely defined by the self-referential use of the conclusion as a premise, not by obscurity or misattributed causality.

Distinguishing Begging the Question from Other Failures
Author places begging the question within a broader genus and distinguishes it from other failures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a demonstrative failure as either begging the question or as another type (form, missing premise, obscurity, etc.) using the author's genus-species framework.

Core Idea
Begging the question is only one species in a broader genus of failed demonstrations.

The author places begging the question under the genus 'what is not demonstrated by what is said' (mā lam yubrahin bi-mā qīla). This lesson explores that genus and its other species, showing clearly what begging the question is not. Mastering this taxonomy helps you diagnose arguments more precisely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus
Find the phrase 'ما لم يبرهن بما قيل' (what is not demonstrated by what is said). Note that the author says begging the question is included in this genus, but the genus is more general.
The genus covers all syllogisms that fail to prove their conclusion.
2
List the other species
The author enumerates four other causes of failure: (1) the statement does not produce a valid form (shakl), (2) a premise is missing, (3) the premises are more hidden (akhfā) than the conclusion, (4) the premises are equal in obscurity (musāwin), (5) the premises require the conclusion for their own proof. None of these is begging the question if the syllogism has a valid form.
The last one (premises needing the conclusion) might seem close, but it is not identical.
3
Understand why they are not begging the question
The author argues that hiddenness or equality in ignorance does not automatically make it begging the question, because the premise could be something other than the desired conclusion. For true begging the question, the premise must be the conclusion itself.
Key distinction: the content of the premise, not its epistemic status.
4
Apply the taxonomy
Given a flawed syllogism, decide which species it belongs to. For example: 'Ali is a man; all men are mortal; therefore Ali is mortal' – this is valid. But if we say 'Ali is mortal because all men are mortal, and Ali is a man' – that's fine. Now consider a flawed one: 'The soul is immaterial because it is not material' – this might be a form issue or obscurity.
Islamic examples: using a premise like 'The Quran is miraculous because it is the word of Allah' could be begging the question if the premise assumes its divine origin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المصادرة على المطلوب الأول فإنها عبارة عن وضع المطلوب بنفسه مقدمة لبيان نفسه، وأما وضع ما ليس بعلة علة في القياس فذلك قريب منه لا هو بعينه. وإنها أي المصادرة على المطلوب الأول داخلة في جنس ما لم يبرهن بما قيل؛ ولكن ذلك الجنس أعم منه لأن ما لم يبرهن بما قيل قد يكون بسبب أن القول غير منتج لشكله أو لمقدمة من مقدماته أو لما أن المقدمات أخفى من المطلوب، أو مساويه في المعرفة، أو مفتقرة في البيان إلى بيان المطلوب؛ وليس شيء منها مصادرة على المطلوب الأول في قياس منتج الشكل. ولا يجب أن تكون المقدمة أخفى من المطلوب، أو مساوية له حتى إذا كان كذلك كانت المصادرة على المطلوب. وذلك لأن الخفي والمساوي في الجهالة قد يكون غير المطلوب، ولا يكون مصادرة على المطلوب. فأما في المصادرة فلا يكون غير المطلوب بل إنما يكون القياس مصادرة على المطلوب إذا كان المطلوب نفسه مقدمة لبيان نفسه بأن يدل أحد جزأيه الذي يراد أن يجعله حدا
As for begging the question (al-muṣādara ʿalā l-maṭlūb al-awwal), it is the placing of the desired conclusion itself as a premise to demonstrate itself. As for placing what is not a cause as a cause in the syllogism, that is close to it but not exactly it. And indeed it (i.e., begging the question) falls under the genus of 'what is not demonstrated by what is said' (mā lam yubrahin bi-mā qīla). But that genus is more general than it, because 'what is not demonstrated by what is said' may be due to the statement not producing its valid form, or due to a premise among its premises, or because the premises are more hidden than the desired conclusion, or equal to it in knowledge, or in need in their demonstration of the demonstration of the desired conclusion. And none of these is begging the question in a syllogism that produces a valid form. Nor must the premise be more hidden than the desired conclusion, or equal to it, such that if it were so it would be begging the question. That is because the hidden and the equal in ignorance may be other than the desired conclusion, and it is not begging the question. As for begging the question, it is not other than the desired conclusion. Rather, the syllogism is begging the question only when the desired conclusion itself is a premise to demonstrate itself, by indicating one of its two parts which is intended to be made a limit (ḥadd).
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage defines the fallacy of begging the question (muṣādara ʿalā l-maṭlūb al-awwal). The author states it is: placing the very desired conclusion as a premise to demonstrate itself. He distinguishes it from a related fallacy: placing a non-cause as a cause in the syllogism, which is close but not identical. Begging the question is a species of the broader genus 'what is not demonstrated by what is said' (mā lam yubrahin bi-mā qīla). This genus also includes other defects: (1) the statement does not produce the correct form, (2) a premise is missing, (3) premises are more hidden than the conclusion, (4) premises are equal in obscurity to the conclusion, (5) premises require the conclusion for their own proof. None of these is begging the question if the syllogism has a valid mood. The author emphasizes that the mere fact that a premise is more hidden or equally obscure does not make it begging the question, because such a premise could be something other than the desired conclusion. True begging the question occurs only when the desired conclusion itself is used as a premise, specifically by taking one of its two parts (i.e., either the subject or predicate) as a term in the premise, essentially assuming what needs to be proved.

Key Terms
ما لم يبرهن بما قيل
غير منتج لشكله
أخفى من المطلوب
مساوي في المعرفة
مفتقر في البيان إلى بيان المطلوب

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a cause for a syllogism to be 'not demonstrated by what is said'?
A) The statement does not produce its valid form.
B) A premise is missing.
C) The conclusion is more hidden than the premises.
D) The premises are more hidden than the desired conclusion.

Key Takeaway
Begging the question is a specific species of demonstrative failure defined by using the conclusion as a premise; all other failures belong to different species under the same genus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following argument: 'It is obligatory to pray five times because the Quran commands prayer, and whatever the Quran commands is obligatory.' Identify whether this is begging the question or another failure from the genus. Explain using the author's categories.
?
List a real example of each species from the genus (form failure, missing premise, obscurity, equality, need for conclusion) using Islamic topics such as the attributes of Allah or rulings on transactions.

Recognizing Begging the Question in Simple Syllogisms
Author defines begging the question and gives simple examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a case of begging the question in a single categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Begging the question hides the conclusion in a premise.

The passage defines the fallacy where the conclusion itself is used as a premise. In simple syllogisms, this occurs when one premise is identical in meaning to the conclusion. Let's learn how to spot this common error.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion (matlab)
Find the statement that the syllogism aims to prove. For example, 'every human is a laugher' in the author's example.
2
Check each premise against the conclusion
Examine whether either premise says exactly the same thing as the conclusion, just with different words. In the author's example, the major premise 'every mortal is a laugher' is identical to the conclusion.
3
Confirm the middle term's role
In a valid syllogism, the middle term should be a distinct concept linking the premises. Here, the middle term 'mortal' appears in both premises, but the major premise already contains the conclusion's predicate.
4
Determine if the argument is circular
If the premise is essentially the conclusion, the argument proves nothing new. That is begging the question.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في المصادرة على المطلوب الأول ]
وأما المصادرة على المطلوب الأول فإنها عبارة عن وضع المطلوب بنفسه مقدمة لبيان نفسه، وأما وضع ما ليس بعلة علة في القياس فذلك قريب منه لا هو بعينه. وإنها أي المصادرة على المطلوب الأول داخلة في جنس ما لم يبرهن بما قيل؛ ولكن ذلك الجنس أعم منه لأن ما لم يبرهن بما قيل قد يكون بسبب أن القول غير منتج لشكله أو لمقدمة من مقدماته أو لما أن المقدمات أخفى من المطلوب، أو مساويه في المعرفة، أو مفتقرة في البيان إلى بيان المطلوب؛ وليس شيء منها مصادرة على المطلوب الأول في قياس منتج الشكل. ولا يجب أن تكون المقدمة أخفى من المطلوب، أو مساوية له حتى إذا كان كذلك كانت المصادرة على المطلوب. وذلك لأن الخفي والمساوي في الجهالة قد يكون غير المطلوب، ولا يكون مصادرة على المطلوب. فأما في المصادرة فلا يكون غير المطلوب بل إنما يكون القياس مصادرة على المطلوب إذا كان المطلوب نفسه مقدمة لبيان نفسه بأن يدل أحد جزأيه الذي يراد أن يجعله حدا أوسط بما يراد فيه احتيالا في الجدل. وإنما يكون كذلك إذا كان المطلوب من شأنه أن لا يكون بينا بنفسه فإن الأشياء البيئة بنفسها لا يقاس عليها؛ وقد يرد ذلك في قياس واحد كما في قولنا كل إنسان بشر وكل بشر ضحاك فكل إنسان ضحاك؛ فالكبرى والمطلوب شيء واحد في المعنى.
وكذلك إذا قلنا كل ضحاك بشر وكل بشر إنسان فكل ضحاك إنسان؛ إذ الصغرى المطلوب شيء واحد ; وقل ما يخفى هذا إلا على ضعيف العقول. وقد يعرض في قياسات فوق واحدة بأن تكون نتيجة تبين بمقدمة غير بينة بنفسها، وتلك المقدمة تبين بمقدمة أخرى، وتلك المقدمة إنما تبين بصحة النتيجة؛ فيكون هذا أيضًا مصادرة على المطلوب الأول بوسائط كما قيل في كتاب إقليديس إن الخطوط المتوارية إذا وقع عليها خط قاطع كانت الزوايا التي كذا وكذا، ومن مقدمات برهان ذلك المطلوب أن الخطين اللذين في سطح واحد إذا وقع عليها خط صادف الزاويتين اللتين من جهة واحدة معادلتين القائمتين لم تلتقيا. فإن رام أحد أن يبين هذا بأن يقول إنهما إذا التقيا كان مثلث من الخطين الواقع، وكانت الزوايا الثلاث أعظم من قائمتين؛ هذا خلف. فإذن لا يلتقيان فقد صادر على المطلوب الأول من حيث لم يشعر لأن كون زوايا المثلث بهذه الحالة إنما يبرهن بعد صحة ذلك يعني بعد صحة قولنا إنه إذا وقع على الخطوط المتوارية خط قاطع كانت الزاويتين المتعادلتين متساويتين. والخارجة مساوية لما يقابلها وهي الداخلة فتعرف حال الزوايا في الخطين بزوايا المثلث، وحال زوايا المثلث بحال زوايا الخطين فيكون استعمال كون الزوايا في الخطين مع الخط الواقع عليها مقدمة في بيان نفسه بوسائط ; وإنه هو المصادرة على المطلوب الأول، ولا يظن أن الإنسان إذا وضع في بيان المطلوب الأول مقدّمة يشارك المطلوب الأول في المحمول وموضوعها مع
Chapter : [ On Begging the Question (petitio principii) ]
As for begging the question, it is putting the desired conclusion itself as a premise to prove itself. As for putting what is not a cause as a cause in the syllogism, that is close to it but not the same. And indeed it, i.e., begging the question, falls under the genus of 'what is not proven by what was said'. But that genus is more general than it, because what is not proven by what was said may be due to the statement not producing its figure, or a premise among its premises, or because the premises are more hidden than the desired conclusion, or equal to it in knowledge, or in need of the desired conclusion's proof for their own proof. None of these is begging the question in a correctly-formed syllogism. And it is not necessary that the premise be more hidden than the desired conclusion, or equal to it, such that if it is so, then it is begging the question. That is because the hidden and the equal in ignorance may be other than the desired conclusion, and it is not begging the question. As for begging the question, it is not other than the desired conclusion. Rather, the syllogism is begging the question only when the desired conclusion itself is a premise to prove itself, by indicating one of its two parts that is intended to be made the middle term, with what is intended therein as deception in dialectic. And it is only so if the desired conclusion is such that it is not self-evident. For self-evident things are not the subject of syllogism. This may occur in a single syllogism, as in our saying: 'Every human is a mortal; every mortal is a laugher; so every human is a laugher.' For the major premise and the desired conclusion are one in meaning.
Likewise if we say: 'Every laugher is a mortal; every mortal is a human; so every laugher is a human.' For the minor premise and the desired conclusion are one thing. And rarely is this hidden except to one weak of intellect. It may also occur in more than one syllogism, such that a result is proven by a premise that is not self-evident, and that premise is proven by another premise, and that premise is only proven by the truth of the result. This too is begging the question through intermediaries, as is said in the book of Euclid: that parallel lines, when a transversal line falls on them, produce certain angles. Among the premises for the proof of that desired conclusion is that two lines in one plane, when a line falls on them meeting the two angles on one side equal to two right angles, do not meet. If someone attempts to prove this by saying that if they met, a triangle would be formed by the two lines and the transversal, and the three angles would be greater than two right angles – this is a reductio ad absurdum. Then they do not meet. He has begged the question without realizing it, because the fact that the angles of the triangle are in this state is only proved after the truth of that, i.e., after the truth of our saying: 'If a transversal line falls on parallel lines, the alternate angles are equal.' And the external angle equals the opposite interior angle. So the state of the angles in the two lines is known by the angles of the triangle, and the state of the angles of the triangle by the state of the angles of the two lines. Thus he uses the state of the angles in the two lines with the transversal as a premise to prove itself through intermediaries. And that is begging the question. And one should not think that when one puts a premise in proving the first desired conclusion that shares with the first desired conclusion in its predicate and subject...
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the logical fallacy known as 'begging the question' (al-musadara 'ala al-matlab al-awwal). The author begins by defining it: it occurs when the very conclusion one wants to prove is placed as a premise for its own proof. He distinguishes it from a related error—putting what is not a cause as a cause—which is close but not identical. The fallacy falls under the broader category of 'what is not proven by what was said', which includes other defects such as improper mood, hidden premises, or premises equal in obscurity to the conclusion. However, none of those constitute begging the question unless the conclusion itself appears as a premise. The author clarifies that hiddenness or equal ignorance alone does not make it begging the question; the premise must be the very same proposition as the conclusion. He then gives two simple examples in a single syllogism: 'Every human is a mortal; every mortal is a laugher; so every human is a laugher' (where the major premise is identical in meaning to the conclusion), and similarly 'Every laugher is a mortal; every mortal is a human; so every laugher is a human' (where the minor premise is identical). He notes this is obvious except to the weak-minded. Next, he treats a more subtle case involving multiple syllogisms, drawing from Euclid's geometry on parallel lines. The desired conclusion is about angles formed when a transversal intersects parallel lines. A common proof uses a reductio ad absurdum: assuming the lines meet, forming a triangle whose angles exceed two right angles, which is impossible. The author points out that this reasoning actually presupposes the very property being proved—namely, that alternate angles are equal—which itself depends on the concept of parallels. Thus, it becomes a circular argument through intermediaries. The passage ends by warning that one should not mistake a premise that shares only the subject or predicate with the conclusion for true begging the question; the identity must be complete.

Key Terms
مصادرة على المطلوب الأول
ما لم يبرهن بما قيل
حد أوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Consider the syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore every nabidh is forbidden.' Is this an example of begging the question (musadara)?
A) Yes, because the major premise ('every intoxicant is forbidden') is the same as the conclusion in meaning.
B) No, because the conclusion is different from both premises and the reasoning is valid.
C) Yes, because the conclusion is already assumed in the minor premise.
D) No, because the syllogism is in the first figure and thus always valid.

Key Takeaway
Begging the question occurs only when the conclusion is used as a premise, not when premises are unclear or equal in ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which premise in the syllogism 'Every intoxicant is haram; every nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore every nabidh is haram' is NOT begging the question. Explain why.
?
Construct a valid categorical syllogism on the proposition 'Prayer is obligatory' that does NOT commit begging the question. Use distinct premises.

Detecting Circular Proofs in Complex Arguments
Author gives a complex example of begging the question from Euclid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify begging the question in a chain of multiple syllogisms (through intermediaries).

Core Idea
Circularity can hide across several steps, not just one.

The passage shows that begging the question can occur through a series of syllogisms, as in Euclid's geometric proof. This lesson will help you trace such circular chains back to the original assumption.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ultimate conclusion (matlab awwal)
In Euclid's example, the ultimate conclusion is about angles formed when a transversal intersects parallel lines.
2
Map the sequence of syllogisms
The proof attempts to show that if two lines do not meet (are parallel), then certain angle relationships hold. The reasoning uses a reductio ad absurdum assuming the lines meet, forming a triangle.
3
Find where a premise depends on the conclusion
The author points out that the property of triangle angles used in the reductio is itself proved using the parallel line property. Thus, the proof uses the conclusion as a premise in an earlier step.
4
Recognize the circular dependency
If premise A is needed to prove B, and B is needed to prove A, then the reasoning is circular. The Euclid example shows this: the angle property of parallel lines is used to prove itself via the triangle argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وكذلك إذا قلنا كل ضحاك بشر وكل بشر إنسان فكل ضحاك إنسان؛ إذ الصغرى المطلوب شيء واحد ; وقل ما يخفى هذا إلا على ضعيف العقول. وقد يعرض في قياسات فوق واحدة بأن تكون نتيجة تبين بمقدمة غير بينة بنفسها، وتلك المقدمة تبين بمقدمة أخرى، وتلك المقدمة إنما تبين بصحة النتيجة؛ فيكون هذا أيضًا مصادرة على المطلوب الأول بوسائط كما قيل في كتاب إقليديس إن الخطوط المتوارية إذا وقع عليها خط قاطع كانت الزوايا التي كذا وكذا، ومن مقدمات برهان ذلك المطلوب أن الخطين اللذين في سطح واحد إذا وقع عليها خط صادف الزاويتين اللتين من جهة واحدة معادلتين القائمتين لم تلتقيا. فإن رام أحد أن يبين هذا بأن يقول إنهما إذا التقيا كان مثلث من الخطين الواقع، وكانت الزوايا الثلاث أعظم من قائمتين؛ هذا خلف. فإذن لا يلتقيان فقد صادر على المطلوب الأول من حيث لم يشعر لأن كون زوايا المثلث بهذه الحالة إنما يبرهن بعد صحة ذلك يعني بعد صحة قولنا إنه إذا وقع على الخطوط المتوارية خط قاطع كانت الزاويتين المتعادلتين متساويتين. والخارجة مساوية لما يقابلها وهي الداخلة فتعرف حال الزوايا في الخطين بزوايا المثلث، وحال زوايا المثلث بحال زوايا الخطين فيكون استعمال كون الزوايا في الخطين مع الخط الواقع عليها مقدمة في بيان نفسه بوسائط ; وإنه هو المصادرة على المطلوب الأول، ولا يظن أن الإنسان إذا وضع في بيان المطلوب الأول مقدّمة يشارك المطلوب الأول في المحمول وموضوعها مع
Chapter : [ On Begging the Question (petitio principii) ]
As for begging the question, it is putting the desired conclusion itself as a premise to prove itself. As for putting what is not a cause as a cause in the syllogism, that is close to it but not the same. And indeed it, i.e., begging the question, falls under the genus of 'what is not proven by what was said'. But that genus is more general than it, because what is not proven by what was said may be due to the statement not producing its figure, or a premise among its premises, or because the premises are more hidden than the desired conclusion, or equal to it in knowledge, or in need of the desired conclusion's proof for their own proof. None of these is begging the question in a correctly-formed syllogism. And it is not necessary that the premise be more hidden than the desired conclusion, or equal to it, such that if it is so, then it is begging the question. That is because the hidden and the equal in ignorance may be other than the desired conclusion, and it is not begging the question. As for begging the question, it is not other than the desired conclusion. Rather, the syllogism is begging the question only when the desired conclusion itself is a premise to prove itself, by indicating one of its two parts that is intended to be made the middle term, with what is intended therein as deception in dialectic. And it is only so if the desired conclusion is such that it is not self-evident. For self-evident things are not the subject of syllogism. This may occur in a single syllogism, as in our saying: 'Every human is a mortal; every mortal is a laugher; so every human is a laugher.' For the major premise and the desired conclusion are one in meaning.
Likewise if we say: 'Every laugher is a mortal; every mortal is a human; so every laugher is a human.' For the minor premise and the desired conclusion are one thing. And rarely is this hidden except to one weak of intellect. It may also occur in more than one syllogism, such that a result is proven by a premise that is not self-evident, and that premise is proven by another premise, and that premise is only proven by the truth of the result. This too is begging the question through intermediaries, as is said in the book of Euclid: that parallel lines, when a transversal line falls on them, produce certain angles. Among the premises for the proof of that desired conclusion is that two lines in one plane, when a line falls on them meeting the two angles on one side equal to two right angles, do not meet. If someone attempts to prove this by saying that if they met, a triangle would be formed by the two lines and the transversal, and the three angles would be greater than two right angles – this is a reductio ad absurdum. Then they do not meet. He has begged the question without realizing it, because the fact that the angles of the triangle are in this state is only proved after the truth of that, i.e., after the truth of our saying: 'If a transversal line falls on parallel lines, the alternate angles are equal.' And the external angle equals the opposite interior angle. So the state of the angles in the two lines is known by the angles of the triangle, and the state of the angles of the triangle by the state of the angles of the two lines. Thus he uses the state of the angles in the two lines with the transversal as a premise to prove itself through intermediaries. And that is begging the question. And one should not think that when one puts a premise in proving the first desired conclusion that shares with the first desired conclusion in its predicate and subject...
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the logical fallacy known as 'begging the question' (al-musadara 'ala al-matlab al-awwal). The author begins by defining it: it occurs when the very conclusion one wants to prove is placed as a premise for its own proof. He distinguishes it from a related error—putting what is not a cause as a cause—which is close but not identical. The fallacy falls under the broader category of 'what is not proven by what was said', which includes other defects such as improper mood, hidden premises, or premises equal in obscurity to the conclusion. However, none of those constitute begging the question unless the conclusion itself appears as a premise. The author clarifies that hiddenness or equal ignorance alone does not make it begging the question; the premise must be the very same proposition as the conclusion. He then gives two simple examples in a single syllogism: 'Every human is a mortal; every mortal is a laugher; so every human is a laugher' (where the major premise is identical in meaning to the conclusion), and similarly 'Every laugher is a mortal; every mortal is a human; so every laugher is a human' (where the minor premise is identical). He notes this is obvious except to the weak-minded. Next, he treats a more subtle case involving multiple syllogisms, drawing from Euclid's geometry on parallel lines. The desired conclusion is about angles formed when a transversal intersects parallel lines. A common proof uses a reductio ad absurdum: assuming the lines meet, forming a triangle whose angles exceed two right angles, which is impossible. The author points out that this reasoning actually presupposes the very property being proved—namely, that alternate angles are equal—which itself depends on the concept of parallels. Thus, it becomes a circular argument through intermediaries. The passage ends by warning that one should not mistake a premise that shares only the subject or predicate with the conclusion for true begging the question; the identity must be complete.

Key Terms
مصادرة على المطلوب الأول
ما لم يبرهن بما قيل
حد أوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the proof uses a reductio ad absurdum, which is always fallacious.
B) Because the property of triangle angles used in the reductio itself depends on the parallel postulate being true.
C) Because the author of the proof was unaware of geometry.
D) Because parallel lines are self-evident and do not need proof.

Key Takeaway
Begging the question can hide in a chain of syllogisms where each step depends on the ultimate conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Analyze this argument: 'The Quran is the word of God because it is miraculous; it is miraculous because it contains prophecies; the prophecies are true because it is the word of God.' Identify the circular dependency and explain which premise assumes the conclusion.

Understanding Circular Reasoning (Musadara 'ala al-Matlub al-Awwal)
Author defines circular reasoning, distinguishes from term reversal, and illustrates with Euclidean geometry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify circular reasoning in a syllogism and distinguish it from valid arguments and simple term reversal.

Core Idea
Circular reasoning assumes what it tries to prove, hiding the conclusion inside the premises.

This passage from the logic text explains the fallacy of musadara, where the conclusion is implicitly or explicitly used as a premise. This undermines the purpose of proof, as it offers no new knowledge. Understanding this fallacy is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences, especially in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh, where rigorous demonstration is required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Find where the author defines circular reasoning. The definition is at the beginning: it occurs when the sought (matlub) is not self-evident and is assumed in the premises.
Self-evident things do not need proof, so circularity only applies to non-evident claims.
2
Examine the simple examples
Look at the two syllogisms: 'Every human is a mortal, every mortal is risible, so every human is risible' and the reversed version. Note that in each, one premise is equivalent to the conclusion in meaning, making the argument circular.
The author notes this is obvious except to the weak-minded.
3
Recognize hidden circularity
Study the Euclidean geometry example. See how a premise about parallel lines is used to prove itself through the triangle argument, creating a loop over multiple steps.
This shows that circularity can be subtle and require careful analysis to detect.
4
Distinguish from term reversal
Understand that simply swapping subject and predicate (e.g., 'human' and 'risible') is not circular because the subjects differ in meaning. Also, using a universal and its particular is not circular.
The middle term must be identical to a term in the conclusion in a problematic way for it to be musadara.

أوسط بما يراد فيه احتيالا في الجدل. وإنما يكون كذلك إذا كان المطلوب من شأنه أن لا يكون بينا بنفسه فإن الأشياء البيئة بنفسها لا يقاس عليها؛ وقد يرد ذلك في قياس واحد كما في قولنا كل إنسان بشر وكل بشر فوق واحدة بأن تكون نتيجة تبين بمقدمة غير بينة بنفسها، وتلك المقدمة تبين بمقدمة أخرى، وتلك المقدمة إنما تبين بصحة النتيجة؛ فيكون هذا أيضًا مصادرة على المطلوب الأول بوسائط كما قيل في كتاب إقليديس إن زوايا المثلث بهذه الحالة إنما يبرهن بعد صحة ذلك يعني بعد صحة قولنا إنه إذا وقع على الخطوط المتوارية خط قاطع كانت الزاويتين المتعادلتين متساويتين. والخارجة مساوية لما يقابلها وهي الداخلة فتعرف حال الزوايا في الخطين بزوايا المثلث، وحال زوايا المثلث بحال زوايا الخطين فيكون استعمال كون الزوايا في الخطين مع الخط الواقع عليها مقدمة في بيان نفسه بوسائط ؛ وإنه هو المصادرة على المطلوب الأول، ولا يظن أن الإنسان إذا وضع في بيان المطلوب الأول مقدّمة يشارك المطلوب الأول في المحمول وموضوعها مع موضوعه يتعاكسان كالإنسان والضاحك كان ذلك مصادرة على المطلوب الأول بل ليس كذلك فإن الموضوعين مختلفان في المعنى. وكذلك إذا أخذ موضوع أحدهما كليّاً وموضوع الآخر جزئياً من جزئيات ذلك الكلي فإنه لا يكون ذلك مصادرة على المطلوب الأول. وإن كانا متحدين في المحمول فيجب أن تعلم أن القياس المؤلف من متقابلتين، والذي نحن بصدده وهو المصادرة يشتركان في أن الحد الأوسط في كل واحد منهما موجود في النتيجة. والمصادرة على المطلوب الأول يمكن استعمالها على هيئة كل شكل من الأشكال الأربعة بالشرائط التي مرَّ ذكرها. فإن كان المطلوب موجباً كلياً يمكن في الشكل الأول أن يكون صغرى وكبرى؛ فإن كان صغرى كان الأكبر والأوسط بمعنى واحد فكانت الكبرى هي تلك المقدمة الصادقة حدا. وإن كان كبرى كان الأصغر والأوسط بمعنى واحد ، وكانت الصغرى هي تلك الصادقة حدا. وإن كان جزءا لم يكن إلا صغرى، وإن كان سالبا لم يكن إلا أكبر. وعلى هذا في الأشكال الباقية. ثمّ المصادرة على المطلوب الأول قد تكون في العلوم؛ وذلك إذا كان صادرًا بالحقيقة وعلى الوجه الذي قلناه، وقد تكون في الجدل؛ وذلك بحسب الظنِّ الموجود وهو الذي يكون حدا المقدمة الصادقة فيه كشيء واحد بحسب الظن المحمود.
The middle term intended for deception in dialectic. It is only like that when the sought [conclusion] is of a nature not to be evident by itself, for self-evident things are not measured by [syllogism]. That may occur in a single syllogism as in our statement: Every human is a mortal, and every mortal is risible, so every human is risible; the major premise and the sought are one thing in meaning. Similarly if we say: Every risible is a mortal, and every mortal is a human, so every risible is a human; for the minor premise and the sought are one thing. This is seldom hidden except from the weak-minded. And it may occur in more than one syllogism, such that a result is shown by a premise not evident by itself, and that premise is shown by another premise, and that premise is only shown by the truth of the result; this too is circular reasoning on the first sought through intermediaries, as was said in Euclid's book: If parallel lines are intersected by a transversal, the angles such-and-such. Among the premises of the proof of that sought is that two lines in a plane, if intersected by a line that happens to make the two interior angles on one side equal to two right angles, do not meet. If someone attempts to show this by saying that if they meet, a triangle would be formed from the two lines and the transversal, and the three angles would be greater than two right angles—this is a [proof by] contradiction. So then they do not meet; he has begged the first sought unknowingly, because the state of the triangle's angles being thus is only proven after the truth of that, meaning after the truth of our statement: If a transversal falls on parallel lines, the alternate angles are equal, and the exterior equals the opposite interior, so the state of the angles of the two lines is known by the angles of the triangle, and the state of the angles of the triangle by the state of the angles of the two lines. Thus using the state of the angles of the two lines with the transversal as a premise to prove itself through intermediaries is the very begging of the first sought. And one should not think that if a person, in proving the first sought, uses a premise that shares the predicate with the first sought and its subject is the converse of the subject, like 'human' and 'risible', that this is circular reasoning on the first sought; rather it is not, because the two subjects differ in meaning. Similarly, if the subject of one is taken as a universal and the subject of the other as a particular under that universal, this is not circular reasoning on the first sought. Even if they are united in the predicate, you must know that a syllogism composed of two converse [propositions] and that which we are discussing—circular reasoning—share that the middle term in each of them exists in the conclusion. Circular reasoning on the first sought can be used in the form of each of the four figures with the conditions previously mentioned. If the sought is a universal affirmative, it can be [used as] minor or major in the first figure. If it is minor, the major and middle are one in meaning, so the major premise is that true proposition as a definition. If it is major, the minor and middle are one in meaning, so the minor premise is that true proposition as a definition. If it is a particular [affirmative], it can only be minor; if negative, it can only be major. And similarly for the remaining figures. Then circular reasoning on the first sought may occur in sciences, when it is truly begged in the manner we have described, and may occur in dialectic, according to prevailing opinion, where it is that the definition of the true premise in it is as one thing according to praiseworthy opinion.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage defines and explains circular reasoning (المصادرة على المطلوب الأول), which is a logical fallacy where the conclusion is assumed in the premises. The author begins by stating that this fallacy occurs when the sought (المطلوب) is not self-evident, because self-evident truths do not require proof. He gives two simple examples: 'Every human is a mortal, every mortal is risible, so every human is risible'—here the major premise and conclusion are the same in meaning because 'mortal' and 'risible' are equivalent in the tradition. Similarly, reversing the order shows the minor premise equivalent to the conclusion. This obvious error is hidden only from the weak-minded. Then the author discusses a more subtle form involving multiple syllogisms, where a premise is proven by another premise that in turn is proven by the original conclusion, creating a circle. He illustrates this with Euclid's geometry: proving that parallel lines do not meet by using properties of triangles that themselves rely on the parallel line properties. That is a hidden circularity. Next, he clarifies a common misunderstanding: if the subject and predicate are simply reversed (like 'human' and 'risible'), it is not circular because the subjects differ in meaning. Similarly, using a universal and its particular is not circular. Even if the predicate is the same, a syllogism with converse propositions differs from true circular reasoning. Finally, he outlines how circular reasoning can occur in each of the four syllogistic figures, with conditions depending on the quantity and quality of the sought. He distinguishes between circular reasoning in sciences (genuine, as described) and in dialectic (based on prevailing opinion). The passage is definitional, clarifying what counts as circular reasoning and what does not.

الحد الأوسط
الجدل

Identifying the Fallacy of False Cause
Definition of false cause fallacy and its distinction from valid inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an argument commits the fallacy of false cause by analyzing which premises actually produce a conclusion.

Core Idea
A false conclusion does not always point to a false premise; the cause may lie elsewhere.

The fallacy of false cause occurs when an argument incorrectly attributes a conclusion to a single premise that does not actually cause it. Understanding this fallacy is crucial for distinguishing sound reasoning from sophistry. In this lesson, we will parse the author's definition and learn how to spot this error in Islamic logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimed cause
Locate the premise that the argument presents as the cause of the conclusion. In the passage, this is the premise from which the falsehood is said to follow.
Be precise: the fallacy involves a premise that alone does not produce the conclusion.
2
Check if the conclusion follows from that premise alone
Test the logical connection: does the conclusion actually follow from only that premise? If not, the argument may be committing false cause.
The author says the falsehood is 'لازم من مقدمة واحدة' (follows from one premise), but the truth is it does not follow from that premise alone.
3
Consider other premises or combinations
Look for other premises that might be necessary for the conclusion. The passage states the falsehood arises 'من غيرها من المقدمات أو من المجموع المركب منها ومن غيرها' (from other premises or the composite).
This step helps expose the misattribution of causality.
4
Compare with reduction to absurdity (قياس الخلف)
The author references qiyas al-khalf, a valid method where the contradictory of the sought is taken. Distinguish this from false cause: in reduction to absurdity, the false conclusion is correctly derived from the assumption, not misattributed.
This contrast clarifies when falsehood is legitimate vs. fallacious.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في وضع ما ليس بسبب للنتيجة على أنه سبب ] وأما وضع ما ليس بسبب للنتيجة على أنه سبب فذلك من جملة ما ينتج كذبًا. ثم إن ذلك الكذب لازم من مقدمة واحدة من تلك المقدمات المذكورة فقط. وكان الحق أن يقال إن ذلك ليس لازما من تلك المقدمة التي ذكرتموها بل إما من غيرها من المقدمات أو من المجموع المركب منها ومن غيرها كما مر من قبل في قياس الخلف؛ وهو أن يؤخذ نقيض المطلوب من القياس
Section: [On placing what is not a cause for the result as a cause]. As for placing what is not a cause for the result as a cause, that is among the things that produce a falsehood. Then that falsehood follows from only one of the mentioned premises. But the truth is that it does not follow from that premise which you mentioned, but rather either from other premises or from the composite of them and others, as has passed before in the syllogism of reduction to absurdity; which is that the contradictory of the sought is taken from the syllogism...
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on a specific logical fallacy: placing what is not a cause for the result as a cause (وضع ما ليس بسبب للنتيجة على أنه سبب), which is the fallacy of false cause. The author states that this fallacy is among the things that produce false conclusions. Then he explains that the falsehood follows from only one of the premises mentioned. However, the correct analysis is that the falsehood does not follow from that particular premise alone, but rather either from other premises or from the combination of that premise and others. He refers back to the earlier discussion of reduction to absurdity (قياس الخلف), which involves taking the contradictory of the sought as a premise. The passage is cut off, but it introduces the concept and distinguishes it from valid reasoning where falsehood arises from multiple premises.

Sophistry and Fallacies
intermediate

Begging the Question (Musādara): When the Premise Assumes the Conclusion
Author defines and explains the fallacy of begging the question across syllogistic figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify cases of begging the question in syllogistic arguments and distinguish them from legitimate arguments where premises do not assume the conclusion.

Core Idea
Begging the question assumes what it aims to prove by making the middle term identical to the conclusion's subject or predicate.

The fallacy of begging the question (musādara 'alā al-maṭlūb al-awwal) occurs when a premise is essentially the same as the conclusion, making the argument circular. This passage from the classical logic text clarifies when this fallacy arises and when it does not, by examining the relationship between the middle term and the conclusion across the four syllogistic figures. Understanding this fallacy is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences, where circular reasoning can undermine proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term and the conclusion
In any syllogism, locate the middle term (ḥadd awsaṭ) that links the premises. The conclusion is the statement to be proved. Begging the question occurs when the middle term is identical in meaning to either the subject or the predicate of the conclusion.
The author emphasizes that mere similarity of terms is not enough; identity of meaning is required.
2
Check for non-fallacious cases
If the subjects of the premises are opposite (e.g., 'human' vs 'laughing') or if one subject is universal and the other is a particular under that universal, the argument is not begging the question. The subjects differ in meaning, so the premises do not simply restate the conclusion.
3
Examine the syllogistic figure and conditions
Apply the conditions for each of the four figures as described. For a universal affirmative conclusion in the first figure, if the middle term equals the major term, then the major premise is the conclusion; if the middle equals the minor term, then the minor premise is the conclusion. For particular or negative conclusions, only certain placements are possible.
The author details these conditions to show how the fallacy can manifest in different figures.
4
Determine the dialectical vs demonstrative context
The author notes that musādara can occur in demonstrative sciences as a genuine error, or in dialectical contexts where it is allowed as a persuasive device based on accepted opinion. Assess the argument's purpose to decide if it is fallacious.
In dialectic, what appears as begging the question may be acceptable if the premise is generally accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موضوعه يتعاكسان كالإنسان والضاحك كان ذلك مصادرة على المطلوب الأول بل ليس كذلك فإن الموضوعين مختلفان في المعنى. وكذلك إذا أخذ موضوع أحدهما كلياً وموضوع الآخر جزئياً من جزئيات ذلك الكلي فإنه لا يكون ذلك مصادرة على المطلوب الأول. وإن كانا متحدين في المحمول فيجب أن تعلم أن القياس المؤلف من متقابلتين، والذي نحن بصدده وهو المصادرة يشتركان في أن الحد الأوسط في كل واحد منهما موجود في النتيجة. والمصادرة على المطلوب الأول يمكن استعمالها على هيئة كل شكل من الأشكال الأربعة بالشرائط التي مرَّ ذكرها. فإن كان المطلوب موجباً كلياً يمكن في الشكل الأول أن يكون صغرى وكبرى؛ فإن كان صغرى كان الأكبر والأوسط بمعنى واحد فكانت الكبرى هي تلك المقدمة الصادقة حدا. وإن كان كبرى كان الأصغر والأوسط بمعنى واحد ، وكانت الصغرى هي تلك الصادقة حدا. وإن كان جزءا لم يكن إلا صغرى، وإن كان سالبا لم يكن إلا أكبر. وعلى هذا في الأشكال الباقية. ثمّ المصادرة على المطلوب الأول قد تكون في العلوم؛ وذلك إذا كان صادرًا بالحقيقة وعلى الوجه الذي قلناه، وقد تكون في الجدل؛ وذلك بحسب الظنِّ الموجود وهو الذي يكون حدا المقدمة الصادقة فيه كشيء واحد بحسب الظن المحمود.
If their subjects are opposite like 'human' and 'laughing', that would be begging the question, but it is not, because the two subjects differ in meaning. Likewise, if the subject of one is taken as universal and the subject of the other as partial among the particulars of that universal, then that is not begging the question. And if they are united in the predicate, then you must know that the syllogism composed of two opposites, which we are discussing—i.e., begging the question—share that the middle term in each of them exists in the conclusion. Begging the question can be used in the form of each of the four figures under the conditions mentioned earlier. If the desired conclusion is affirmative universal, in the first figure it can be minor or major; if it is minor, then the major and middle have one meaning, so the major is that true premise as a limit. And if it is major, then the minor and middle have one meaning, so the minor is that true premise as a limit. And if it is particular, it is only minor; and if negative, it is only major. And likewise for the remaining figures. Then, begging the question may occur in the sciences; that is when it is issued in truth and in the manner we said; and it may occur in dialectic; that is according to the existing opinion, and it is that which makes the true premise as if it were one thing according to praiseworthy opinion.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage clarifies the fallacy of begging the question (musādara 'alā al-maṭlūb al-awwal). The author first distinguishes cases that are not begging the question: when the subjects of the premises are opposite in meaning (e.g., 'human' and 'laughing' are not identical) or when one subject is universal and the other is a particular under that universal, the premises are not circular. Begging the question requires that the middle term be identical in meaning to either the subject or predicate of the conclusion. The passage then explains how this fallacy can appear in each of the four syllogistic figures under specific conditions. For a universal affirmative conclusion in the first figure, the middle may be identical to the major (so the major premise is the very thing to be proved) or to the minor (so the minor premise is the conclusion). For particular or negative conclusions, only certain placements are possible. Finally, the author notes that begging the question can occur in demonstrative sciences (where it is a genuine error) or in dialectical contexts (where it is allowed as a matter of persuasion based on accepted opinion).

Key Terms
المصادرة على المطلوب الأول
حد أوسط

A) Both subjects are identical in meaning.
B) Subjects are opposite in meaning, like 'human' and 'laughing'.
C) One subject is universal and the other is a particular under it.
D) Both B and C are correct.

Key Takeaway
The essence of begging the question is the identity of meaning between the middle term and the conclusion's subject or predicate, making the argument circular.
