وقد علمت بأن من الألفاظ ما يكون مشتركًا، ومنها ما يكون مترادفا مثلا، واللفظ في الكل من الألفاظ المشتركة وغيرها إما مفرد وإما مركب والمركب قد يكون مركبا بوجه الصحة، وقد يكون مشبها به وهو المغالطي، والمغالطة قد يكون عن قصد، وقد تكون عن ضلالة. وبالجملة فالمغالطي على ضربين فإنه إما سوفسطائي وهو الذي يترائى بالحكمة ويدعي أنه مبرهن؛ ولا يكون كذلك بل أكثر ما يناله أن يظن به ذلك، وإما مشاغبي وهو الذي يتراءى بأنه جدلي وأنه في محاوراته بقياس من المشهورات المحمودة؛ ولا يكون كذلك بل أكثر ما يناله أن يظن به ذلك. والحكيم بالحقيقة هو الذي إذا قضى بقضية يخاطب بها نفسه أو غير نفسه كان ذلك حقا فإنه ما قال إلا وقد عقل الحق عقلاً مضاعفا؛ وذلك لاقتداره على قوانين تميز بين الحق والباطل. وإذا سمع من غيره ما لا يكون حقا يمكنه أن يظهر؛ فبالحري أن يكون، أول ما ينصرف إليه السوفسطائي فكره أن يستقرئ الألفاظ المشتركة؛ ويجمعها وينصبها خداعية بل أن يحفظ علما بجميع المخاطبات والمحاورات السوفسطائية وأصنافها لتكون مادة معدة له لما يفعله ويكاد أن يكون اشتراك أنفع شيء في أن يظن به أنه حكيم؛ ولا حاجة لنا إلى إثبات وجود هذه الألفاظ؛ إذ الأمر في وجودها ظاهر. ونقول: إن أجناس المحاورات القياسية المتعلقة بالأمور الكلية أربعة: البرهانية، والجدلية، والامتحانية، والمشاغبية. وقد عرفتها من قبل، وعرفت الفرق بين السوفسطائية والمشاغبية، وعرفت أن المغالطي يجمعها جميعًا لكن ما عرفت المشاغبية كما ينبغي. فنقول إن أجزاء الصناعة المشاغبية خمسة: أحدها التسكيت المغالطي؛ وثانيها التشنيع بما يتسلم مما يسلمه أو يقوله المخاطب؛ وثالثها سوق الكلام إلى الكذب وإلى خلاف المشهور؛ ورابعها إيراد ما يتحير فيه المخاطب ويشتبه عليه معناه من جهة اللفظ؛ وخامسها الهذيان والتكرير والتسكيت منه ما هو داخل في المعنى. والفرق بينه وبين غيره أن التسكيت هو نفس القول الذي يراد به إنتاج نقيض الوضع. وأما الغير فليس المخالط يوردها على هذا الطريق بل قد يبتدئ بها فلا يعلم المخاطب مقصوده بها. وكثيرًا ما يسائل السوفسطائي عن طرفي النقيض؛ فإن سلمت له الموجبة مثلا عقد منها التسكيت، وإن سلمت له السالبة لم ينتفع بها في التسكيت، وينتفع بأن هذا الذي سلمه مخالف وغيره مشهور فيكون صنعه هذا من باب التشنيع ليس من باب التسكيت.
And you have learned that among words are those that are common (ambiguous) and those that are synonymous, for example, and the word in all common and other words is either singular or compound, and the compound may be compound in a valid way, or similar to it, which is fallacious, and the fallacy may be intentional or due to error. In summary, the fallacious (person) is of two types: either a sophist, who appears as a wise man and claims to be a demonstrator, but is not; rather, the most he attains is that it is thought of him that way; or an eristic, who appears as a dialectician and that in his debates he uses a syllogism from the praiseworthy accepted things, but is not; rather, the most he attains is that it is thought of him that way. The truly wise man is one who, when he makes a judgment addressing himself or others, it is true; for he does not say except that he has grasped the truth with a double intellect; that is due to his power over laws that distinguish between truth and falsehood. And if he hears from someone else what is not true, he can show it. So it is fitting that the first thing the sophist's mind turns to is to gather common words and collect them and set them as a deception, or rather to memorize a knowledge of all sophistical speeches and debates and their types, to be a prepared material for what he does; and equivocation is almost the most useful thing in making him thought of as a wise man. There is no need for us to prove the existence of these words, for the matter of their existence is apparent. And we say: The genera of syllogistic debates concerning universal matters are four: demonstrative, dialectical, examination, and eristic. You have known them before, and you have known the difference between sophistical and eristic, and you have known that the fallacious person combines them all, but you have not known the eristic as it should be. So we say: The parts of the eristic art are five: First, the fallacious silencing; second, making odious by what is accepted from what the opponent admits or says; third, driving speech to falsehood and opposition to the accepted; fourth, presenting what the opponent is perplexed about and whose meaning is ambiguous due to the word; fifth, nonsense and repetition, and of the silencing, some is internal to the meaning. The difference between it (the silencing) and other (parts) is that the silencing is the very statement by which producing the contradictory of the position is intended. As for the other, the disputant does not bring it in this way, but rather he may begin with it, so the opponent does not know his intention by it. And often the sophist asks about the two contradictories; if the affirmative is conceded to him, he constructs the silencing from it, and if the negative is conceded, he does not benefit from it in silencing; and he benefits from that this conceded (matter) is disagreeable and other is accepted; so his action is from the category of making odious, not from the category of silencing.
Source pages p. 582

Commentary
This passage classifies types of syllogistic discussions and details the parts of the eristic art. It begins by distinguishing between words that are ambiguous (mushtarak) and those that are synonymous (mutaradif), then defines the fallacious person as one who uses deceptive arguments. The fallacious person is of two types: the sophist (sufistai) who pretends to be a demonstrator, and the eristic (mushaghbi) who pretends to be a dialectician. The truly wise person is one who can distinguish truth from falsehood. The sophist's main tool is equivocal words. The author then lists the four genera of syllogistic discussions: demonstrative (burhaniyya), dialectical (jadaliyya), examination (imtihaniyya), and eristic (mushaghbiyya). Focusing on the eristic art, he enumerates its five parts: (1) fallacious silencing (taskit mughalati), which aims to produce the contradictory of the opponent's position; (2) making odious (tashni') by using the opponent's admissions; (3) driving speech to falsehood or against common opinion; (4) introducing perplexing statements whose meaning is ambiguous; and (5) nonsense and repetition. The passage then clarifies the difference between silencing and making odious: silencing is the direct attempt to produce a contradiction, while making odious involves using the opponent's concession in a way that makes it seem objectionable. The sophist often asks about contradictories; if the affirmative is conceded, he constructs a silencing argument; if the negative is conceded, he uses it for making odious instead. This classification is foundational for understanding logical fallacies in dialectical contexts.

Key Terms
التسكيت المغالطي (al-taskīt al-mughāliṭī): fallacious silencing
التشنيع (al-tashnī'): making odious
سوق الكلام إلى الكذب (sawq al-kalām ilā al-kadhib): driving speech to falsehood
إيراد ما يتحير فيه (īrād mā yataḥayyaru fīhi): presenting what causes perplexity
الهذيان والتكرير (al-hadhayān wa al-takrīr): nonsense and repetition

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first part of the eristic art (al-sinā'ah al-mushāghabiyyah)?
A) Al-tashnī' (making odious)
B) Al-taskīt al-mughāliṭī (fallacious silencing)
C) Siyāq al-kalām ilā al-kadhib (driving speech to falsehood)
D) Īrād mā yataḥayyaru fīhi (introducing perplexing statements)

Key Takeaway
The eristic art consists of five parts: silencing, making odious, driving to falsehood, causing perplexity, and nonsense; the key distinction is between silencing (aiming at contradiction) and making odious (aiming at making the opponent's view seem bad).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate about the legal status of tobacco, a debater asks: 'Do you say smoking is haram or halal?' If the opponent says haram, the first debater says: 'So you claim that millions of Muslims for centuries were sinning?' Which eristic part is this? (Answer: Tashnī' - making odious, because he uses the opponent's admission to paint them as accusing past Muslims of sin.)
?
Identify the eristic part used when a debater, after the opponent says 'I believe in free will,' starts repeating the same question in different words to confuse him. (Answer: Hadhayān wa takrīr - nonsense and repetition.)

Sophistry and Fallacies

Six Verbal Fallacies and Their Effect on Syllogisms
Author lists six verbal fallacies and explains their impact.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the six verbal fallacies and explain how they invalidate a syllogism by altering the relation of terms.

Core Idea
Verbal fallacies break syllogisms by exploiting word meaning.

In this passage, the author enumerates six types of verbal fallacies that cause error in syllogisms. These are not mere stylistic errors; they affect the very relation of the middle term to the extremes, rendering a syllogism invalid. By learning to recognize these fallacies, you sharpen your ability to detect sophistry in arguments, especially those found in theological and juristic debates.

Guided Path
1
Locate the six types
In the Arabic passage, find the list: اشتراك الاسم, المماراة, اشتراك التأليف, اشتراك القسمة, اختلاف العجمة والإعراب, اختلاف شكل اللفظ. Write them down.
These six types are the genera of verbal fallacies.
2
Understand each fallacy's core
For each type, note the definition. Ishtirāk al-ism is equivocation of a single word with multiple meanings. Mumarāh involves disputation. Ishtirāk al-ta'līf is composition equivocation. Ishtirāk al-qismah is division equivocation. Ikhtilāf al-'ujmah wa al-i'rāb is difference in foreignness and inflection. Ikhtilāf shakl al-lafẓ is difference in word form.
Each exploits a different linguistic ambiguity.
3
Analyze the effect on syllogism
The author says that when these occur, 'the relation of the middle term to the two extremes must differ'. This means the middle term is not used in the same sense in both premises, breaking the logical connection and invalidating the syllogism.
The syllogism is not truly a syllogism if such fallacies occur.
4
Study the example of ishtirāk al-ism
Look at the example: 'He knows and does not know.' The word 'knows' is ambiguous between 'acquiring knowledge' and 'having knowledge'. In the first premise, it might mean one; in the second, the other. This equivocation invalidates the inference.
The same word is used in two different senses across the argument.
5
Apply the lesson
Think of another example from Islamic sciences where a word has two meanings that could cause a fallacy, e.g., 'wajib' meaning 'obligatory' vs. 'necessary'. Consider how it could be exploited.
Practice identifying equivocation in real arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما التسكيت الداخل في اللفظ فإنه يوقع الغلط بستة أقسام باشتراك الاسم والمماراة واشتراك التأليف واشتراك القسمة واختلاف العجمة والإعراب واختلاف شكل اللفظ؛ وجميع ذلك يؤثر في القياس ويؤثر في الاستقراء. ثم القياس يوجب عليك أنه إذا وقع من اشتراك الاسم أو الاستعجام أو غير ذلك وجب أن تختلف نسبة الوسط إلى الطرفين بل نسبة الطرفين النتيجة فيعرض لا محالة أن لا يكون القياس في الحقيقة قياسًا. والقياس يوجب عليك عكس هذا أيضًا وهو أن أجناس المغالطات اللفظية هي هذه؛ وسيرد عليك هذا القياس في موضعه من بعد ومثال التسكيت المغالطي لاشتراك الاسم كمن يقول للمتعلم إنه يعلم ولا يعلم؛ فإن لم يعلم فليس بمتعلم، وإن علم فليس يحتاج إلى أن يتعلم والمغالطة في هذا أن قوله يعلم يعني به أنه يحصل له العلم ويكذب إذا كان بمعنى حصل له العلم. وربَّما كان لفظة «يتعقل» في لغة العرب دالة على الفكرة، وربَّما كان دالة على حصول العقل نفسه. وكذلك قول قائل هل شيء من الشرور بواجب أو ليس بواجب؛ فإن كان
As for the silencing that is internal to the word, it causes error by six types: equivocation of the name, disputation (mumarāh), equivocation of composition, equivocation of division, difference of foreignness and inflection, and difference of the form of the word. All of that affects the syllogism and affects induction. Then the syllogism necessitates for you that if equivocation of the name or foreignness or other occurs, the relation of the middle term to the two extremes must differ—indeed, the relation of the two extremes to the conclusion—so it necessarily occurs that the syllogism is not truly a syllogism. The syllogism also necessitates the converse for you, that the genera of verbal fallacies are these, and this syllogism will come to you in its place later. An example of fallacious silencing through equivocation of the name is someone saying to a learner: 'He knows and does not know'; if he does not know, he is not a learner; and if he knows, he does not need to learn. The fallacy in this is that his statement 'he knows' means that he acquires knowledge, and it is false if it means he has acquired knowledge. And perhaps the word 'ya'qil' in the Arabic language indicates thought, and perhaps it indicates the acquisition of the intellect itself. And likewise, someone's saying: 'Is anything of evils necessary or not necessary? For if it is...'
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage focuses on verbal fallacies that cause error in syllogisms and induction. It lists six types: (1) equivocation of the name (ishtirāk al-ism), (2) disputation (mumarāh), (3) equivocation of composition (ishtirāk al-ta'līf), (4) equivocation of division (ishtirāk al-qismah), (5) difference of foreignness and inflection (ikhtilāf al-'ujmah wa al-i'rāb), and (6) difference of word form (ikhtilāf shakl al-lafẓ). The author explains that these fallacies affect the syllogism by altering the relation of the middle term to the extremes, thus rendering the syllogism invalid. The syllogism itself requires that if such fallacies occur, the argument is not a true syllogism. Conversely, the genera of verbal fallacies are exactly these six. The author then provides an example of equivocation: saying to a learner 'he knows and he does not know.' The word 'knows' is ambiguous, meaning either 'acquiring knowledge' or 'having knowledge.' The sophist uses this ambiguity to argue that the learner does not need to learn if he knows, or cannot learn if he does not know. Another example involves the word 'ya'qil' (intellect), which can mean 'thinking' or 'having intellect.' Finally, an example about evil being necessary or not is introduced but cut off. This lesson teaches students to identify and guard against verbal fallacies that exploit linguistic ambiguity.

C) Difference of inflection (ikhtilāf al-i'rāb)
D) Difference of word form (ikhtilāf shakl al-lafẓ)

Key Takeaway
The six verbal fallacies undermine syllogistic reasoning by altering the meaning of terms, so a valid syllogism in form can be fallacious in content.

Therefore, Allah has an attribute called knowledge.' Is there any equivocation? If not, think of an argument where the word 'ʿAlīm' might be used in different senses by a sophist.

The Definition of Taskit (Silencing) and Its Special Status Among Fallacies
Author defines taskit (silencing) and lists six types of verbal fallacies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define taskit and explain how it differs from other verbal fallacies based on the author's criteria.

Core Idea
Taskit aims to produce the contradictory of the opponent's position to silence him.

In this passage, the author distinguishes a special type of sophistical fallacy called 'taskit' from other confusing statements. Understanding this distinction is crucial: it reveals that not all ambiguous statements are taskit—only those deliberately constructed to invert the opponent's thesis. We will examine the author's definition and the tactical use of contradictory extremes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find 'التسكيت' in the Arabic and note its literal meaning (silencing). The author explicitly defines it.
The term appears after listing the fifth type of fallacy.
2
Extract the essential definition
The author says: 'التسكيت هو نفس القول الذي يراد به إنتاج نقيض الوضع' — taskit is the very statement intended to produce the contradictory of the position.
The phrase 'نقيض الوضع' means the contradictory of the opponent's claim.
3
Contrast with other fallacies
Other fallacies do not have this specific intention; they may simply confuse from the start. The author notes that in other fallacies the speaker may begin with a confusing statement without aiming to produce a contradiction.
This distinction is key to recognizing taskit in disputations.
4
Understand the sophist's method
The sophist asks about two contradictory extremes. If the affirmative is conceded, he uses it to construct a taskit. If only the negative is conceded, he cannot use it for taskit but instead uses it for tashni' (condemnation).
This shows that taskit depends on the opponent's concession of the affirmative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى الكذب وإلى خلاف المشهور؛ ورابعها إيراد ما يتحير فيه المخاطب ويشتبه عليه معناه من جهة اللفظ؛ وخامسها الهذيان والتكرير والتسكيت. والفرق بينه وبين غيره أن التسكيت هو نفس القول الذي يراد به إنتاج نقيض الوضع. وأما الغير فليس المخالط يوردها على هذا الطريق بل قد يبتدئ بها فلا يعلم المخاطب مقصوده بها. وكثيرًا ما يسائل السوفسطائي عن طرفي النقيض؛ فإن سلمت له الموجبة مثلا عقد منها التسكيت، وإن سلمت له السالبة لم ينتفع بها في التسكيت، وينتفع بأن هذا الذي سلمه مخالف وغيره مشهور فيكون صنعه هذا من باب التشنيع ليس من باب التسكيت. وأما التسكيت الداخل في اللفظ فإنه يوقع الغلط بستة أقسام باشتراك الاسم والمماراة واشتراك التأليف واشتراك القسمة واختلاف العجمة والإعراب واختلاف شكل اللفظ؛ وجميع ذلك يؤثر في القياس ويؤثر في الاستقراء.
[Some fallacies lead] to falsehood and to opposition to common opinion; the fourth is bringing what confuses the addressee and whose meaning is ambiguous from the aspect of wording; the fifth is nonsense, repetition, and taskit (silencing). The difference between it and others is that taskit is the very statement by which it is intended to produce the contradictory of the position. As for the other, the disputant does not present it in this way; rather he may begin with it and the addressee does not know his intention by it. Frequently the sophist asks about the two contradictory extremes; if the affirmative is conceded to him, he constructs from it the taskeet, and if the negative is conceded to him, he does not benefit from it in taskeet, but he benefits by that which he conceded is contrary and other than what is well-known; so his action is of the type of condemnation (tashni'), not of taskeet. As for taskeet that is internal to the wording, it causes error in six divisions: equivocation (ishtirak al-ism), mumarah (paronomasia/ambiguity of composition), amphiboly (ishtirak al-ta'lif), division (ishtirak al-qisma), difference in barbarism and grammar (ikhtilaf al-'ujma wa al-i'rab), and difference in form of the wording (ikhtilaf shakl al-lafz); and all of that affects the syllogism and affects induction.
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory excerpt from a logic text on sophistical fallacies. It begins by listing, in continuation from a prior missing context, the types of fallacies: some lead to falsehood, others to what is contrary to common opinion; the fourth type is bringing something that confuses the listener through ambiguous wording; and the fifth is nonsense, repetition, and 'taskeet' (silencing). The author then distinguishes taskeet from other fallacies: taskeet is a statement intended to produce the contradictory of the opponent's thesis, whereas in other fallacies the speaker does not aim for such a direct reversal but simply begins with a confusing statement. The sophist often asks about contradictory statements; if the affirmative is conceded, he uses it to construct a silencing argument, but if only the negative is conceded, he cannot use it for silencing; instead he treats the concession as contrary to common opinion, which becomes a form of condemnation (tashni'), not silencing. The passage then delves into taskeet that occurs within wording itself, which causes error through six verbal divisions: (1) equivocation (ishtirak al-ism) — different meanings for the same word; (2) mumarah (ambiguity of composition) — confusion due to composition of words; (3) amphiboly (ishtirak al-ta'lif) — ambiguous sentence structure; (4) division (ishtirak al-qisma) — ambiguous division of terms; (5) difference in barbarism and grammar (ikhtilaf al-'ujma wa al-i'rab) — foreign words or grammatical inflection; (6) difference in form (ikhtilaf shakl al-lafz) — variations in written form. All these affect both syllogistic reasoning and induction, meaning they undermine the validity of arguments.

Key Terms
التسكيت

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Taskit is the fourth type of fallacy, while the fifth is nonsense.

Key Takeaway
Taskit is a specific sophistical fallacy defined by its aim to produce a contradiction that silences the opponent, distinguishing it from general confusion.

Sophistry and Fallacies

The Six Verbal Fallacies and Their Impact on Reasoning
Author defines taskit (silencing) and lists six types of verbal fallacies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and name the six types of verbal fallacies as classified by the author and explain how they affect syllogistic reasoning and induction.

Core Idea
Six verbal fallacies rooted in linguistic ambiguity undermine both qiyas and istiqra'.

After defining taskit, the author enumerates six subdivisions of verbal fallacies that cause error in reasoning. These fallacies exploit equivocation, ambiguous composition, amphiboly, division, foreignness, and written form. Understanding each helps you detect when an argument's form is deceptive due to language.

Guided Path
1
List the six types from the text
Identify each in Arabic: اشتراك الاسم، المماراة، اشتراك التأليف، اشتراك القسمة، اختلاف العجمة والإعراب، اختلاف شكل اللفظ.
The author presents them as divisions of taskeet internal to wording.
2
Define each type briefly
Equivocation (same word, different meanings); mumarah (ambiguity in composition); amphiboly (ambiguous sentence structure); division (incorrectly dividing a composite); foreignness/grammar (words from another language or different inflection); form (homographs).
Use the provided key terms for precise definitions.
3
Note the author's claim about their effect
The author concludes: 'وجميع ذلك يؤثر في القياس ويؤثر في الاستقراء' (all of that affects syllogism and affects induction). This means these fallacies can invalidate both deductive and inductive arguments.
This shows the broad scope of verbal fallacies in logic.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Think of an equivocation example: the word 'Quran' can mean the recited book or the uncreated attribute, leading to fallacious arguments in kalam.
Identify which of the six is operating in your example.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى الكذب وإلى خلاف المشهور؛ ورابعها إيراد ما يتحير فيه المخاطب ويشتبه عليه معناه من جهة اللفظ؛ وخامسها الهذيان والتكرير والتسكيت. والفرق بينه وبين غيره أن التسكيت هو نفس القول الذي يراد به إنتاج نقيض الوضع. وأما الغير فليس المخالط يوردها على هذا الطريق بل قد يبتدئ بها فلا يعلم المخاطب مقصوده بها. وكثيرًا ما يسائل السوفسطائي عن طرفي النقيض؛ فإن سلمت له الموجبة مثلا عقد منها التسكيت، وإن سلمت له السالبة لم ينتفع بها في التسكيت، وينتفع بأن هذا الذي سلمه مخالف وغيره مشهور فيكون صنعه هذا من باب التشنيع ليس من باب التسكيت. وأما التسكيت الداخل في اللفظ فإنه يوقع الغلط بستة أقسام باشتراك الاسم والمماراة واشتراك التأليف واشتراك القسمة واختلاف العجمة والإعراب واختلاف شكل اللفظ؛ وجميع ذلك يؤثر في القياس ويؤثر في الاستقراء.
[Some fallacies lead] to falsehood and to opposition to common opinion; the fourth is bringing what confuses the addressee and whose meaning is ambiguous from the aspect of wording; the fifth is nonsense, repetition, and taskit (silencing). The difference between it and others is that taskit is the very statement by which it is intended to produce the contradictory of the position. As for the other, the disputant does not present it in this way; rather he may begin with it and the addressee does not know his intention by it. Frequently the sophist asks about the two contradictory extremes; if the affirmative is conceded to him, he constructs from it the taskeet, and if the negative is conceded to him, he does not benefit from it in taskeet, but he benefits by that which he conceded is contrary and other than what is well-known; so his action is of the type of condemnation (tashni'), not of taskeet. As for taskeet that is internal to the wording, it causes error in six divisions: equivocation (ishtirak al-ism), mumarah (paronomasia/ambiguity of composition), amphiboly (ishtirak al-ta'lif), division (ishtirak al-qisma), difference in barbarism and grammar (ikhtilaf al-'ujma wa al-i'rab), and difference in form of the wording (ikhtilaf shakl al-lafz); and all of that affects the syllogism and affects induction.
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory excerpt from a logic text on sophistical fallacies. It begins by listing, in continuation from a prior missing context, the types of fallacies: some lead to falsehood, others to what is contrary to common opinion; the fourth type is bringing something that confuses the listener through ambiguous wording; and the fifth is nonsense, repetition, and 'taskeet' (silencing). The author then distinguishes taskeet from other fallacies: taskeet is a statement intended to produce the contradictory of the opponent's thesis, whereas in other fallacies the speaker does not aim for such a direct reversal but simply begins with a confusing statement. The sophist often asks about contradictory statements; if the affirmative is conceded, he uses it to construct a silencing argument, but if only the negative is conceded, he cannot use it for silencing; instead he treats the concession as contrary to common opinion, which becomes a form of condemnation (tashni'), not silencing. The passage then delves into taskeet that occurs within wording itself, which causes error through six verbal divisions: (1) equivocation (ishtirak al-ism) — different meanings for the same word; (2) mumarah (ambiguity of composition) — confusion due to composition of words; (3) amphiboly (ishtirak al-ta'lif) — ambiguous sentence structure; (4) division (ishtirak al-qisma) — ambiguous division of terms; (5) difference in barbarism and grammar (ikhtilaf al-'ujma wa al-i'rab) — foreign words or grammatical inflection; (6) difference in form (ikhtilaf shakl al-lafz) — variations in written form. All these affect both syllogistic reasoning and induction, meaning they undermine the validity of arguments.

Key Terms
اشتراك الاسم
المماراة
اشتراك التأليف
اشتراك القسمة
اختلاف العجمة والإعراب
اختلاف شكل اللفظ

According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the six verbal fallacies?
A) اشتراك الاسم (equivocation)
B) المماراة (mumarah)
C) اشتراك التأليف (amphiboly)
D) الاستقراء (induction)

Key Takeaway
The six verbal fallacies – equivocation, composition, amphiboly, division, foreignness/grammar, and form – all cause errors in syllogistic and inductive reasoning by exploiting linguistic ambiguity.

Sophistry and Fallacies

General Principle: How Verbal Fallacies Corrupt Syllogisms
General principle of syllogism corruption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a syllogism fails when the middle term's relation to extremes is affected by verbal ambiguity.

Core Idea
Verbal fallacies break the syllogism by changing the relation of the middle term.

The author begins this passage with a reminder: a valid syllogism depends on a stable relationship between the middle term and the extremes. When a verbal fallacy occurs—be it equivocation, barbarism, or something else—this relationship becomes inconsistent, and the syllogism loses its force. This lesson introduces that general principle, setting the stage for the specific examples that follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogistic condition
The author states that a valid syllogism requires a consistent relation between the middle term (al-wasat) and the two extremes (al-tarafayn). If that relation is disrupted, the syllogism is not truly a syllogism.
2
Recognize the cause of disruption
The disruption occurs when a verbal fallacy like equivocation or barbarism affects the meaning of the middle term or its connection to the extremes.
3
Understand the converse principle
The author also notes that the fact that such fallacies corrupt syllogisms is itself a proof that these are the genera of verbal fallacies. This shows the logical relationship between the general rule and its application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القياس يوجب عليك أنه إذا وقع من اشتراك الاسم أو الاستعجام أو غير ذلك وجب أن تختلف نسبة الوسط إلى الطرفين بل نسبة الطرفين النتيجة فيعرض لا محالة أن لا يكون القياس في الحقيقة قياسًا. والقياس يوجب عليك عكس هذا أيضًا وهو أن أجناس المغالطات اللفظية هي هذه؛ وسيرد عليك هذا القياس في موضعه من بعد ومثال التسكيت المغالطي لاشتراك الاسم كمن يقول للمتعلم إنه يعلم ولا يعلم؛ فإن لم يعلم فليس بمتعلم، وإن علم فليس يحتاج إلى أن يتعلم والمغالطة في هذا أن قوله يعلم يعني به أنه يحصل له العلم ويكذب إذا كان بمعنى حصل له العلم. وربَّما كان لفظة «يتعقل» في لغة العرب دالة على الفكرة، وربَّما كان دالة على حصول العقل نفسه. وكذلك قول قائل هل شيء من الشرور بواجب أو ليس بواجب؛ فإن كان واجبًا، وكل واجب خير فبعض الشرور خير. وإن كان ليس بواجب فلا يوجد البتة فإن ما لا يجب فليس بموجود. والمغالطة بسبب أن الواجب وجوده غير الواجب العمل به؛ وإنما يقال لهما واجب باشتراك الاسم. ومفهوم الواجب الأول أن وجوده ضروري، ومفهوم الواجب الآخر أن إيثاره محمود. وكذلك يقال في الذي هو قائم إنه هو القاعد بعينه، أو لا يكون؛ وإنما كان يكون قاعدًا وقائما معا، أو لا يكون قادرًا على أن يقعده وهذه الأمثلة فيما يقع باشتراك اللفظ. وما يقع باشتراك اللفظ فذلك باعتبار لفظ مفرد. وأما المشاغبة أعني المماراة فلا يكون الغلط فيها باعتبار لفظ مفرد بل يكون لاختلاف مفهوم التركيب كمن يقول ما يعلمه الإنسان فهو ما يعلمه، ويعلم الحجر فهو حجر؛ والمغالطة بسبب أن لفظه هو تارة يشير إلى المعلوم وتارة إلى الإنسان. وكذلك ما قلته موجودًا أنتَ موجود هو وقلت إن الحجر موجود فأنتَ موجود حجرًا لأن قولك أنتَ يجوز أن يفهم أنتَ موضوعا وموجودًا هو محمول عليه، ويجوز أن يكون أنتَ هو تأكيد لقوله قلته أو صلة لقوله قلت. ويجوز أن يقال الغلط في هذا على جهة أخرى ما قلت إنه موجود أنتَ ذاك موجود؛ وقد قلت إن الحجر موجود. ويكون هذا فيه أظهر؛ فهذا ما يقع الغلط فيه بسبب استناد أجزاء التركيب بعضها إلى بعض. وأما ما يكون بسبب اختلاف إبهام التقديم والتأخير فذلك كمن يقول إن العالم شريف؛ فإنه يجوز أن يكون العالم موضوعا والشريف محمولا، ويجوز أن يكون على العكس كما في قولهم إن الساكت متكلم أمكن أن يفهم أن الساكت متكلم، وأمكن أن يفهم أن المتكلم ساكت . وأما في المشاغبة فاختلاف المفهوم بحسب التركيب بين المفردات كقول القائل معرفة الكتابة فقد يفهم منه معرفة يكون العارف بها الكتابة، ويفهم منه معرفة يكون المعروف بها الكتابة؛ وتركيبه
Then syllogism imposes upon you that if there occurs equivocation (ishtirak al-ism) or barbarism (al-ist'jam) or otherwise, there must be a difference in the relation of the middle term to the two extremes, rather the relation of the two extremes to the conclusion, so it necessarily occurs that the syllogism is not in reality a syllogism. And syllogism also imposes upon you the converse of this, namely that the genera of verbal fallacies are these; and this syllogism will come to you in its place later. An example of a sophistical silencing (taskeet) due to equivocation is like someone who says to the learner: he knows and does not know; if he does not know then he is not a learner, and if he knows then he does not need to learn. The fallacy in this is that his statement 'he knows' means that he obtains knowledge, and it is false if it means that he has already obtained knowledge. And sometimes the word 'ya'qil' in the Arabic language indicates thought, and sometimes it indicates the acquisition of intellect itself. Similarly, the saying of someone: 'Is something of evils necessary (wajib) or not necessary?' If it is necessary, and every necessary is good, then some evils are good. If it is not necessary, then it does not exist at all, for what is not necessary does not exist. The fallacy is due to the fact that the necessary of existence is different from the necessary of action; they are both called 'wajib' by equivocation. The meaning of the first 'wajib' is that its existence is necessary, and the meaning of the other is that its preference is praiseworthy. Similarly, it is said of the one who is standing that he is the sitting one itself, or not; and he could only be sitting and standing together, or he is not able to sit. These examples fall under equivocation of the word. What falls under equivocation is with respect to a single word. As for sophistry (mushaghaha), meaning mumarah, error in it does not occur with respect to a single word but rather due to difference in the concept of composition, like someone who says: 'What man knows, he knows, and he knows stone, so he is stone.' The fallacy is due to the fact that the word 'he' sometimes refers to the known thing and sometimes to man. Similarly: 'What you said is existent, you are existent,' and you said 'stone is existent' so you are existent as a stone. Because your saying 'you' can be understood as 'you' being the subject and 'is existent' as the predicate applied to it, and it can be that 'you' is an emphasis for your saying 'you said it' or a connective for your saying 'you said'. And it can be said that the error in this is in another way: 'What you said is existent: you are that existent'; and you said 'stone is existent'. This is more apparent. So this is what causes error due to the dependence of the parts of composition on each other. As for what is due to difference in ambiguity of precedence and postponement, it is like someone who says: 'The scholar (al-'alim) is noble (al-sharif)'; it can be that 'al-'alim' is the subject and 'al-sharif' is the predicate, and it can be the opposite, as in their saying: 'The silent one is a speaker'; it can be understood that the silent one is a speaker, and it can be understood that the speaker is silent. As for in sophistry (mushaghaha), the difference in concept is according to the composition of the singular terms, like the saying of someone: 'Knowledge of writing' (ma'rifat al-kitaba) can be understood as knowledge whose knower is writing, and it can be understood as knowledge whose known is writing; and its composition... [text breaks off]
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage provides examples and further analysis of the verbal fallacies listed earlier, specifically focusing on how they affect syllogisms. The author begins with a general principle: in a valid syllogism, if equivocation or other verbal fallacies occur, the relation of the middle term to the extremes becomes inconsistent, and the syllogism is not truly a syllogism. Conversely, the fact that verbal fallacies corrupt syllogisms serves as a proof that these are the genera of verbal fallacies. The author then gives detailed examples. The first example illustrates equivocation (ishtirak al-ism) with the word 'ya'lam' (know). A sophist argues that a learner both knows and does not know: if he does not know, he is not a learner (since a learner seeks what he does not know), but if he knows, he does not need to learn. The fallacy arises because 'know' can mean either acquiring knowledge or already possessing knowledge. Similarly, the verb 'ya'qil' can mean either to think or to possess intellect. Another equivocation example uses 'wajib' (necessary/obligatory). The sophist asks whether some evil is 'wajib'. If it is necessary (wajib al-wujud), then it is good (since all necessary existence is good), so some evil is good. If it is not necessary, then it does not exist at all. The fallacy is that 'wajib' has two meanings: necessary existence vs. praiseworthy action. The author then moves to the fallacy of composition (mumarah). Here the error stems not from a single word but from the combination of words. For instance: 'What man knows, he knows; he knows stone; therefore he is stone.' The word 'he' (huwa) shifts reference: in the minor premise it refers to the known object, in the conclusion it refers to the man. Another example: 'What you said is existent; you are existent; you said stone is existent; therefore you are existent as stone.' The phrase 'you' can be subject or emphasis, causing confusion. The author also discusses ambiguity due to word order (taqdim wa ta'khir). For example, 'al-'alimu sharifun' could mean 'the scholar is noble' or 'the noble is a scholar'. Similarly, 'al-sakitu mutakallimun' could mean 'the silent one is speaking' or 'the speaker is silent'. Finally, the author begins an example of composition ambiguity with 'ma'rifat al-kitaba' (knowledge of writing), where the construct phrase can be interpreted as knowledge that writes (active) or knowledge of writing (passive). This passage serves to illustrate the concrete operation of the six verbal fallacies in actual argumentation.

Key Terms
الوسط
المغالطات اللفظية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence of a verbal fallacy in a syllogism?
A) The conclusion becomes false.
B) The relation of the middle term to the extremes becomes inconsistent, so the syllogism is not a real syllogism.
C) The syllogism becomes a different figure.
D) The fallacy only affects the conclusion, not the premises.

Key Takeaway
A valid syllogism requires a stable relation of the middle term to the extremes; verbal fallacies destroy this stability.

Equivocation Fallacies: Examples with 'Know' and 'Wajib'
Examples of equivocation with 'know', 'intellect', and 'wajib'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify equivocation fallacies in arguments where a single word has multiple meanings.

Core Idea
One word with two meanings can make an argument deceptive.

After establishing the general principle, the author gives concrete examples of equivocation (ishtirak al-ism). These illustrate how a sophist exploits a word's ambiguity to produce a seeming contradiction or an invalid inference. We will examine the examples of 'ya'lam' (know), 'ya'qil' (intellect), and 'wajib' (necessary/obligatory).

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous term
In each example, pick out the word that has two distinct meanings. For instance, 'ya'lam' can mean either 'acquiring knowledge' or 'already possessing knowledge'.
2
Parse the sophist's argument
Break down how the sophist moves from one meaning to another within the same argument. For 'ya'lam': the learner is said to both know (acquire) and not know (already possess).
3
Locate where the shift happens
Determine at which step the meaning changes. In the 'wajib' example, the shift occurs between 'necessary existence' and 'praiseworthy action'.
4
Explain why it is a fallacy
The argument appears valid only if the term is used consistently. When the meaning shifts, the syllogism is no longer sound.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The fallacy is due to the fact that the necessary of existence is different from the necessary of action; they are both called 'wajib' by equivocation. The meaning of the first 'wajib' is that its existence is necessary, and the meaning of the other is that its preference is praiseworthy. And it can be said that the error in this is in another way: 'What you said is existent: you are that existent'; and you said 'stone is existent'. This is more apparent. So this is what causes error due to the dependence of the parts of composition on each other. As for what is due to difference in ambiguity of precedence and postponement, it is like someone who says: 'The scholar (al-'alim) is noble (al-sharif)'; it can be that 'al-'alim' is the subject and 'al-sharif' is the predicate, and it can be the opposite, as in their saying: 'The silent one is a speaker'; it can be understood that the silent one is a speaker, and it can be understood that the speaker is silent. As for in sophistry (mushaghaha), the difference in concept is according to the composition of the singular terms, like the saying of someone: 'Knowledge of writing' (ma'rifat al-kitaba) can be understood as knowledge whose knower is writing, and it can be understood as knowledge whose known is writing; and its composition... [text breaks off]
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage provides examples and further analysis of the verbal fallacies listed earlier, specifically focusing on how they affect syllogisms. The author begins with a general principle: in a valid syllogism, if equivocation or other verbal fallacies occur, the relation of the middle term to the extremes becomes inconsistent, and the syllogism is not truly a syllogism. Conversely, the fact that verbal fallacies corrupt syllogisms serves as a proof that these are the genera of verbal fallacies. The author then gives detailed examples. The first example illustrates equivocation (ishtirak al-ism) with the word 'ya'lam' (know). A sophist argues that a learner both knows and does not know: if he does not know, he is not a learner (since a learner seeks what he does not know), but if he knows, he does not need to learn. The fallacy arises because 'know' can mean either acquiring knowledge or already possessing knowledge. Similarly, the verb 'ya'qil' can mean either to think or to possess intellect. Another equivocation example uses 'wajib' (necessary/obligatory). The sophist asks whether some evil is 'wajib'. If it is necessary (wajib al-wujud), then it is good (since all necessary existence is good), so some evil is good. If it is not necessary, then it does not exist at all. The fallacy is that 'wajib' has two meanings: necessary existence vs. praiseworthy action. The author then moves to the fallacy of composition (mumarah). Here the error stems not from a single word but from the combination of words. For instance: 'What man knows, he knows; he knows stone; therefore he is stone.' The word 'he' (huwa) shifts reference: in the minor premise it refers to the known object, in the conclusion it refers to the man. Another example: 'What you said is existent; you are existent; you said stone is existent; therefore you are existent as stone.' The phrase 'you' can be subject or emphasis, causing confusion. The author also discusses ambiguity due to word order (taqdim wa ta'khir). For example, 'al-'alimu sharifun' could mean 'the scholar is noble' or 'the noble is a scholar'. Similarly, 'al-sakitu mutakallimun' could mean 'the silent one is speaking' or 'the speaker is silent'. Finally, the author begins an example of composition ambiguity with 'ma'rifat al-kitaba' (knowledge of writing), where the construct phrase can be interpreted as knowledge that writes (active) or knowledge of writing (passive). This passage serves to illustrate the concrete operation of the six verbal fallacies in actual argumentation.

Key Terms
اشتراك الاسم
يعلم
يتعقل
واجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example with 'ya'lam', what are the two meanings that cause the fallacy?
A) To think and to possess intellect.
B) To acquire knowledge and to already possess knowledge.
C) To teach and to learn.
D) To exist and to be obligatory.

Key Takeaway
Equivocation fallacies occur when a key term shifts meaning within an argument, making the reasoning invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the ambiguous term in this argument: 'Every action that is wajib must be done; prayer is wajib; therefore prayer must be done.' Does the argument commit equivocation? Why or why not? (Hint: Consider the two meanings of 'wajib'.)

Sophistry and Fallacies

Composition Fallacy (Mushaghaha): Examples with 'He' and 'You'
Examples of composition fallacy (mushaghaha) with pronouns

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to detect composition fallacies where the meaning of a phrase changes due to how its parts are combined.

Core Idea
Composition fallacy occurs when a term's reference changes within the same argument due to syntactic composition.

The author now turns to composition fallacies (mushaghaha or mumarah), where the error arises not from a single word but from the way words are combined. The examples involve shifts in the reference of pronouns like 'huwa' (he) and 'anta' (you). Understanding these helps us see how even simple sentences can be manipulated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous phrase
Find the phrase where the same word or expression can refer to different entities depending on parsing. In the first example, 'huwa' in 'he knows' can refer to the man or to the known stone.
2
Trace the shift in reference
Note how the pronoun 'huwa' in the premise 'he knows stone' refers to the stone (the known), but in the conclusion 'he is stone' it refers to the man. This shift invalidates the inference.
3
Analyze the second example with 'anta'
The phrase 'anta' in 'what you said is existent' can be interpreted as the subject or as an emphasis. When the sophist says 'you are existent as stone', the meaning changes based on how 'anta' is attached.
4
Understand the principle
The author explains that the error stems from 'istinad ajza' al-tarkib ba'diha ila ba'd' – the dependence of the parts of the composition on each other. The fallacy lies in exploiting different syntactic relationships.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القياس يوجب عليك أنه إذا وقع من اشتراك الاسم أو الاستعجام أو غير ذلك وجب أن تختلف نسبة الوسط إلى الطرفين بل نسبة الطرفين النتيجة فيعرض لا محالة أن لا يكون القياس في الحقيقة قياسًا. والقياس يوجب عليك عكس هذا أيضًا وهو أن أجناس المغالطات اللفظية هي هذه؛ وسيرد عليك هذا القياس في موضعه من بعد ومثال التسكيت المغالطي لاشتراك الاسم كمن يقول للمتعلم إنه يعلم ولا يعلم؛ فإن لم يعلم فليس بمتعلم، وإن علم فليس يحتاج إلى أن يتعلم والمغالطة في هذا أن قوله يعلم يعني به أنه يحصل له العلم ويكذب إذا كان بمعنى حصل له العلم. وربَّما كان لفظة «يتعقل» في لغة العرب دالة على الفكرة، وربَّما كان دالة على حصول العقل نفسه. وكذلك قول قائل هل شيء من الشرور بواجب أو ليس بواجب؛ فإن كان واجبًا، وكل واجب خير فبعض الشرور خير. وإن كان ليس بواجب فلا يوجد البتة فإن ما لا يجب فليس بموجود. والمغالطة بسبب أن الواجب وجوده غير الواجب العمل به؛ وإنما يقال لهما واجب باشتراك الاسم. ومفهوم الواجب الأول أن وجوده ضروري، ومفهوم الواجب الآخر أن إيثاره محمود. وكذلك يقال في الذي هو قائم إنه هو القاعد بعينه، أو لا يكون؛ وإنما كان يكون قاعدًا وقائما معا، أو لا يكون قادرًا على أن يقعده وهذه الأمثلة فيما يقع باشتراك اللفظ. وما يقع باشتراك اللفظ فذلك باعتبار لفظ مفرد. وأما المشاغبة أعني المماراة فلا يكون الغلط فيها باعتبار لفظ مفرد بل يكون لاختلاف مفهوم التركيب كمن يقول ما يعلمه الإنسان فهو ما يعلمه، ويعلم الحجر فهو حجر؛ والمغالطة بسبب أن لفظه هو تارة يشير إلى المعلوم وتارة إلى الإنسان. وكذلك ما قلته موجودًا أنتَ موجود هو وقلت إن الحجر موجود فأنتَ موجود حجرًا لأن قولك أنتَ يجوز أن يفهم أنتَ موضوعا وموجودًا هو محمول عليه، ويجوز أن يكون أنتَ هو تأكيد لقوله قلته أو صلة لقوله قلت. ويجوز أن يقال الغلط في هذا على جهة أخرى ما قلت إنه موجود أنتَ ذاك موجود؛ وقد قلت إن الحجر موجود. ويكون هذا فيه أظهر؛ فهذا ما يقع الغلط فيه بسبب استناد أجزاء التركيب بعضها إلى بعض. وأما ما يكون بسبب اختلاف إبهام التقديم والتأخير فذلك كمن يقول إن العالم شريف؛ فإنه يجوز أن يكون العالم موضوعا والشريف محمولا، ويجوز أن يكون على العكس كما في قولهم إن الساكت متكلم أمكن أن يفهم أن الساكت متكلم، وأمكن أن يفهم أن المتكلم ساكت . وأما في المشاغبة فاختلاف المفهوم بحسب التركيب بين المفردات كقول القائل معرفة الكتابة فقد يفهم منه معرفة يكون العارف بها الكتابة، ويفهم منه معرفة يكون المعروف بها الكتابة؛ وتركيبه
Then syllogism imposes upon you that if there occurs equivocation (ishtirak al-ism) or barbarism (al-ist'jam) or otherwise, there must be a difference in the relation of the middle term to the two extremes, rather the relation of the two extremes to the conclusion, so it necessarily occurs that the syllogism is not in reality a syllogism. And syllogism also imposes upon you the converse of this, namely that the genera of verbal fallacies are these; and this syllogism will come to you in its place later. An example of a sophistical silencing (taskeet) due to equivocation is like someone who says to the learner: he knows and does not know; if he does not know then he is not a learner, and if he knows then he does not need to learn. The fallacy in this is that his statement 'he knows' means that he obtains knowledge, and it is false if it means that he has already obtained knowledge. And sometimes the word 'ya'qil' in the Arabic language indicates thought, and sometimes it indicates the acquisition of intellect itself. Similarly, the saying of someone: 'Is something of evils necessary (wajib) or not necessary?' If it is necessary, and every necessary is good, then some evils are good. If it is not necessary, then it does not exist at all, for what is not necessary does not exist. The fallacy is due to the fact that the necessary of existence is different from the necessary of action; they are both called 'wajib' by equivocation. The meaning of the first 'wajib' is that its existence is necessary, and the meaning of the other is that its preference is praiseworthy. Similarly, it is said of the one who is standing that he is the sitting one itself, or not; and he could only be sitting and standing together, or he is not able to sit. These examples fall under equivocation of the word. What falls under equivocation is with respect to a single word. As for sophistry (mushaghaha), meaning mumarah, error in it does not occur with respect to a single word but rather due to difference in the concept of composition, like someone who says: 'What man knows, he knows, and he knows stone, so he is stone.' The fallacy is due to the fact that the word 'he' sometimes refers to the known thing and sometimes to man. Similarly: 'What you said is existent, you are existent,' and you said 'stone is existent' so you are existent as a stone. Because your saying 'you' can be understood as 'you' being the subject and 'is existent' as the predicate applied to it, and it can be that 'you' is an emphasis for your saying 'you said it' or a connective for your saying 'you said'. And it can be said that the error in this is in another way: 'What you said is existent: you are that existent'; and you said 'stone is existent'. This is more apparent. So this is what causes error due to the dependence of the parts of composition on each other. As for what is due to difference in ambiguity of precedence and postponement, it is like someone who says: 'The scholar (al-'alim) is noble (al-sharif)'; it can be that 'al-'alim' is the subject and 'al-sharif' is the predicate, and it can be the opposite, as in their saying: 'The silent one is a speaker'; it can be understood that the silent one is a speaker, and it can be understood that the speaker is silent. As for in sophistry (mushaghaha), the difference in concept is according to the composition of the singular terms, like the saying of someone: 'Knowledge of writing' (ma'rifat al-kitaba) can be understood as knowledge whose knower is writing, and it can be understood as knowledge whose known is writing; and its composition... Word Order Ambiguity: Examples with 'Scholar' and 'Silent'
Examples of word order ambiguity (taqdim wa ta'khir) with 'scholar' and 'silent'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recognize fallacies caused by ambiguous word order (taqdim wa ta'khir) where switching subject and predicate changes meaning.

Core Idea
Changing the order of words can invert a proposition's meaning, creating a fallacy.

The final type of verbal fallacy the author discusses is ambiguity due to word order (taqdim wa ta'khir). In Arabic, the subject-predicate order is flexible, and this can cause misunderstandings. The author gives examples with 'al-'alimu sharifun' and 'al-sakitu mutakallimun'. Recognizing this helps us see how even simple declarative sentences can be ambiguous.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous sentence
Take the sentence 'al-'alimu sharifun'. It can be parsed as 'the scholar is noble' (subject 'alim, predicate sharif) or 'the noble is a scholar' (subject sharif, predicate 'alim).
2
Recognize the two interpretations
Write down both possible meanings. They are often opposites or differ significantly.
3
See how the sophist exploits it
The sophist might use one interpretation in a premise and the other in the conclusion, or argue that if one is true, the other must also be true.
4
Understand the word order ambiguity
The author notes that in such cases, the sentence can be understood in two ways depending on which word is taken as subject and which as predicate.

The fallacy arises because 'know' can mean either acquiring knowledge or already possessing knowledge. Similarly, the verb 'ya'qil' can mean either to think or to possess intellect. Another equivocation example uses 'wajib' (necessary/obligatory). The sophist asks whether some evil is 'wajib'. If it is necessary (wajib al-wujud), then it is good (since all necessary existence is good), so some evil is good. If it is not necessary, then it does not exist at all. The fallacy is that 'wajib' has two meanings: necessary existence vs. praiseworthy action. The author then moves to the fallacy of composition (mumarah). Here the error stems not from a single word but from the combination of words. For instance: 'What man knows, he knows; he knows stone; therefore he is stone.' The word 'he' (huwa) shifts reference: in the minor premise it refers to the known object, in the conclusion it refers to the man. Another example: 'What you said is existent; you are existent; you said stone is existent; therefore you are existent as stone.' The phrase 'you' can be subject or emphasis, causing confusion. The author also discusses ambiguity due to word order (taqdim wa ta'khir). For example, 'al-'alimu sharifun' could mean 'the scholar is noble' or 'the noble is a scholar'. Similarly, 'al-sakitu mutakallimun' could mean 'the silent one is speaking' or 'the speaker is silent'. Finally, the author begins an example of composition ambiguity with 'ma'rifat al-kitaba' (knowledge of writing), where the construct phrase can be interpreted as knowledge that writes (active) or knowledge of writing (passive). This passage serves to illustrate the concrete operation of the six verbal fallacies in actual argumentation.

Key Terms
التقديم والتأخير
العالم
الشريف
الساكت
متكلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'al-sakitu mutakallimun', what are the two possible interpretations?
A) The silent one is a speaker, and the speaker is silent.
B) The silent one is silent, and the speaker is speaking.
C) The silent one is a mute, and the speaker is articulate.
D) The silent one is the same as the speaker.

Key Takeaway
Word order ambiguity (taqdim wa ta'khir) can completely reverse the meaning of a sentence, leading to fallacious arguments when the two interpretations are conflated.

The sentence 'al-mu'minu salihun' could be parsed as 'the believer is righteous' or 'the righteous is a believer'. Explain how this ambiguity could be exploited in a fallacy if used in an argument about salvation.

The Fallacy of Equivocation: Wājib and Qā'im
Author explains the fallacy of equivocation using the terms wajib and qa'im.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and analyze the fallacy of equivocation (ishtirāk al-lafẓ) using examples from Islamic theology and jurisprudence, distinguishing between different meanings of terms like wājib and qā'im.

Core Idea
Equivocation conceals a semantic shift; the argument's validity depends on which meaning you use.

The author warns against confusing two distinct meanings of the word 'wājib': necessary existence and obligatory action. This classic logical fallacy—equivocation—arises when a single term is used in two different senses within the same argument, making the conclusion seem valid when it is not. Understanding this fallacy is crucial for sound reasoning in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the equivocal term in the argument
Locate the word that appears twice but with different meanings. In the source passage, the word 'wājib' is used first to mean 'necessary in existence' and then to mean 'obligatory in action'.
2
Distinguish the two meanings
State the two distinct definitions. For 'wājib', one is ontological (necessary existence), the other is ethical (obligatory action).
3
Trace the argument's premises
See how each premise uses a different sense of the term: 'Every wājib (necessary) is good' uses the first sense; 'Some evils are wājib (obligatory)' uses the second. The conclusion 'some evils are good' follows only if the term is univocal.
4
Explain why the argument fails
Because the term is equivocal, the premises do not share a common subject; the argument commits the fallacy of equivocation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واجبًا، وكل واجب خير فبعض الشرور خير. وإن كان ليس بواجب فلا يوجد البتة فإن ما لا يجب فليس بموجود. والمغالطة بسبب أن الواجب وجوده غير الواجب العمل به؛ وإنما يقال لهما واجب باشتراك الاسم. ومفهوم الواجب الأول أن وجوده ضروري، ومفهوم الواجب الآخر أن إيثاره محمود. وكذلك يقال في الذي هو قائم إنه هو القاعد بعينه، أو لا يكون؛ وإنما كان يكون قاعدًا وقائما معا، أو لا يكون قادرًا على أن يقعده وهذه الأمثلة فيما يقع باشتراك اللفظ.
Necessary, and every necessary is good, so some evils are good. And if it is not necessary, it does not exist at all, because whatever is not necessary does not exist. The fallacy is because the necessary in existence is different from the necessary to act by; rather, they are both called 'necessary' by equivocation. The concept of the first necessary is that its existence is necessary, and the concept of the other necessary is that its choice is praiseworthy. Similarly, it is said of the one who is standing that he is the same as the sitting one, or he is not; rather, he would be both sitting and standing together, or he is not able to make him sit. These examples occur by equivocation.
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage defines the fallacy of equivocation (اشتراك اللفظ). The author first presents a problematic argument: 'Every necessary is good, so some evils are good.' The fallacy lies in the term 'واجب' which is used for necessary existence (as in logical necessity) and for obligatory action (in ethics). The two meanings are homonymous. Then another example: the word 'قائم' (standing) and 'قاعد' (sitting) can be used equivocally to produce paradoxical statements. This guards against confusing different senses of a term in logical reasoning. In Islamic jurisprudence, for instance, the term 'wajib' is used for actions that are obligatory, while in kalam it can mean logically necessary. Equivocating these leads to invalid syllogisms.

Key Terms
اشتراك اللفظ (ishtirāk al-lafẓ): A word that has multiple distinct meanings, causing fallacy when its different senses are confused in an argument. [Note: No significant shift; stable term across logical tradition.]
واجب (wājib): Here, two meanings: (1) necessary in existence (logical necessity), (2) obligatory to act (ethical obligation). [Note: In jurisprudence, wājib predominantly means obligatory; in philosophy, it often means necessary being.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which fallacy is committed when someone argues: 'Prayer is wājib (obligatory), and the existence of the Creator is wājib (necessary), so prayer is the existence of the Creator'?
A) Composition fallacy
B) Equivocation
C) Begging the question
D) Division fallacy

Key Takeaway
Equivocation occurs when a single term is used with two different meanings within an argument, rendering the conclusion invalid if the shift is unnoticed.

Distinguishing Structural Ambiguity from Equivocation
Author distinguishes single-word equivocation from structural ambiguity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the fallacy of equivocation (single-word) and the fallacy of structural ambiguity (mushāghaba), and identify the latter in a given argument.

Core Idea
Structural ambiguity masquerades as logical proof by exploiting dependent relationships between words.

The author explains that fallacies are not limited to single words with multiple meanings. Entire structures can mislead when the syntactic relations are ambiguous. This lesson unpacks the first example: the pronoun 'هو' in a compound sentence causing confusion between the known and the knower.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of fallacy
Find where the author contrasts fallacies of single-word equivocation with those of structural composition.
2
Analyze the example sentence
Read the sentence: 'ما يعلمه الإنسان فهو ما يعلمه، ويعلم الحجر فهو حجر'. Notice how the pronoun 'هو' can refer back to either 'ما' (the known) or 'الإنسان' (the human).
3
Explain the fallacy
The argument appears to prove something but actually shifts the referent, making it a form of sophistry.
This is a subtle error that often goes unnoticed.
4
Generalize the principle
Structural ambiguity arises when the same pronoun or relational word can attach to different antecedents in the sentence structure.
This is not a case of a word having two meanings, but of the sentence allowing two different parse trees.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يقع باشتراك اللفظ فذلك باعتبار لفظ مفرد. وأما المشاغبة أعني المماراة فلا يكون الغلط فيها باعتبار لفظ مفرد بل يكون لاختلاف مفهوم التركيب كمن يقول ما يعلمه الإنسان فهو ما يعلمه، ويعلم الحجر فهو حجر؛ والمغالطة بسبب أن لفظه هو تارة يشير إلى المعلوم وتارة إلى الإنسان. وكذلك ما قلته موجودًا أنتَ موجود هو وقلت إن الحجر موجود فأنتَ موجود حجرًا لأن قولك أنتَ يجوز أن يفهم أنتَ موضوعا وموجودًا هو محمول عليه، ويجوز أن يكون أنتَ هو تأكيد لقوله قلته أو صلة لقوله قلت. ويجوز أن يقال الغلط في هذا على جهة أخرى ما قلت إنه موجود أنتَ ذاك موجود؛ وقد قلت إن الحجر موجود. ويكون هذا فيه أظهر؛ فهذا ما يقع الغلط فيه بسبب استناد أجزاء التركيب بعضها إلى بعض. وأما ما يكون بسبب اختلاف إبهام التقديم والتأخير فذلك كمن يقول إن العالم شريف؛ فإنه يجوز أن يكون العالم موضوعا والشريف محمولا، ويجوز أن يكون على العكس كما في قولهم إن الساكت متكلم أمكن أن يفهم أن الساكت متكلم، وأمكن أن يفهم أن المتكلم ساكت . وأما في المشاغبة فاختلاف المفهوم بحسب التركيب بين المفردات كقول القائل معرفة الكتابة فقد يفهم منه معرفة يكون العارف بها الكتابة، ويفهم منه معرفة يكون المعروف بها الكتابة؛ وتركيبه يوقع كثرة في مفهومه . وكل واحد من لفظي الكتابة والمعرفة ليس بمشترك في هذا الموضع. وأما الذي بالتركيب فهو أن يكون للقول عند التركيب حكم فيطلب أن يصدق ذلك الحكم عند التفصيل ؛ ويكون الغلط في التركيب كقول من يقول قد يمكن للجالس أن يمشي ، والذي ليس بكاتب أن يكتب. فإنه لما عطف قوله الذي ليس بكاتب أن يكتب عطفه على أنه في مثل حكمه من الإمكان، وعلى أنه حكمه حكم المعطوف عليه، فإن فصل هذا كذب. وإنما كان يصدق مركَّبًا على الإمكان والقوة فكان معناه؛ والذي ليس بكاتب هو بالقوة كاتب.
What occurs by equivocation is by a single word. As for sophistry, i.e., disputation, the error in it is not by a single word but by difference in the meaning of the structure, like someone saying: 'What a human knows is what he knows, and a stone knows, so it is a stone.' The fallacy is because his word 'هو' sometimes refers to the known and sometimes to the human. Similarly, what I said: 'You are existing you are existing,' and I said 'The stone is existing, so you are existing a stone,' because your word 'أنت' can be understood as 'you' as subject and 'existing' as predicate, or 'أنت' can be an emphasis of 'قلته' or a connection of 'قلت'. And it may be said the error in this is in another way: I said 'It is existing you are that existing' and I said 'The stone is existing.' This is clearer. So this is what occurs due to the dependence of parts of the structure on each other. As for what occurs due to ambiguity of precedence and delay, that is like someone saying 'The scholar is noble'; it is possible that 'scholar' is subject and 'noble' predicate, or the reverse, as in their saying 'The silent is speaking' – it may be understood that the silent is speaking, or that the speaker is silent. As for sophistry, difference of meaning according to structure between individual words, like someone saying 'knowledge of writing' – it may be understood as knowledge whose knower is writing, or knowledge whose known is writing; its structure produces multiplicity in its meaning. Each of the words 'writing' and 'knowledge' is not equivocal in this context. As for what is by composition, it is that a statement when composed has a ruling, and one demands that ruling be true when detailed; the error in composition is like someone saying: 'It is possible for the sitting one to walk, and for the one who is not a writer to write.' Because when he juxtaposed his statement 'the one who is not a writer to write', he juxtaposed it as being in the same ruling of possibility, and that its ruling is the ruling of the conjunct, but if he separates it, it is false. Rather, it is true when composed as possibility and potentiality, so its meaning is: the one who is not a writer is potentially a writer.
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage explains fallacies due to structural ambiguity (المشاغبة). It distinguishes equivocation (single word) from structural ambiguity. Several examples are given: 'ما يعلمه الإنسان...' where 'هو' is ambiguous; 'أنت موجود' where grammatical function changes; ambiguity due to subject-predicate order (like 'العالم شريف' can be read as 'the scholar is noble' or 'the noble is scholar'); and composition where a statement is true as a whole but false when parts are taken separately, like 'the one who is not a writer can write' – true in potential sense, not actual. This guards against misinterpreting syntactic structures in argumentation. In Islamic legal reasoning, such ambiguities can lead to incorrect interpretations of texts or rulings.

Key Terms
المشاغبة
التركيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main difference between equivocation and structural ambiguity according to the passage?
A) Equivocation involves full sentences; structural ambiguity involves single words.
B) Equivocation involves a single ambiguous word; structural ambiguity involves the arrangement of words.
C) Both are exactly the same.
D) Structural ambiguity only occurs in Greek logic.

Key Takeaway
Fallacies are not always due to ambiguous words; often the syntax itself is the culprit, requiring careful analysis of sentence structure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Ambiguity of Word Order and Construct Phrases
Author explains fallacies of ambiguous word order and ambiguous idafa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify fallacies arising from ambiguous subject-predicate order and from ambiguous construct phrases (idafa).

Core Idea
Word order and genitive constructions can yield opposite meanings, hiding a fallacy.

The author demonstrates that even without pronoun ambiguity, the order of subject and predicate can reverse the intended meaning. Similarly, a construct phrase like 'knowledge of writing' can have two interpretations. This lesson dissects these examples.

Guided Path
1
Examine the subject-predicate reversal example
Look at the sentence 'العالم شريف'. Explain the two possible readings: 'the scholar is noble' and 'the noble is scholar'.
2
Repeat for the second example
The sentence 'الساكت متكلم' can be read as 'the silent is speaking' or 'the speaker is silent'. Identify which reading is logically intended.
3
Analyze the idafa example
The phrase 'معرفة الكتابة' can mean either 'knowledge possessed by writing' or 'knowledge of writing'. Show how two different meanings arise from the same two words.
The source explicitly says neither 'معرفة' nor 'كتابة' is ambiguous alone.
4
Compare to prior fallacy
Note that in these examples, the ambiguity comes from structure, not from words. This reinforces the general principle.

intermediate

The Fallacy of Composition in Potential Statements
Author explains the composition fallacy with potential vs actual.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of composition where a statement is true as a whole but false when its parts are separated.

Core Idea
A statement true in potential can be false in actual — don't confuse the whole with its parts.

The author illustrates a subtle fallacy: a compound statement may be true when taken as a whole (e.g., as potential), but when broken down into separate claims, each part becomes false. The example of the non-writer who can write (potentially) shows this. This lesson teaches how to spot such fallacies in theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compound statement
Look at the sentence: 'قد يمكن للجالس أن يمشي، والذي ليس بكاتب أن يكتب.'
2
Understand why it is true as a whole
When read together, the sentence means 'It is possible for the sitter to walk, and it is possible for the non-writer to write' – this is true because it speaks of potentiality.
3
See what happens when separated
If we take 'الذي ليس بكاتب أن يكتب' alone, it seems to claim that the non-writer is actually writing, which is false. The fallacy is treating the compound as if each part holds independently.
4
Generalize the fallacy type
This is the fallacy of composition (المغالطة بالتركيب) where the whole's truth does not ensure each part's truth when details are considered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يقع باشتراك اللفظ فذلك باعتبار لفظ مفرد. وأما المشاغبة أعني المماراة فلا يكون الغلط فيها باعتبار لفظ مفرد بل يكون لاختلاف مفهوم التركيب كمن يقول ما يعلمه الإنسان فهو ما يعلمه، ويعلم الحجر فهو حجر؛ والمغالطة بسبب أن لفظه هو تارة يشير إلى المعلوم وتارة إلى الإنسان. وكذلك ما قلته موجودًا أنتَ موجود هو وقلت إن الحجر موجود فأنتَ موجود حجرًا لأن قولك أنتَ يجوز أن يفهم أنتَ موضوعا وموجودًا هو محمول عليه، ويجوز أن يكون أنتَ هو تأكيد لقوله قلته أو صلة لقوله قلت. ويجوز أن يقال الغلط في هذا على جهة أخرى ما قلت إنه موجود أنتَ ذاك موجود؛ وقد قلت إن الحجر موجود. ويكون هذا فيه أظهر؛ فهذا ما يقع الغلط فيه بسبب استناد أجزاء التركيب بعضها إلى بعض. وأما ما يكون بسبب اختلاف إبهام التقديم والتأخير فذلك كمن يقول إن العالم شريف؛ فإنه يجوز أن يكون العالم موضوعا والشريف محمولا، ويجوز أن يكون على العكس كما في قولهم إن الساكت متكلم أمكن أن يفهم أن الساكت متكلم، وأمكن أن يفهم أن المتكلم ساكت . وأما في المشاغبة فاختلاف المفهوم بحسب التركيب بين المفردات كقول القائل معرفة الكتابة فقد يفهم منه معرفة يكون العارف بها الكتابة، ويفهم منه معرفة يكون المعروف بها الكتابة؛ وتركيبه يوقع كثرة في مفهومه . وكل واحد من لفظي الكتابة والمعرفة ليس بمشترك في هذا الموضع. وأما الذي بالتركيب فهو أن يكون للقول عند التركيب حكم فيطلب أن يصدق ذلك الحكم عند التفصيل ؛ ويكون الغلط في التركيب كقول من يقول قد يمكن للجالس أن يمشي ، والذي ليس بكاتب أن يكتب. فإنه لما عطف قوله الذي ليس بكاتب أن يكتب عطفه على أنه في مثل حكمه من الإمكان، وعلى أنه حكمه حكم المعطوف عليه، فإن فصل هذا كذب. وإنما كان يصدق مركَّبًا على الإمكان والقوة فكان معناه؛ والذي ليس بكاتب هو بالقوة كاتب.
What occurs by equivocation is by a single word. As for sophistry, i.e., disputation, the error in it is not by a single word but by difference in the meaning of the structure, like someone saying: 'What a human knows is what he knows, and a stone knows, so it is a stone.' The fallacy is because his word 'هو' sometimes refers to the known and sometimes to the human. Similarly, what I said: 'You are existing you are existing,' and I said 'The stone is existing, so you are existing a stone,' because your word 'أنت' can be understood as 'you' as subject and 'existing' as predicate, or 'أنت' can be an emphasis of 'قلته' or a connection of 'قلت'. And it may be said the error in this is in another way: I said 'It is existing you are that existing' and I said 'The stone is existing.' This is clearer. So this is what occurs due to the dependence of parts of the structure on each other. As for what occurs due to ambiguity of precedence and delay, that is like someone saying 'The scholar is noble'; it is possible that 'scholar' is subject and 'noble' predicate, or the reverse, as in their saying 'The silent is speaking' – it may be understood that the silent is speaking, or that the speaker is silent. As for sophistry, difference of meaning according to structure between individual words, like someone saying 'knowledge of writing' – it may be understood as knowledge whose knower is writing, or knowledge whose known is writing; its structure produces multiplicity in its meaning. Each of the words 'writing' and 'knowledge' is not equivocal in this context. As for what is by composition, it is that a statement when composed has a ruling, and one demands that ruling be true when detailed; the error in composition is like someone saying: 'It is possible for the sitting one to walk, and for the one who is not a writer to write.' Because when he juxtaposed his statement 'the one who is not a writer to write', he juxtaposed it as being in the same ruling of possibility, and that its ruling is the ruling of the conjunct, but if he separates it, it is false. Rather, it is true when composed as possibility and potentiality, so its meaning is: the one who is not a writer is potentially a writer.
Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage explains fallacies due to structural ambiguity (المشاغبة). It distinguishes equivocation (single word) from structural ambiguity. Several examples are given: 'ما يعلمه الإنسان...' where 'هو' is ambiguous; 'أنت موجود' where grammatical function changes; ambiguity due to subject-predicate order (like 'العالم شريف' can be read as 'the scholar is noble' or 'the noble is scholar'); and composition where a statement is true as a whole but false when parts are taken separately, like 'the one who is not a writer can write' – true in potential sense, not actual. This guards against misinterpreting syntactic structures in argumentation. In Islamic legal reasoning, such ambiguities can lead to incorrect interpretations of texts or rulings.

Key Terms
التركيب
القوة
الفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is the statement 'الذي ليس بكاتب أن يكتب' true?
A) When taken as a separate claim
B) When interpreted as potentiality
C) Never
D) Only if the person is actually writing

Key Takeaway
Do not assume that the truth of a compound statement implies the truth of each isolated part, especially when modality is involved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement: 'The person who has not performed Hajj can perform Hajj.' Explain how this could commit the fallacy of composition if taken out of context. What must be added to make it valid?
