The Fallacy of Composition: True Whole from False Parts
Author explains composition fallacy with conditional example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of composition in arguments where the truth of the whole is inferred from supposedly true parts, and distinguish it from valid logical reasoning about conditionals.

Core Idea
A composite can be true even when its parts are false.

In logical analysis, we often encounter statements made up of parts. The fallacy of composition mistakenly assumes that if the overall statement is true, then each part must also be true. The author provides a striking counterexample: the conditional 'If a human were a stone, then a human is inanimate' is a true logical implication, yet both its antecedent and consequent are false. This lesson trains you to spot such fallacies in theological arguments where a valid form may hide false content.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fallacy
The fallacy of composition occurs when one concludes that parts are true because the whole is true. The example in the passage is a conditional that is true even though its parts are false.
2
Analyze the conditional example
The conditional 'If a human were a stone, then a human is inanimate' is true because the antecedent is impossible, not because humans are stones. In logic, a conditional with a false antecedent is always true (material implication). Thus the whole is true, but the parts are false.
3
Connect to Islamic science
In Islamic theology (kalam), one might mistakenly argue that since a conditional argument like 'If the world were eternal, it would be necessary' is valid, its premises must be true. This is a composition fallacy because the truth of the conditional does not guarantee the truth of its components.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم المركَّب قد يكون كاذبًا وأجزاؤه صادقة؛ وهذا يكون على العكس كقول القائل لو كانت الخمسة زوجًا كان الزوج لا ينقسم. وأما الذي من القسمة فذلك أن يكون الشيء عند التحليل صادقا، وعند التركيب غير صادق؛ وذلك التحليل إما بحسب الموضوع وإما بحسب نفس القول. والذي بحسب الموضوع إما أن يكون صادقًا على أجزاء الشيء جمعًا أو على الأجزاء تفريقًا؛ وإن للشيء أجزاء أو لها أحكام في التفصيل فيجعل الشيء أجزاء نفسه، وإنما أحكامه التي بالتفصيل، وربما كانت متقابلة: مثال الأول قول القائل إن الخمسة زوج و فرد، وكل ما هو زوج و فرد زوج فالخمسة زوج؛ وليس كذلك بل الزوج جزء منها، والفرد جزء. وليست هي بحسبها زوجا وفردًا وإن كان في نفسه فردًا، وكذلك قول القائل إن الأعظم مساءٍ وزيادة فهو مساو. ومثال الثاني أن الخمسة ثلاثة واثنان فهي ثلاثة واثنان معا؛ وهذا خلف. والذي بحسب القول مثل قولهم إن كان الإنسان حجرًا فالإنسان جماد؛ وهذا تركيب صادق من منفصلتين كاذبتين. وكذلك قد يكون القول باختلاف التركيبين والمنفصلتين، ومن الأمثلة قولهم أنا أستعيذ بك جزء ربّك وهو يعني أنا أستعيذ بك وهو
Then the composite may be false while its parts are true; and this may be the reverse, like someone saying: 'If five were even, then the even would be indivisible.' As for what is from division, that is when something is true when analyzed, but false when composed. That analysis is either according to the subject or according to the statement itself. According to the subject, it is either true of the parts of the thing collectively or of the parts individually; and the thing has parts or has rulings in detail, so one makes the thing consist of its own parts, and its rulings are only in detail, and they might be contradictory. Example of the first: someone says: 'Five is even and odd, and everything that is even and odd is even, so five is even.' This is not so; rather, 'even' is part of it, and 'odd' is part. It is not by virtue of itself even and odd, although it is in itself odd. Similarly, the saying: 'The greater is equal and more, so it is equal.' Example of the second: 'Five is three and two, so it is three and two together'; this is a contradiction. As for according to the statement, like their saying: 'If a human were a stone, then a human is inanimate'; this is a true composite of two false disjuncts. Similarly, the statement may be with difference of two compositions and two disjuncts, and among the examples is their saying: 'I seek refuge in you, part of your Lord' meaning 'I seek refuge in you' and...
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This passage discusses fallacies where the composite statement is false but its parts are true (composition fallacy) and vice versa (division fallacy). Examples include mathematical statements about the number five: 'Five is even and odd' – true individually but false together as a composite; and 'Five is three and two' – true as parts but false as a composite claim of identity. Also, conditional statements like 'If man were a stone, then man is inanimate' is a true conditional (if-then) even though both parts are false. The text introduces analysis according to subject (analyzing a whole into parts) and according to the statement itself (analyzing conditional structure). In Islamic theology, one might commit a division fallacy by arguing that since the world has parts that are created, the whole world must be created (true), but then concluding that it is temporally originated (which is a separate claim). This guards against conflating properties of parts with the whole.

Key Terms
المركب (al-murakkab): A composite statement or whole; its truth value is not necessarily determined by its parts. [Note: In grammar, murakkab can mean compound word; here it is logical composition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of the fallacy of composition?
A) 'If five were even, then the even would be indivisible' – the conditional is true but its parts are false.
B) 'Five is three and two' – true analysis but false composite.
C) 'Every man is mortal; Socrates is a man; therefore Socrates is mortal.'
D) 'The world is composed of atoms; each atom is created; therefore the world is created.'

Key Takeaway
The truth or falsehood of the whole does not automatically distribute to the parts; beware of the fallacy of composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a conditional statement in a kalam text, such as 'If the world were eternal, it would be necessary', and determine whether it is a true conditional. Then check if anyone mistakenly infers the truth of the premises from the truth of the conditional.

The Fallacy of Division: False Whole from True Parts
Author explains division fallacy with number example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of division in arguments where a property of the parts is wrongly attributed to the whole when they are combined.

Core Idea
What is true for the parts separately may be false for the whole.

The fallacy of division is the mirror image of composition: it occurs when one assumes that because something is true when the parts are taken separately (analyzed), it remains true when those parts are combined into a whole. The author gives the example: 'Five is three and two' – it is true that five can be decomposed into three and two, but it is false to say that the number five itself is simultaneously three and two. This fallacy is especially relevant in debates about divine attributes and created entities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fallacy
The fallacy of division occurs when a property true of the parts in isolation is incorrectly applied to the whole.
2
Analyze the number example
The statement 'Five is three and two' is true when we are analyzing (dividing) five into its components, but it is false when we say 'Five is three and two' as a composite identity, because five is not equal to both three and two simultaneously.
3
Apply to theology
In kalam, one might argue: 'The world consists of parts; each part is temporally originated; therefore the world is temporally originated.' This argument is actually valid because temporality is additive. A fallacious division would be: 'The world has properties that are contradictory in its parts, so the whole is contradictory' – e.g., some parts are hot, some cold, so the world is both hot and cold – that is a division fallacy because the properties of parts do not always apply to the whole in the same way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage discusses fallacies where the composite statement is false but its parts are true (composition fallacy) and vice versa (division fallacy). Examples include mathematical statements about the number five: 'Five is even and odd' – true individually but false together as a composite; and 'Five is three and two' – true as parts but false as a composite claim of identity. Key Terms
القسمة (al-qismah): Division or analysis of a whole into parts; the fallacy arises from improper handling of parts. [Note: Stable in logic; also means division in mathematics.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which fallacy is committed by the argument: 'The number five is composed of two and three, therefore five is two and three simultaneously'?
A) Composition

intermediate

Error by Composition (al-Tarkib)
Explanation of error by composition with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and analyze a composition fallacy in an Islamic argument.

Core Idea
A composite statement may be false even if every part is true separately.

In this lesson, we examine the first type of logical fallacy: error by composition. This occurs when a statement is true when considered as a whole but false when analyzed into its parts, or vice versa. Ibn Sina provides clear examples from everyday reasoning that help us detect such fallacies in Islamic scholarly discourse.

Guided Path
1
Define composition fallacy
Understand the definition: error occurs when a ruling that holds for a composite statement is incorrectly applied to its parts when separated.
2
Examine the example of the sitter and non-writer
Analyze the statement: 'It is possible for the sitter to walk, and the one who is not a writer to write.' Taken as a composite, it is true potentially; but if separated, each part becomes false.
This illustrates that a composite can be true while its parts are false.
3
Examine the example of 'if five were even'
Consider the conditional: 'If five were even, then the even would not be divisible.' Here the composite is false but its parts (antecedent and consequent) could be true individually.
This is the converse: a false composite from true parts.
4
Identify composition fallacies in Islamic reasoning
Practice by finding cases where a ruling on a compound (e.g., a fiqh ruling on a composite act) is mistakenly applied to each component.
For example, if an act is permissible in combination, it does not mean each part is permissible alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يوقع كثرة في مفهومه. وكل واحد من لفظي الكتابة والمعرفة ليس بمشترك في هذا الموضع. وأما الذي بالتركيب فهو أن يكون للقول عند التركيب حكم فيطلب أن يصدق ذلك الحكم عند التفصيل؛ ويكون الغلط في التركيب كقول من يقول قد يمكن للجالس أن يمشي، والذي ليس بكاتب أن يكتب. فإنه لما عطف قوله الذي ليس بكاتب أن يكتب عطفه على أنه في مثل حكمه من الإمكان، وعلى أنه حكمه حكم المعطوف عليه، فإن فصل هذا كذب. وإنما كان يصدق مركَّبًا على الإمكان والقوة فكان معناه؛ والذي ليس بكاتب هو بالقوة كاتب. ثم المركَّب قد يكون كاذبًا وأجزاؤه صادقة؛ وهذا يكون على العكس كقول القائل لو كانت الخمسة زوجًا كان الزوج لا ينقسم. وأما الذي من القسمة فذلك أن يكون الشيء عند التحليل صادقًا، وعند التركيب غير صادق؛ وذلك التحليل إما بحسب الموضوع وإما بحسب نفس القول. والذي بحسب الموضوع إما أن يكون صادقًا على أجزاء الشيء جمعًا أو على الأجزاء تفريقًا؛ وإن للشيء أجزاء أو لها أحكام في التفصيل فيجعل الشيء أجزاء نفسه، وإنما أحكامه التي بالتفصيل، وربَّما كانت متقابلة: مثال الأول قول القائل إن الخمسة زوج وفرد، وكل ما هو زوج وفرد زوج فالخمسة زوج؛ وليس كذلك بل الزوج جزء منها، والفرد جزء. وليست هي بحسبها زوجًا وفردًا وإن كان في نفسه فردًا، وكذلك قول القائل إن الأعظم مساوٍ وزيادة فهو مساوٍ. ومثال الثاني أن الخمسة ثلاثة واثنان فهي ثلاثة واثنان معًا؛ وهذا خلف. والذي بحسب القول مثل قولهم إن كان الإنسان حجرًا فالإنسان جماد؛ وهذا تركيب صادق من منفصلتين كاذبتين. وكذلك قد يكون القول باختلاف التركيبين والمنفصلتين، ومن الأمثلة قولهم أنا أستعيذ بك جزء ربّك وهو يعني أنا أستعيذ بك وهو صادق، وأنا جزء ربّك وهو صادق؛ فإذا أخذ مركَّبًا على أنه يقول أنا أستعيذ بك حق ما جزء ربك حتى يكونا مركبين معًا كان كاذبًا. وأما الذي بالإعجام فمن الناس من قصره على المكتوب؛ والشيخ يجعله أعم من ذلك وهو أن يعتبر المعنى بترك الإعراب أو يعتبره لفظًا، وبالنثرات والتثقيلات والتخفيفات والمدات والتشديدات بحسب العادات في اللغات، وبالعجم كتابة: مثال الأول قيل عمر بتسكين الراء فلا يبتدئ أن عمر فاعل أو مفعول به. مثال الثاني أن يقول بدل قوله: إِنَّ عَلَيْنَا جَمَعَهُ وَقُرْآنَهُ [القيامة: ١٧]، مثال الثالث أن ينقط على قوله ما أطرف زيدًا ينقطه من يجب فيصير ما أظرف زيدًا. وكذلك جميع ما يختلف بالتشديد والتليين والمد والقصر؛ وتتشابه حروفه في الأصل، ويختلف بالنقط. وأما المتعلق بشكل اللفظ فإن تختلف مفهوماته باختلاف أشكال التصاريف والتأنيث والتذكير والفاعل والمفعول حتى يكون عند بعضهم الألم فاعلًا شيئًا أو الراجح[؟]؛ ويكون قول القائل إن الهيولي قابلة بطبعها فعلاً ما فهذه هي الأنحاء التي تقع تشبيهاً الغلط من جهة هذه لا غير عند الشيخ. وذلك لأن اللفظ إذا طابق المعنى لم يقع من جهته غلط، وإذا لم يطابق فإما أن يدل أو لا يدل. فإن لم يدل لم يقع من غلط أيضًا؛ إذ الفهم من الشرائط. وإن دل على معنى فواضح أن ذلك المعنى قد يفهم منه وحده أو يفهم منه لا وحده؛ فإن كان يفهم منه وحده فإما أن يكون هو منفرد وإما أن يكون وهو مركب فإن كان منفردًا فإما أن يكون من جوهره وإما أن يكون من حال فيه وإما أن يكون من حال تلحقه من خارج؛ فإن كان في جوهره فهو المشترك في جوهره، وإن كان في حاله فهو المشترك في شكله وهيئته، وإن كان من حال تلحقه من خارج فهو المشترك بحسب ما اللفظ؛ وهي جهته.
It causes multiplicity in its concept. And each of the two words writing and knowledge is not ambiguous in this place. As for that which is by composition, it is that a statement has a ruling in composition, and one seeks that ruling to be true in analysis; and the error in composition is like the saying of one who says: 'It is possible for the sitter to walk, and the one who is not a writer to write.' For when he conjoined his saying 'the one who is not a writer to write' he conjoined it as being in the same ruling of possibility, and that its ruling is the ruling of the conjoined-to, then if he separates it, it is false. And it was only true as a composite based on possibility and potentiality, so its meaning was: 'the one who is not a writer is potentially a writer.' Then the composite may be false while its parts are true; and this is conversely like the saying of one who says: 'If five were even, then the even would not be divisible.' And as for that which is by division, it is that a thing is true in analysis, but not true in composition; and that analysis is either according to the subject or according to the statement itself. That which is according to the subject is either true of the parts of the thing collectively or of the parts separately; and the thing has parts or has rulings in detail, so one makes the thing [i.e., the whole] into its own parts, and its rulings are only those in detail, and they may be opposite: example of the first is the saying of one who says: 'Five is even and odd, and everything that is even and odd is even, therefore five is even'; but it is not so, rather even is part of it, and odd is part. And it is not, by virtue of them, even and odd, although in itself it is odd; similarly the saying of one who says: 'The greater is equal and more, therefore it is equal.' And example of the second is that five is three and two, so it is three and two together; and this is a contradiction. And that which is according to the statement is like their saying: 'If man were stone, then man is inanimate'; this is a true composition from two false disjuncts. And similarly the statement may be with different compositions and disjuncts, and among the examples is their saying: 'I seek refuge in You, part of Your Lord' meaning: 'I seek refuge in You' is true, and 'I am part of Your Lord' is true; but if it is taken as a composite meaning 'I seek refuge in You truly as part of Your Lord' so that both are together, it is false. And as for that which is by ambiguity, some people limit it to writing; but the Master makes it more general than that, and it is that one considers the meaning by dropping inflection or considers it as a word, and by vowelizations, doublings, lightenings, elongations, and geminations according to customary practices in languages, and by non-Arabic writing: example of the first is saying 'Umar' with sukun on the ra, so one does not know whether 'Umar' is subject or object. Example of the second is when one says instead of His saying: 'Indeed upon Us is its gathering and its recitation' [Qiyamah 17]; example of the third is when one dots [the diacritics] on his saying 'ma atraba Zaydan' and dots it as required, so it becomes 'ma azrafa Zaydan'. And similarly all that differs by gemination, softening, lengthening and shortening; and its letters are similar in origin, and differ by dotting. And as for that which is related to the form of the word, its meanings differ according to the different forms of conjugation, femininity and masculinity, subject and object, so that according to some, 'pain' is an agent of something or the pedestrian[?]; and the saying of one who says 'Hyle is receptive by nature of an actual action' — these are the ways in which error occurs from this aspect, no other according to the Master. And that is because if the word matches the meaning, no error occurs from its direction; and if it does not match, then either it indicates or does not indicate. If it does not indicate, no error occurs from it either, for understanding is among the conditions. And if it indicates a meaning, then it is clear that that meaning may be understood from it alone or not alone; if it is understood from it alone, then it is either simple or composite; if it is simple, then either it is from its essence, or from a state in it, or from a state that attaches to it from outside; if it is from its essence, then it is the common in its essence; if it is from its state, then it is the common in its form and shape; and if it is from a state that attaches to it from outside, then it is the common according to what the word is; and that is its aspect.
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of three main sources of logical fallacy: composition (tarkib), division (qisma), and ambiguity (i'jam). The author first explains composition error: when a statement is true as a whole but false when analyzed into parts, or vice versa. The example given is 'the sitter can walk and the non-writer can write'—true as a potential composite but false when each part is considered separately. Another example is 'if five were even, then the even would not be divisible'—a false composite from true parts. Next, division error: when something true in analysis is false when composed. This has two subtypes: according to the subject (e.g., saying 'five is even and odd'—true of parts but false of the whole) and according to the statement itself (e.g., 'if man were stone, man is inanimate'—a true conditional from two false antecedents). The example of 'I seek refuge in You part of Your Lord' illustrates a composite that is false even though each part is true. Finally, ambiguity error: this includes phonetic ambiguity (e.g., 'Umar' with sukun on ra making it unclear whether it is subject or object), substitution of words (e.g., misquoting a verse), and dotting (diacritical) ambiguity (e.g., 'atraba' vs 'azrafa'). The Master (Ibn Sina) extends this to include all variations in pronunciation and writing. The author then gives a general principle: error arises only when the word does not match the meaning; if it does not indicate, no error occurs because understanding fails; if it indicates, the error stems from the nature of the word (essence, state, or external attachment). This classification helps the logician identify and avoid fallacies in reasoning.

Key Terms
التركيب
التحليل
الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best illustrates the fallacy of composition as described in the passage?
A) Since each player on the team is skilled, the team is skilled.
B) Since the team is winning, each player must be winning.
C) Since water is wet, each molecule of water is wet.
D) Since the building is heavy, each brick is heavy.

Key Takeaway
Composition fallacy arises when truth of parts is mistaken for truth of whole, or vice versa, ignoring how composition can change truth conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a fatwa where the permissibility of a compound transaction is justified by the permissibility of its individual components. Identify if this involves a composition fallacy. Use an example from a fiqh manual.
?

Sophistry and Fallacies
intermediate
5 min
Error by Division (al-Qisma)
Explanation of error by division with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and analyze a division fallacy in Islamic scholarly arguments.

Core Idea
Dividing a whole into parts may make a true statement appear false or a false statement appear true.

The second major source of logical error is division, which is essentially the inverse of composition. Here, a statement that is true when the parts are analyzed becomes false when composed into a whole, or vice versa. Ibn Sina categorizes division fallacy into two subtypes: according to the subject and according to the statement itself. This lesson unpacks these with Islamic examples.

Guided Path
1
Define division fallacy
Understand that error occurs when something true in analysis (taking parts separately) is assumed true of the composite whole, or when something true of the whole is assumed true of each part.
2
Examine the 'five is even and odd' example
The statement 'Five is even and odd' is true if 'even' and 'odd' refer to different parts of five (e.g., two parts: 3 and 2). But the composite claim 'five is even and odd' is false because five itself is odd.
This is error by division according to the subject: the parts have opposite properties, but the whole does not.
3
Examine the 'greater is equal and more' example
Saying 'The greater is equal and more' is similar: if 'greater' refers to one part and 'equal' to another, the composite is false.
4
Examine the conditional example
The statement 'If man were stone, then man is inanimate' is a true conditional (if antecedent true, consequent true), though both antecedent and consequent are false. This illustrates division fallacy according to the statement itself: a true statement made of false parts.
Here the 'parts' are the disjuncts (antecedent and consequent).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يوقع كثرة في مفهومه. وكل واحد من لفظي الكتابة والمعرفة ليس بمشترك في هذا الموضع. وأما الذي بالتركيب فهو أن يكون للقول عند التركيب حكم فيطلب أن يصدق ذلك الحكم عند التفصيل؛ ويكون الغلط في التركيب كقول من يقول قد يمكن للجالس أن يمشي، والذي ليس بكاتب أن يكتب. فإنه لما عطف قوله الذي ليس بكاتب أن يكتب عطفه على أنه في مثل حكمه من الإمكان، وعلى أنه حكمه حكم المعطوف عليه، فإن فصل هذا كذب. وإنما كان يصدق مركَّبًا على الإمكان والقوة فكان معناه؛ والذي ليس بكاتب هو بالقوة كاتب. ثم المركَّب قد يكون كاذبًا وأجزاؤه صادقة؛ وهذا يكون على العكس كقول القائل لو كانت الخمسة زوجًا كان الزوج لا ينقسم. وأما الذي من القسمة فذلك أن يكون الشيء عند التحليل صادقًا، وعند التركيب غير صادق؛ وذلك التحليل إما بحسب الموضوع وإما بحسب نفس القول. والذي بحسب الموضوع إما أن يكون صادقًا على أجزاء الشيء جمعًا أو على الأجزاء تفريقًا؛ وإن للشيء أجزاء أو لها أحكام في التفصيل فيجعل الشيء أجزاء نفسه، وإنما أحكامه التي بالتفصيل، وربَّما كانت متقابلة: مثال الأول قول القائل إن الخمسة زوج وفرد، وكل ما هو زوج وفرد زوج فالخمسة زوج؛ وليس كذلك بل الزوج جزء منها، والفرد جزء. وليست هي بحسبها زوجًا وفردًا وإن كان في نفسه فردًا، وكذلك قول القائل إن الأعظم مساوٍ وزيادة فهو مساوٍ. ومثال الثاني أن الخمسة ثلاثة واثنان فهي ثلاثة واثنان معًا؛ وهذا خلف. والذي بحسب القول مثل قولهم إن كان الإنسان حجرًا فالإنسان جماد؛ وهذا تركيب صادق من منفصلتين كاذبتين. وكذلك قد يكون القول باختلاف التركيبين والمنفصلتين، ومن الأمثلة قولهم أنا أستعيذ بك جزء ربّك وهو يعني أنا أستعيذ بك وهو صادق، وأنا جزء ربّك وهو صادق؛ فإذا أخذ مركَّبًا على أنه يقول أنا أستعيذ بك حق ما جزء ربك حتى يكونا مركبين معًا كان كاذبًا. وأما الذي بالإعجام فمن الناس من قصره على المكتوب؛ والشيخ يجعله أعم من ذلك وهو أن يعتبر المعنى بترك الإعراب أو يعتبره لفظًا، وبالنثرات والتثقيلات والتخفيفات والمدات والتشديدات بحسب العادات في اللغات، وبالعجم كتابة: مثال الأول قيل عمر بتسكين Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of three main sources of logical fallacy: composition (tarkib), division (qisma), and ambiguity (i'jam). The author first explains composition error: when a statement is true as a whole but false when analyzed into parts, or vice versa. The example given is 'the sitter can walk and the non-writer can write'—true as a potential composite but false when each part is considered separately. Another example is 'if five were even, then the even would not be divisible'—a false composite from true parts. Next, division error: when something true in analysis is false when composed. This has two subtypes: according to the subject (e.g., saying 'five is even and odd'—true of parts but false of the whole) and according to the statement itself (e.g., 'if man were stone, man is inanimate'—a true conditional from two false antecedents). The example of 'I seek refuge in You part of Your Lord' illustrates a composite that is false even though each part is true. Finally, ambiguity error: this includes phonetic ambiguity (e.g., 'Umar' with sukun on ra making it unclear whether it is subject or object), substitution of words (e.g., misquoting a verse), and dotting (diacritical) ambiguity (e.g., 'atraba' vs 'azrafa'). The Master (Ibn Sina) extends this to include all variations in pronunciation and writing. The author then gives a general principle: error arises only when the word does not match the meaning; if it does not indicate, no error occurs because understanding fails; if it indicates, the error stems from the nature of the word (essence, state, or external attachment). This classification helps the logician identify and avoid fallacies in reasoning.

C) 'If man were stone, then man is inanimate.'

Key Takeaway
Division fallacy occurs when what holds for parts in analysis is wrongly assumed to hold for the whole, or when a truth of the whole is broken down incorrectly into parts.

Sophistry and Fallacies

Error by Ambiguity (al-I'jam)
Explanation of error by ambiguity with subtypes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and analyze ambiguity fallacies arising from pronunciation, diacritics, and script in Islamic texts.

Core Idea
Ambiguity in words (i'jam) can cause false conclusions even from true premises.

The third source of logical fallacy is ambiguity (i'jam), which Ibn Sina expands beyond mere dotting to include all phonetic and script variations that affect meaning. This is especially relevant for Islamic scholarship where the precise reading of Qur'anic verses, hadith, and legal texts is paramount. This lesson explores the subtypes of ambiguity and their impact on reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Define ambiguity fallacy
Understand that i'jam (ambiguity) occurs when a word can be interpreted in multiple ways due to pronunciation (tashkil), writing (nuqt), or form (shakl), leading to different meanings.
2
Examine the phonetic ambiguity example
Consider the name 'Umar' (عمر) with sukun on the ra. Without vowel signs, it is unclear whether 'Umar' is the subject (fā'il) or object (maf'ūl) of a sentence.
This demonstrates how dropping inflection (i'rab) causes ambiguity.
3
Examine the substitution and dotting examples
The author gives examples: misquoting a verse (substitution of words) and changing diacritics: 'ma atraba Zaydan' (how dusty Zayd is) vs. 'ma azrafa Zaydan' (how witty Zayd is). A single dot changes the meaning entirely.
Such ambiguity can lead to false interpretations in religious texts.
4
Understand the general principle
The author concludes that error arises when the word does not match its intended meaning. If the word does not indicate any meaning, no error occurs (since understanding fails). If it indicates a meaning, the error stems from the word's essence, state, or external attachment.
This classification helps diagnose the source of ambiguity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يوقع كثرة في مفهومه. وكل واحد من لفظي الكتابة والمعرفة ليس بمشترك في هذا الموضع. وأما الذي بالتركيب فهو أن يكون للقول عند التركيب حكم فيطلب أن يصدق ذلك الحكم عند التفصيل؛ ويكون الغلط في التركيب كقول من يقول قد يمكن للجالس أن يمشي، والذي ليس بكاتب أن يكتب. فإنه لما عطف قوله الذي ليس بكاتب أن يكتب عطفه على أنه في مثل حكمه من الإمكان، وعلى أنه حكمه حكم المعطوف عليه، فإن فصل هذا كذب. وإنما كان يصدق مركَّبًا على الإمكان والقوة فكان معناه؛ والذي ليس بكاتب هو بالقوة كاتب. ثم المركَّب قد يكون كاذبًا وأجزاؤه صادقة؛ وهذا يكون على العكس كقول القائل لو كانت الخمسة زوجًا كان الزوج لا ينقسم. وأما الذي من القسمة فذلك أن يكون الشيء عند التحليل صادقًا، وعند التركيب غير صادق؛ وذلك التحليل إما بحسب الموضوع وإما بحسب نفس القول. والذي بحسب الموضوع إما أن يكون صادقًا على أجزاء الشيء جمعًا أو على الأجزاء تفريقًا؛ وإن للشيء أجزاء أو لها أحكام في التفصيل فيجعل الشيء أجزاء نفسه، وإنما أحكامه التي بالتفصيل، وربَّما كانت متقابلة: مثال الأول قول القائل إن الخمسة زوج وفرد، وكل ما هو زوج وفرد زوج فالخمسة زوج؛ وليس كذلك بل الزوج جزء منها، والفرد جزء. وليست هي بحسبها زوجًا وفردًا وإن كان في نفسه فردًا، وكذلك قول القائل إن الأعظم مساوٍ وزيادة فهو مساوٍ. ومثال الثاني أن الخمسة ثلاثة واثنان فهي ثلاثة واثنان معًا؛ وهذا خلف. والذي بحسب القول مثل قولهم إن كان الإنسان حجرًا فالإنسان جماد؛ وهذا تركيب صادق من منفصلتين كاذبتين. وكذلك قد يكون القول باختلاف التركيبين والمنفصلتين، ومن الأمثلة قولهم أنا أستعيذ بك جزء ربّك وهو يعني أنا أستعيذ بك وهو صادق، وأنا جزء ربّك وهو صادق؛ فإذا أخذ مركَّبًا على أنه يقول أنا أستعيذ بك حق ما جزء ربك حتى يكونا مركبين معًا كان كاذبًا. وأما الذي بالإعجام فمن الناس من قصره على المكتوب؛ والشيخ يجعله أعم من ذلك وهو أن يعتبر المعنى بترك الإعراب أو يعتبره لفظًا، وبالنثرات والتثقيلات والتخفيفات والمدات والتشديدات بحسب العادات في اللغات، وبالعجم كتابة: مثال الأول قيل عمر بتسكين الراء فلا يبتدئ أن عمر فاعل أو مفعول به. مثال الثاني أن يقول بدل قوله: إِنَّ عَلَيْنَا جَمَعَهُ وَقُرْآنَهُ [القيامة: ١٧]، مثال الثالث أن ينقط على قوله ما أطرف زيدًا ينقطه من يجب فيصير ما أظرف زيدًا. وكذلك جميع ما يختلف بالتشديد والتليين والمد والقصر؛ وتتشابه حروفه في الأصل، ويختلف بالنقط. وأما المتعلق بشكل اللفظ فإن تختلف مفهوماته باختلاف أشكال التصاريف والتأنيث والتذكير والفاعل والمفعول حتى يكون عند بعضهم الألم فاعلًا شيئًا أو الراجح[؟]؛ ويكون قول القائل إن الهيولي قابلة بطبعها فعلاً ما فهذه هي الأنحاء التي تقع تشبيهاً الغلط من جهة هذه لا غير عند الشيخ. وذلك لأن اللفظ إذا طابق المعنى لم يقع من جهته غلط، وإذا لم يطابق فإما أن يدل أو لا يدل. فإن لم يدل لم يقع من غلط أيضًا؛ إذ الفهم من الشرائط. وإن دل على معنى فواضح أن ذلك المعنى قد يفهم منه وحده أو يفهم منه لا وحده؛ فإن كان يفهم منه وحده فإما أن يكون هو منفرد وإما أن يكون وهو مركب فإن كان منفردًا فإما أن يكون من جوهره وإما أن يكون من حال فيه وإما أن يكون من حال تلحقه من خارج؛ فإن كان في جوهره فهو المشترك في جوهره، وإن كان في حاله فهو المشترك في شكله وهيئته، وإن كان من حال تلحقه من خارج فهو المشترك بحسب ما اللفظ؛ وهي جهته.
It causes multiplicity in its concept. And each of the two words writing and knowledge is not ambiguous in this place. As for that which is by composition, it is that a statement has a ruling in composition, and one seeks that ruling to be true in analysis; and the error in composition is like the saying of one who says: 'It is possible for the sitter to walk, and the one who is not a writer to write.' For when he conjoined his saying 'the one who is not a writer to write' he conjoined it as being in the same ruling of possibility, and that its ruling is the ruling of the conjoined-to, then if he separates it, it is false. And it was only true as a composite based on possibility and potentiality, so its meaning was: 'the one who is not a writer is potentially a writer.' Then the composite may be false while its parts are true; and this is conversely like the saying of one who says: 'If five were even, then the even would not be divisible.' And as for that which is by division, it is that a thing is true in analysis, but not true in composition; and that analysis is either according to the subject or according to the statement itself. That which is according to the subject is either true of the parts of the thing collectively or of the parts separately; and the thing has parts or has rulings in detail, so one makes the thing [i.e., the whole] into its own parts, and its rulings are only those in detail, and they may be opposite: example of the first is the saying of one who says: 'Five is even and odd, and everything that is even and odd is even, therefore five is even'; but it is not so, rather even is part of it, and odd is part. And it is not, by virtue of them, even and odd, although in itself it is odd; similarly the saying of one who says: 'The greater is equal and more, therefore it is equal.' And example of the second is that five is three and two, so it is three and two together; and this is a contradiction. And that which is according to the statement is like their saying: 'If man were stone, then man is inanimate'; this is a true composition from two false disjuncts. And similarly the statement may be with different compositions and disjuncts, and among the examples is their saying: 'I seek refuge in You, part of Your Lord' meaning: 'I seek refuge in You' is true, and 'I am part of Your Lord' is true; but if it is taken as a composite meaning 'I seek refuge in You truly as part of Your Lord' so that both are together, it is false. And as for that which is by ambiguity, some people limit it to writing; but the Master makes it more general than that, and it is that one considers the meaning by dropping inflection or considers it as a word, and by vowelizations, doublings, lightenings, elongations, and geminations according to customary practices in languages, and by non-Arabic writing: example of the first is saying 'Umar' with sukun on the ra, so one does not know whether 'Umar' is subject or object. Example of the second is when one says instead of His saying: 'Indeed upon Us is its gathering and its recitation' [Qiyamah 17]; example of the third is when one dots [the diacritics] on his saying 'ma atraba Zaydan' and dots it as required, so it becomes 'ma azrafa Zaydan'. And similarly all that differs by gemination, softening, lengthening and shortening; and its letters are similar in origin, and differ by dotting. And as for that which is related to the form of the word, its meanings differ according to the different forms of conjugation, femininity and masculinity, subject and object, so that according to some, 'pain' is an agent of something or the pedestrian[?]; and the saying of one who says 'Hyle is receptive by nature of an actual action' — these are the ways in which error occurs from this aspect, no other according to the Master. And that is because if the word matches the meaning, no error occurs from its direction; and if it does not match, then either it indicates or does not indicate. If it does not indicate, no error occurs from it either, for understanding is among the conditions. And if it indicates a meaning, then it is clear that that meaning may be understood from it alone or not alone; if it is understood from it alone, then it is either simple or composite; if it is simple, then either it is from its essence, or from a state in it, or from a state that attaches to it from outside; if it is from its essence, then it is the common in its essence; if it is from its state, then it is the common in its form and shape; and if it is from a state that attaches to it from outside, then it is the common according to what the word is; and that is its aspect.
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of three main sources of logical fallacy: composition (tarkib), division (qisma), and ambiguity (i'jam). The author first explains composition error: when a statement is true as a whole but false when analyzed into parts, or vice versa. The example given is 'the sitter can walk and the non-writer can write'—true as a potential composite but false when each part is considered separately. Another example is 'if five were even, then the even would not be divisible'—a false composite from true parts. Next, division error: when something true in analysis is false when composed. This has two subtypes: according to the subject (e.g., saying 'five is even and odd'—true of parts but false of the whole) and according to the statement itself (e.g., 'if man were stone, man is inanimate'—a true conditional from two false antecedents). The example of 'I seek refuge in You part of Your Lord' illustrates a composite that is false even though each part is true. Finally, ambiguity error: this includes phonetic ambiguity (e.g., 'Umar' with sukun on ra making it unclear whether it is subject or object), substitution of words (e.g., misquoting a verse), and dotting (diacritical) ambiguity (e.g., 'atraba' vs 'azrafa'). The Master (Ibn Sina) extends this to include all variations in pronunciation and writing. The author then gives a general principle: error arises only when the word does not match the meaning; if it does not indicate, no error occurs because understanding fails; if it indicates, the error stems from the nature of the word (essence, state, or external attachment). This classification helps the logician identify and avoid fallacies in reasoning.

Key Terms
الإعجام
الشكل
النقط
التشديد
المد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT an example of ambiguity (i'jam) as defined by the Master (Ibn Sina)?
A) Saying 'Umar' with sukun on the ra.
B) Misquoting a Qur'anic verse.
C) Changing 'atraba' to 'azrafa' by changing dots.
D) Saying 'the sitter can walk' as a composite.

Key Takeaway
Ambiguity fallacy arises from phonetic, orthographic, or morphological multiplicity in words, which can cause misinterpretation of premises and false conclusions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Sophistry and Fallacies
intermediate

Six Types of Fallacies Due to Linguistic Ambiguity
Author classifies fallacies arising from linguistic ambiguity into six types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the six types of fallacies arising from pronunciation (lafz) according to the classification in this passage.

Core Idea
Words can cause fallacies when they don't match meanings; there are exactly six ways this happens.

This passage from al-Imam al-Taftazani's commentary on logic classifies fallacies that stem from the ambiguity of words. Understanding this classification helps us identify the source of equivocation in arguments, a crucial skill in Islamic debate (munazara). We will parse the four-level tree that yields six distinct types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the root condition
The passage states that fallacy occurs when the word does not match the meaning. If it matches, no error. Also, if the word indicates a meaning, then fallacy may arise. So first step is to check the relationship between lafz and ma'na.
This is the foundation: lafz must be non-matching and indicative.
2
Distinguish indication alone vs. not alone
The passage splits: the meaning indicated may be understood from the word alone (if it is the whole meaning) or not alone (requires something else). This directs to the main branches.
If alone, go to simple vs. compound; if not alone, it involves composition or division.
3
If alone, split into simple vs. compound
A word that conveys its meaning alone can be either simple (mufrad) or compound (murakkab). Simple is a single term; compound is a phrase. Wait to see how each causes ambiguity.
Simple words have three subtypes; compound have three as well.
4
Analyze the three types of simple words
Simple words can be ambiguous by essence (true homonym, e.g., 'ayn meaning eye or spring), by form (same shape but different meaning due to conjugation or grammatical role), or by accidental features (like dots or diacritics, e.g., changing tashdid or sukun).
These are common by essence (mushtarak fi dhatihi), common by form (mushtarak fi shaklihi), and common by accidental like i'jam.
5
Analyze the three types of compound words
Compound phrases can be ambiguous in themselves (compositional ambiguity, mushaghhabi), or when combined with others leading to fallacy of composition (taking separated parts as combined) or fallacy of division (taking combined as separated).
These three: common by composition itself, composition fallacy, and division fallacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صادق، وأنا جزء ربّك وهو صادق؛ فإذا أخذ مركبا على أنه يقول أنا أستعيذ بك حق ما جزء ربك حتى يكونا مركبين معا كان كاذبًا. وأما الذي بالإعجام فمن الناس من قصره على المكتوب؛ والشيخ يجعله أعم من ذلك وهو أن يعتبر المعنى بترك الإعراب أو يعتبره لفظا، وبالنثرات والتثقيلات والتخفيفات والمدات والتشديدات بحسب العادات في اللغات، وبالعجم كتابة: مثال الأول قيل عمر بتسكين الراء فلا يبتدئ أن عمر فاعل أو مفعول به. مثال الثاني أن يقول بدل قوله : إِنَّ عَلَيْنَا جَمَعَهُ، وَقُرْءَانَهُ ﴾ [القيامة: ١٧]، مثال الثالث أن ينقط على قوله ما أطرف زيدًا ينقطه من يجب فيصير ما أظرف زيدًا. وكذلك جميع ما يختلف بالتشديد والتليين والمد والقصر؛ وتتشابه خروفه في الأصل، ويختلف بالنقط. وأما المتعلق بشكل اللفظ فإن تختلف مفهوماته باختلاف أشكال التصاريف والتأنيث والتذكير والفاعل والمفعول حتى يكون عند بعضهم الألم فاعلا شيئًا أو الراجح؛ ويكون قول القائل إن الهيولي قابلة بطبعها فعلا ما فهذه هي الأنحاء التي تقع تشبيها الغلط من جهة هذه لا غير عند الشيخ. وذلك لأن اللفظ إذا طابق المعنى لم يقع من جهته غلط، وإذا لم يطابق فإما أن يدل أو لا يدل. فإن لم يدل لم يقع من غلط أيضًا؛ إذ الفهم من الشرائط . وإن دل على معنى فواضح أن ذلك المعنى قد يفهم منه وحده أو يفهم منه لا وحده؛ فإن كان يفهم منه وحده فإما أن يكون هو منفرد وإما أن يكون وهو مركب فإن كان منفردًا فإما أن يكون من جوهره وإما أن يكون من حال فيه وإما أن يكون من حال تلحقه من خارج؛ فإن كان في جوهره فهو المشترك في جوهره ، وإن كان في حاله فهو المشترك في شكله وهيئته، وإن كان من حال تلحقه من خارج فهو المشترك بحسب ما يلحقه من الإعجام والنقط وغير ذلك، وهذه أقسام ثلاثة. وإن كان مركبًا فإما أن يلحقه في نفسه وحده وهو الذي في تأليفه اشتراك وهو المشاغبي، وإما أن يلحقه لا وحده فيكون مع غيره؛ فيكون إما صدقه مع غيره أو لا صدقه مع غيره: فيكون إما التركيب وإما القسمة؛ فهذه أيضًا ثلاثة فالمجموع ستة.
True, and I am a part of your Lord and it is true. But if it is taken as a compound such that it says 'I seek refuge in You from the right of what is part of your Lord' so that both are compounded together, then it is false. As for that which is by pointing (i'jam), some people restrict it to the written; the Shaykh makes it more general than that, and it is that one considers the meaning by leaving i'rab or considers it as a word, and by diacritical marks, tashkil, tashdid, madd, etc., according to the customs of languages, and by non-Arabic writing. Example of the first: 'Umar' with sukun on the ra', so one does not begin whether 'Umar is subject or object. Example of the second: one says instead of His saying 'Indeed upon Us is its collection and its recitation' (al-Qiyama 75:17). Example of the third: one places dots on his saying 'ma atrafa Zaydan' by dotting it as required, so it becomes 'ma azrafa Zaydan'. Likewise all that differs by tashdid, softening, madd, and shortening; and its letters are similar in origin, and differ by dots. As for that which is related to the form of the word, its concepts vary according to the forms of conjugation, femininity, masculinity, subject and object, such that according to some, 'al-alama' (pain) is a subject of something or the preferrer; and the saying of the speaker that 'hyle is receptive by nature to an actual effect' — these are the ways in which fallacy occurs from this aspect only according to the Shaykh. That is because if the word matches the meaning, no error occurs from it; and if it does not match, then either it indicates or does not indicate. If it does not indicate, then no error occurs from it either, for understanding is from the conditions. And if it indicates a meaning, it is clear that that meaning may be understood from it alone or understood from it not alone. If it is understood from it alone, then either it is simple or it is compound. If it is simple, then either from its essence, or from a state in it, or from a state that attaches to it from outside. If from its essence, it is the common (mushtarak) in its essence; if from its state, it is the common in its form and shape; if from a state that attaches to it from outside, it is the common in terms of what attaches to it of i'jam, dots, etc. These are three divisions. And if it is compound, then either it attaches to it itself alone — and that is the one in whose composition there is sharing (commonality) and it is 'al-mushaghhabi' [likely 'common by composition'] — or it attaches to it not alone, so it is with another; then either its truth with another or not its truth with another: thus either composition or division; these are also three, so the total is six.
Source pages p. 586

Commentary
This passage is a classification of fallacies that arise due to the ambiguity of words (lafz). The author begins with an example to illustrate a fallacy of composition: the statement 'I am a part of your Lord' is true when each part is taken separately, but when combined as a compound declaration it becomes false. Then the author discusses the different ways words can cause fallacy. First, through i'jam (pointing or diacritical marks), which some restrict to writing, but the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) extends to include any consideration of meaning by leaving i'rab, or by considering the word itself with its vocalization and changes according to language customs, and by non-Arabic script. Examples are given: reading 'Umar' with sukun on the ra' ignores whether it is subject or object; changing Qur'anic recitation; and dotting the word 'ma atrafa' to become 'ma azrafa' (changing the meaning). Second, through the form of the word (shakl al-lafz) where conjugations, gender, and grammatical roles change the meaning. The author then provides a systematic classification: if the word does not match the meaning, either it indicates or does not indicate. If it indicates, then the meaning is understood either alone or not alone. If alone, then either the word is simple or compound. Simple words can be common by essence (like homonyms), by form (like similar shapes), or by accidental features (like pointing). Compound words can be common in themselves (composition) or in combination with others (leading to fallacies of composition or division). Thus there are six types in total. This classification helps the student identify the source of equivocation in logical arguments.

Key Terms
الإعجام
المشترك
المشاغبي

Key Takeaway
When a word does not match its meaning, it can cause fallacy in six distinct ways; knowing this classification equips you to diagnose equivocation in arguments.

The Seven Meaning-Based Fallacies
Author lists seven meaning-based fallacies and elaborates on the fallacy of accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and identify the seven fallacies that occur due to meaning as described in this passage.

Core Idea
The seven meaning-based fallacies are the traps of conceptual reasoning, distinct from linguistic fallacies.

After covering linguistic fallacies, the author turns to fallacies arising from the meaning of statements, not their wording. These seven are crucial for any scholar engaged in debate, as they expose common errors in argumentation. In this lesson, we will learn their names and basic definitions to build a foundation for recognizing them in Islamic discourse.

Guided Path
1
Locate the list in the Arabic
Find the sentence beginning with 'فهي سبعة' (they are seven) and identify each numbered fallacy.
The list is clearly enumerated; pay attention to the order.
2
Match each Arabic term to an English equivalent
For each fallacy, note the Arabic name and its common English translation. For example, 'بالعرض' is 'accident', 'المصادرة على المطلوب الأول' is 'begging the question'.
Some terms like 'التسكيت' may be obscure; understand the general sense.
3
Understand the meaning of each fallacy
Read the brief descriptions in the commentary to grasp how each fallacy distorts reasoning. Group them: some involve predication (accident, bad predication), others involve proof (begging the question, false cause).
The author will later elaborate on the first; focus on the list for now.
4
Distinguish meaning-based from wording-based fallacies
Note that these fallacies are due to conceptual confusion, not ambiguous words. They are more subtle and often occur in technical debates among theologians and jurists.
This distinction is key in the overall classification of fallacies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في المغالطات التي تقع بحسب المعنى ] وأما المغالطات التي تقع بحسب المعنى فهي سبعة؛ الأول من جهة بالعرض، والثاني من سوء اعتبار الحمل، والثالث من قلة العلم بالتسكيت. والرابع من إيهام عكس اللوازم والخامس من المصادرة على المطلوب الأول، والسادس من جعل ما ليس بعلة علة، والسابع من جمع المسائل الكثيرة في مسألة واحدة. [أ] أما الذي بالعرض فهو أن يؤخذ شيء عرض له معادية شيء على سبيل ما يعرض عروضًا غير واجب؛ فيؤخذ واجبًا تعرض له أعراض كثيرة فتجعل الأعراض بعضها محمولا على البعض في كل موضع، أو يعرض شيء لشيء فيؤخذ في حكمه مثل أن تقول إن زيدا غير عمرو، وعمرو إنسان فزيد غير إنسان؛ وهذا من جملة ما يتخبَّط فيه أهل الكلام في هذا الباب عند بعضهم أنه غلط لأنه غير منتج، ويكون من جهة الصورة لا من جهة المادة واحد بالعرض. وإنما هو غير منتج لأن الصغرى إما سالبة، وإما الأوسط ليس لمحمول لأصغر بل جزء محموله. وعند بعضهم إنه منتج نتيجة صادقة فإن زيدا غير إنسان ما؛ وهذا صحيح. فنقول إنه لما كان عمرا غيره، وأيضًا إنسان؛ فينتج أن عمرًا غير إنسان إذا كان عرض للإنسان إن كان غير عمرو: فأحدهما
Chapter on fallacies that occur according to meaning. As for the fallacies that occur according to meaning, they are seven: first, from the aspect of accident; second, from bad consideration of predication; third, from lack of knowledge of silencing (tasket); fourth, from the illusion of the converse of implications; fifth, from begging the question (musadara); sixth, from treating what is not a cause as a cause; seventh, from gathering many questions into one question. [A] As for the one by accident, it is that something which has an accidental attribute is taken in the manner of a necessary attribute, such that it is taken as necessary; or many accidents occur to it and those accidents are made predicated on each other in every place; or something occurs to something and is taken in its judgment, like when you say 'Zayd is not 'Amr, and 'Amr is a human, so Zayd is not a human.' This is among that which the theologians (ahl al-kalam) confuse in this topic. According to some, it is fallacious because it is non-productive, and it is from the aspect of form, not matter, of the fallacy of accident. And it is non-productive because the minor premise is either negative, or the middle term is not the predicate of the minor but a part of its predicate. According to others, it is productive with a true conclusion, for Zayd is indeed not a human. We say: since he ('Amr) is other than him (Zayd), and also a human, it follows that 'Amr is not a human if it is an accident of the human that he is other than 'Amr: so one of them...
Source pages p. 587

Commentary
This passage is a list of seven types of fallacies that occur due to the meaning (conceptual content) rather than due to wording. The author enumerates them and then begins to explain the first one, the fallacy of accident (al-'arad). The seven are: (1) accident, (2) bad consideration of predication (sū' i'tibar al-haml), (3) ignorance of refutation (qillat al-'ilm bil-taskīt), (4) illusion of the converse of implications (īhām 'aks al-lawāzim), (5) begging the question (musādara 'alā al-maṭlūb al-awwal), (6) false cause (ja'l mā laysa bi-'illa 'illa), and (7) combining many questions into one (jam' al-masā'il al-kathīra fī mas'ala wāḥida). The author then details the fallacy of accident: it occurs when an accidental property is treated as if it were essential or necessary. An example is given: 'Zayd is not 'Amr, and 'Amr is a human, therefore Zayd is not a human.' This argument is problematic because the premise 'Zayd is not 'Amr' is an accidental fact (Zayd could have been 'Amr), not an essential difference. The author notes that theologians (mutakallimūn) often fall into this confusion. Some say the argument is invalid because the middle term is not properly connected; the minor premise is negative or the middle term is only part of the predicate. Others claim it is valid and yields a true conclusion (Zayd is indeed not a human). The author's own view seems to be left incomplete, but the passage illustrates the importance of distinguishing essential from accidental attributes in logical reasoning.

The Fallacy of Accident Explained
Author elaborates on the fallacy of accident with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze an argument to determine if it commits the fallacy of accident by distinguishing accidental from essential attributes.

Core Idea
The fallacy of accident mistakes incidental attributes for necessary ones, invalidating the argument.

The first meaning-based fallacy is the fallacy of accident (al-'arad). The author illustrates it with the example: 'Zayd is not 'Amr, and 'Amr is human, so Zayd is not human.' This argument is controversial: some scholars deem it invalid because it treats an accidental relation as essential, while others accept it. In this lesson, we will dissect this example and learn how to spot the fallacy in Islamic discourse.

Guided Path
1
Define the fallacy of accident from the text
Locate where the author describes the fallacy: 'it is that something which has an accidental attribute is taken in the manner of a necessary attribute.' Understand that it involves treating an accidental property as essential.
The Arabic term 'بالعرض' means 'by accident' or 'incidentally'.
2
Extract the example argument
The author gives: 'Zayd is not 'Amr, and 'Amr is human, therefore Zayd is not human.' Write it in standard logical form: premise 1: Zayd is not 'Amr; premise 2: 'Amr is human; conclusion: Zayd is not human.
Note that the argument is presented as an example of the fallacy.
3
Identify why it is considered fallacious
The author explains that the minor premise ('Zayd is not 'Amr') is a negative or accidental relation, not an essential one. The middle term ('Amr') is not the predicate of the minor but only part of its predicate, so the syllogism is invalid in form.
Some scholars argue the argument is valid because the conclusion 'Zayd is not human' is true, but the author suggests the form is flawed.
4
Apply the concept to a new Islamic example
Consider: 'This act is not prayer, prayer is obligatory, so this act is not obligatory.' If 'this act' is accidentally not prayer (e.g., it is a permissible action), it may commit the fallacy of accident.
The key is whether the attribute 'not being prayer' is essential to the act or just a temporary state.

Enumeration of the Seven Fallacies by Meaning
Author lists the seven types of fallacies that occur by meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list each of the seven fallacies by meaning and identify the core error of each.

Core Idea
Seven fallacies by meaning: the material errors that corrupt reasoning.

In this lesson, we encounter a foundational classification in the study of fallacy (mughalata). The author distinguishes seven types of fallacies that arise from the content of propositions (bi-hasab al-ma'na), not their wording. Memorizing this list equips you to spot common logical pitfalls in theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the category
Fallacies by meaning (bi-hasab al-ma'na) are errors in the content of reasoning, as opposed to verbal errors (lafziyya). They corrupt the reasoning itself, not just its expression.
2
Memorize the seven Arabic names
Learn each fallacy by its Arabic name and number: 1. al-'arad (العرض) – accident; 2. su' i'tibar al-haml (سوء اعتبار الحمل) – bad predication; 3. qillat al-'ilm bi al-taskit (قلة العلم بالتسكيت) – ignorance of elenchus; 4. iham 'aks al-lawazim (إيهام عكس اللوازم) – illusion of converse of concomitants; 5. musadara 'ala al-matlab al-awwal (المصادرة على المطلوب الأول) – begging the question; 6. ja'l ma laysa bi 'illa 'illa (جعل ما ليس بعلة علة) – false cause; 7. jam' al-masa'il al-kathira fi mas'ala wahida (جمع المسائل الكثيرة في مسألة واحدة) – combining many issues.
Use mnemonic devices or index cards to memorize.
3
Understand each fallacy's core error
For each, identify what goes wrong: 1) treating accidental property as essential; 2) misapplying predication conditions; 3) refutation missing its mark due to lack of technique; 4) confusing necessary and sufficient conditions; 5) assuming the conclusion; 6) positing a non-cause as a cause; 7) overwhelming the opponent with multiple questions.
The source text does not elaborate; this understanding comes from the commentary tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في المغالطات التي تقع بحسب المعنى ]
وأما المغالطات التي تقع بحسب المعنى فهي سبعة؛ الأول من جهة بالعرض، والثاني من سوء اعتبار الحمل، والثالث من قلة العلم بالتسكيت. والرابع من إيهام عكس اللوازم والخامس من المصادرة على المطلوب الأول، والسادس من جعل ما ليس بعلة علة، والسابع من جمع المسائل الكثيرة في مسألة واحدة.
Chapter: [On Fallacies that Occur by Meaning]
As for the fallacies that occur by meaning, they are seven: first, from the aspect of accident; second, from bad consideration of predication; third, from lack of knowledge of elenchus; fourth, from illusion of the converse of concomitants; fifth, from begging the question; sixth, from taking what is not a cause as a cause; seventh, from combining many issues into one issue.
Source pages p. 587

Commentary
This passage is a classification of fallacies that occur by meaning (as opposed to verbal fallacies). The author enumerates seven distinct types, each defined by a specific error in the content of reasoning. The first is the fallacy of accident (bil-'arad), where an accidental property is treated as essential. The second is bad consideration of predication (sū' i'tibār al-ḥaml), involving mishandling of predication conditions. The third is ignorance of elenchus (qillat al-'ilm bi al-taskīt), where a refutation misses its target due to oversight. The fourth is illusion of the converse of concomitants (īhām 'aks al-lawāzim), confusing necessary and sufficient conditions. The fifth is begging the question (muṣādarah 'alā al-maṭlūb al-awwal). The sixth is taking what is not a cause as a cause (ja'l mā laysa bi 'illah 'illah). The seventh is combining many issues into one (jam' al-masā'il al-kathīrah fī mas'alah wāḥidah). This list serves as a map for the detailed discussions that follow.

Key Terms
المغالطة (al-mughālaṭah): fallacy
بحسب المعنى (bi-ḥasab al-ma'nā): by meaning

The Fallacy of Accident (Bil-'Arad)
Author defines and exemplifies the fallacy of accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of accident in a categorical syllogism and explain why it fails.

Core Idea
The fallacy of accident mistakes an accidental property for an essential one.

In this passage, the author defines the fallacy of accident and illustrates it with a syllogism about Zayd and Amr. This fallacy often occurs when an accidental attribute is confused with a defining characteristic. Understanding it is crucial for sound reasoning in Islamic theology (kalām), where such errors can lead to invalid arguments about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Define the fallacy of accident
Read the author's definition: the fallacy occurs when something is taken as necessary (essential) when it is only accidental. Note the two subtypes: universal predication of accidents and taking an accidental relation as a ruling basis.
The term 'bil-'arad' literally means 'by accident'.
2
Parse the example syllogism
Identify the premises: 'Zayd is not Amr' (minor) and 'Amr is a human' (major). The conclusion: 'Zayd is not a human'. Determine the mood and figure: it is a first figure syllogism (Barbara would be universal affirmatives, but here minor is negative).
The middle term is 'human' (Amr).
3
Locate the accidental attribute
The attribute 'being not Amr' is accidental to Zayd – it is not part of Zayd's essence. The fallacy treats this accidental relation as if it transfers the predicate 'human' from Amr to Zayd.
Zayd's difference from Amr is a contingent fact.
4
Determine why the reasoning fails
According to the author, some say it is invalid because the minor premise is negative (violating the rule that the minor must be affirmative in the first figure) or because the middle term is not predicated of the minor but is part of its predicate. This makes the syllogism non-productive.
Others defend the conclusion as accidentally true, but the reasoning remains fallacious.
5
Recognize the fallacy in other contexts
The passage also shows a modified version that leads to self-contradiction (Zayd not being Zayd). Practice spotting similar fallacies in kalām arguments where accidental attributes of created things are applied to the Creator.
Always ask: Is the property essential or accidental?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الذي بالعرض فهو أن يؤخذ شيء عرض له معادية شيء على سبيل ما يعرض عروضًا غير واجب؛ فيؤخذ واجبًا تعرض له أعراض كثيرة فتجعل الأعراض بعضها محمولا على البعض في كل موضع، أو يعرض شيء لشيء فيؤخذ في حكمه مثل أن تقول إن زيدا غير عمرو، وعمرو إنسان فزيد غير إنسان؛ وهذا من جملة ما يتخبَّط فيه أهل الكلام في هذا الباب عند بعضهم أنه غلط لأنه غير منتج، ويكون من جهة الصورة لا من جهة المادة واحد بالعرض. وإنما هو غير منتج لأن الصغرى إما سالبة، وإما الأوسط ليس لمحمول لأصغر بل جزء محموله. وعند بعضهم إنه منتج نتيجة صادقة فإن زيدا غير إنسان ما؛ وهذا صحيح. فنقول إنه لما كان عمرا غيره، وأيضًا إنسان؛ فينتج أن عمرًا غير إنسان إذا كان عرض للإنسان إن كان غير عمرو: فأحدهما واحدا فلما كان زيدًا غير عمرو واحد الإنسان، وعمرو شيئًا واحدًا جعل زيدًا غير الإنسان أيضًا. وكذلك إذا قلت فجعل زيدًا إنسانًا، وكان أحد الإنسان وعمرو شيئًا واحدا، وعمر و غير زيد كان زيد غير زيد. ويمكن أن يخرج لهذا وجه آخر من باب ما بالعرض لكن بهذا كفاية.
As for the one from accident, it is that something is taken which has an accidental relation to something else, in a way that occurs non-necessarily; then it is taken as necessary, and many accidents occur to it, so some of the accidents are predicated of others in every position; or something occurs to something and then taken in its ruling, like when you say: 'Zayd is not Amr, and Amr is a human, so Zayd is not a human.' This is among what the theologians stumble in this chapter. According to some, it is a mistake because it is not productive, and it is from the aspect of form, not from the aspect of matter, and it is the fallacy of accident. It is not productive because the minor premise is either negative, or the middle term is not predicated of the minor but is part of its predicate. According to others, it is productive and yields a true conclusion, for 'Zayd is not a human' is true. So we say: since he is other than Amr, and also Amr is a human, it yields that Amr is not a human if it is an accident of human that he is other than Amr. So one of them is one, and since Zayd is other than Amr, one of humans, and Amr is one thing, Zayd is made not human as well. And similarly if you say: 'You made Zayd a human, and one of humans, and Amr one thing, and Amr is other than Zayd, then Zayd is other than Zayd.' Another aspect from the fallacy of accident can be given, but this suffices.
Source pages p. 587

Commentary
This passage defines the fallacy of accident (bil-'arad). The author begins by describing its logical structure: something is taken as necessary when it is only accidental. Two subtypes are mentioned: (1) taking many accidents and predicating them of each other universally, and (2) taking an accidental relation as a basis for a ruling. The example given is the syllogism: 'Zayd is not Amr; Amr is a human; therefore Zayd is not a human.' The author notes disagreement among theologians: some say it is invalid because the minor is negative or the middle term 'human' is not a predicate of the subject but part of the predicate; others say the conclusion is accidentally true but the reasoning is fallacious. The discussion then modifies the example to show how the fallacy can produce self-contradiction (Zayd not being himself). The key is that an accidental property of a thing (like 'being other than Amr') is wrongly taken as essential to the thing itself.

Key Terms
العرض
بالعرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the conclusion is false
B) Because the minor premise is negative or the middle term is part of the predicate
C) Because the major premise is false
D) Because the terms are equivocal

Key Takeaway
The fallacy of accident arises when an accidental attribute is treated as essential, leading to invalid categorical reasoning that must be identified and avoided in Islamic logical analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Fallacy of Bad Consideration of Predication
Author explains fallacy from mishandling predication modes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of bad consideration of predication and distinguish between absolute and conditional predication in Islamic logical arguments.

Core Idea
Ambiguity in predication mode (absolute vs. conditional) misleads reasoning.

In Islamic logic, the way a predicate is attached to a subject matters. A predicate can be taken with a condition (bi-sharṭ), absolutely (muṭlaq), essentially (fī nafsih), or accidentally (bil-'araḍ). Failing to distinguish these modes leads to the fallacy of bad consideration of predication. The author illustrates this with an argument about 'non-existent' and 'conjectured' to show how shifting meaning of 'existent' produces a deceptive inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the predicate and its possible modes
In any proposition, the predicate can be asserted under different conditions. For instance, 'existent' can mean absolutely existent, or existent in the mind (as a copula). Recognize that the same predicate term may carry different modes.
The passage lists: conditional (bi-sharṭ), absolute (muṭlaq), per se (fī nafsih), per accidens (bil-'araḍ).
2
Analyze the example given
Look at the argument: 'What is not existent is conjectured; every conjectured thing is existent.' The predicate 'existent' in the conclusion is used absolutely, but in the minor premise it is used as a mental existence (conjectured thing exists in the mind). This equivocation causes the fallacy.
The author warns: it is not the same to predicate 'existent' absolutely versus as a copula or 'something existent in some way'.
3
Apply the conditions from contradiction
Recall earlier rules about contradiction (tanāquḍ): they require same mode. Here, predication conditions must be consistent. Check whether the predicate is taken uniformly across premises and conclusion.
The author says these conditions must be observed in every predication in a premise or conclusion.
4
Recognize the fallacy in practice
When you encounter an argument in Islamic texts (e.g., kalam debates about divine attributes), verify whether the predicate is used in the same mode throughout. If not, the argument commits this fallacy.
Example: 'God is existent' can be absolute existence or existence as a mental concept; equivocation leads to error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الذي من سوء اعتبار الحمل فلأن المحمول قد يكون محمولا بشرط، وقد يكون مطلقا، وقد يكون محمولاً في نفسه، وقد يكون محمولاً بالعرض أعني محمولاً لأجل غيره كمن يقول إن ما ليس بموجود فهو مظنون، وكل مظنون هو موجود؛ فلأنه لا سواء أن يحمل الموجود على الإطلاق، وأن يحمل كأنه رابطة، أو كأنه موجود شيئًا ما . وكذلك فرق بين غير الموجود على الإطلاق وغير الموجود شيئًا ما . وكذلك إذا كان الحمل على جزء واحد على الكل أو على جزء آخر. والشرائط الأخرى قد مرَّ ذكرها في التناقض يجب أن يراعى في كل حمل كان في مقدمة أو نتيجة.
As for that from bad consideration of predication, it is because the predicate may be predicated with a condition, or absolutely, or predicated per se, or predicated per accidens, meaning predicated for the sake of another, like one who says: 'What is not existent is conjectured, and every conjectured thing is existent'; because it is not the same to predicate 'existent' absolutely, or to predicate it as a copula, or as something existent in some way. Similarly there is a difference between 'non-existent absolutely' and 'non-existent in some way'. Likewise if the predication is of one part on the whole or on another part. Other conditions have been mentioned in the discussion of contradiction and must be observed in every predication in a premise or conclusion.
Source pages p. 588

Commentary
This passage defines the fallacy of bad consideration of predication (sū' i'tibār al-ḥaml). The author explains that the predicate can be taken in various ways: conditionally (bi-sharṭ), absolutely (muṭlaq), essentially (fī nafsih), or accidentally (bil-'araḍ, meaning for the sake of another). The example given is the argument: 'What is not existent is conjectured; every conjectured thing is existent.' The fallacy arises because 'existent' is used ambiguously: absolute existence vs. existence as a mental object (copula). The author stresses that one must distinguish between 'non-existent absolutely' and 'non-existent in some way' (e.g., in the mind). Additionally, predication on a part from the whole vs. on another part must be handled carefully. The conditions previously stated in the discussion of contradiction (tanāquḍ) must be observed in every predication, whether in a premise or conclusion. This fallacy thus involves equivocating on the mode of predication.

The True Elenchus: Conditions for a Valid Refutation
Definition of true elenchus and its conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a refutation (taskīt) meets the conditions of a true elenchus and detect fallacies caused by neglecting those conditions.

Core Idea
A true elenchus contradicts meaning, not just words, across all aspects like predicate, modality, and tense.

In the study of fallacies, one subtle type arises from ignorance of elenchus (taskīt). The author defines what makes a refutation valid: it must address the meaning in all relevant logical dimensions. Understanding these conditions sharpens your ability to evaluate arguments in Islamic theological and juristic debates, ensuring refutations are sound and persuasive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term 'taskīt'
Find the Arabic term 'التسكيت' and note that it means elenchus—a refutation that silences the opponent. The passage distinguishes a 'true elenchus' from a defective one.
2
List the conditions for true elenchus
The passage states that true elenchus must contradict the meaning in the predicate (المحمول), subject (الموضوع), relation (الإضافة), modality (الجهة), tense (الزمان), and other aspects (as previously mentioned). Extract these conditions.
These are the elements that ensure the refutation addresses the opponent's actual claim.
3
Understand why neglect causes fallacy
If any of these aspects is ignored, the refutation becomes corrupted (يفسد). The fallacy is that the refutation misses its target—it may appear to contradict but does so inadequately, leading to a false sense of victory.
This fallacy is called 'fallacy of ignorance of elenchus' because the refuter lacks knowledge of how to properly execute a taskīt.
4
Recognize the overlap with verbal fallacies
The author notes that this fallacy can also be considered a verbal fallacy because the error often lies in wording—e.g., using a word that does not capture the intended meaning across all aspects.

The author notes that this type can also be considered a verbal fallacy because the error often lies in the wording used in the refutation. Thus, it straddles the line between material and verbal fallacies.

Fallacy of Illusion of the Converse of Concomitants
Author defines fallacy of confusing necessary and sufficient conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of illusion of the converse of concomitants (īhām 'aks al-lawāzim) in an argument and explain why it is invalid.

Core Idea
Mistaking a necessary condition for an equivalent condition is a fallacy of confusing one-way implication with two-way equivalence.

In this passage, the author introduces a specific sophism that occurs when reasoning about necessary conditions (lawāzim). The fallacy lies in assuming that if A is a necessary concomitant of B, then B is also a necessary concomitant of A. This error, called 'illusion of the converse,' is common in both formal logic and everyday reasoning, especially when dealing with sense perception. Understanding this fallacy helps you avoid flawed reasoning in Islamic sciences, such as in legal analogy (qiyās) or theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fallacy's name
The author calls it 'illusion of the converse of concomitants' (īhām 'aks al-lawāzim). Note that 'lawāzim' means concomitants—necessary attributes or conditions.
2
Define 'concomitant' (lāzim)
From the passage, a concomitant is 'every predicate of the whole, essential or accidental, and every necessary concomitant in conditional statements.' This is a broad definition covering essential and accidental predicates.
The term 'lāzim' here includes both logical consequences and accidental attributes.
3
Understand the error
The error is that 'it brings to mind that the malzūm is also necessary for its malzūm' – i.e., the thing that has the concomitant is thought to be a concomitant of that concomitant. In other words, if A implies B, one mistakenly thinks B also implies A.
This is assuming the converse without justification.
4
See the sensory source
The author notes this often comes from sense perception, giving an incomplete example: seeing something large and sweet and thinking that everything sweet is large.
The example is cut off but the intended fallacy is clear.
5
Recognize the fallacy in practice
In Islamic reasoning, this fallacy can appear when arguing about causes and effects or attributes. For instance, if wine is intoxicating (intoxication is a concomitant), one might wrongly conclude that everything intoxicating is wine.
This is the fallacy of illicit conversion in analogical reasoning.

As for that from the aspect of concomitants, the cause of it is illusion of the converse. I mean by 'concomitants' every predicate of the whole, essential or accidental, and every necessary concomitant of the subject in conditional statements. The error occurs through illusion of the converse, in that it brings to mind that the necessary condition is also necessary for its necessary condition. Most of that is from sense, like one who sees something not smaller and sweet and thinks that...
Source pages p. 588

Commentary
This passage begins the definition of the fallacy of illusion of the converse of concomitants (īhām 'aks al-lawāzim). The author explains that the cause is a mistaken reversal: thinking that if A is a necessary concomitant of B, then B is also a necessary concomitant of A. The term 'lawāzim' (concomitants) is defined broadly: any predicate of the whole (essential or accidental) and any necessary condition in conditional propositions. The error often arises from sensory perception, as in the incomplete example: seeing something that is not smaller (i.e., large) and sweet, and then erroneously concluding that whatever is sweet is large. The passage is cut off, but the intended fallacy is clear: mistaking a one-way implication for a two-way equivalence. This fallacy is common in reasoning about causes and effects or accidental properties.

The fallacy of illusion of the converse arises when one assumes that because A is a necessary condition for B, B is also a necessary condition for A; this is a common error in reasoning about relationships and especially in sense perception.

Fallacy from Ignorance of Taskīt
Author defines fallacy due to ignorance of proper refutation (taskīt) involving meaning, not just wording.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define real taskīt and identify whether a refutation addresses meaning or merely wording.

Core Idea
Real taskīt contradicts meaning, not just words.

This lesson addresses a subtle fallacy in debate: the error of thinking you have refuted an opponent when you have only contradicted their wording. The author explains that proper taskīt (silencing refutation) must engage with the meaning across multiple logical dimensions. Understanding this distinction is crucial for rigorous jadal and for avoiding superficial objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find 'taskīt' in the Arabic. Note that the author distinguishes between 'real taskīt' (التسكيت الحقيقي) and a shallow version that only targets wording.
Taskīt literally means 'silencing' – the goal is to render the opponent unable to respond.
2
Extract the dimensions of meaning
The author lists the aspects of meaning that real taskīt must address: predicate, subject, relation, modality, time, and other matters. Identify each in the passage.
These correspond to elements of a proposition: the subject (موضوع), predicate (محصول), and additional attributes like relation (إضافة), modality (جهة), and time (زمان).
3
Understand the fallacy condition
The passage states that neglecting any of these dimensions invalidates the refutation. That neglect is the core of this fallacy: ignorance of how to properly contradict in meaning.
The fallacy is not about the refutation being false, but about the debater lacking knowledge of complete taskīt.
4
Classify the fallacy type
The author notes that this fallacy can be considered a verbal fallacy because the error occurs in the wording – but it is rooted in ignorance of meaning dimensions. Reflect on why it belongs to verbal fallacies.
Verbal fallacies arise from ambiguous or equivocal language; here, the fallacy arises from an incomplete grasp of how words relate to meanings.

Sophistry and Fallacies
