5 min
The Fallacy of the Illusion of the Converse
Author explains fallacy due to confusing necessary concomitants, often from sensory or intellectual illusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of the illusion of the converse (īhām al-'aks) and distinguish it from valid logical consequences in Islamic arguments.

Core Idea
Not every necessary concomitant is reversible: the illusion of the converse is a common fallacy.

This lesson tackles a subtle but dangerous logical error: assuming that if A is a necessary consequence of B, then B is also a necessary consequence of A. The classical logicians called this 'īhām al-'aks—the illusion of the converse. Through sensory and intellectual examples, the author shows how easily this fallacy creeps into reasoning, especially when dealing with necessary concomitants (lawāzim). Mastering this distinction is crucial for sound theological and legal argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Understand necessary concomitant (lāzim)
A necessary concomitant is a predicate that necessarily follows from a subject, e.g., 'heat' is a necessary concomitant of 'fire' (if fire exists, heat exists). Not all concomitants are essential; some are accidental but still necessary in the sense of being always present under certain conditions.
In Islamic logic, lawāzim include both essential (dhātī) and accidental ('araḍī) concomitants.
2
Identify the illusion of the converse
The fallacy occurs when one mistakenly believes that the relationship is symmetric: if A is a concomitant of B, then B is also a concomitant of A. For example, 'small' and 'sweet' are both concomitants of 'honey', but one might think that everything small and flowing is sweet like honey.
The author stresses that this error arises from the mind being tricked by the apparent connection.
3
Distinguish sensory vs. intellectual errors
The author gives two categories: sensory-based errors (e.g., seeing small and sweet objects and generalizing) and intellectual errors (e.g., assuming that because every feverish person is hot, every hot person is feverish). The latter is purely from reasoning, not perception.
Intellectual errors are more dangerous because they seem logical but are not.
4
Apply the test of reversibility
When you encounter a claim of necessary concomitance, ask: Is the reverse also necessarily true? If not, the inference is invalid. For example, in kalam, 'every created thing is temporally originated' is true, but 'everything temporally originated is created' may be contested for divine attributes.
This test helps in evaluating arguments in jadal and usul al-fiqh.
5
Practice with Islamic examples
Take a valid necessary concomitance from fiqh: 'Every prohibition entails sin.' The converse 'every sin is prohibited' is generally true in sharia, but not identical. The fallacy would be to argue: 'This action causes sin, therefore it is prohibited' without considering other factors like necessity.
Use examples from your own Islamic studies to become alert to this fallacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الذي من جهة اللوازم فالسبب فيه إيهام العكس؛ وأعني باللوازم كل محمول على الكل ذاتي أو عرضي، وكل لازم للموضع في المتصلات. وإنما يغلط فيه إيهام العكس بأن يسيق إلى الذهن أن الملزوم أيضًا لازم لملزومه. وأكثر ذلك من قبل الحس كمن يرى شيئًا لا أصغر وحلوا فيظن أن كل واحد لازم للكل؛ فيظن أن كل سيال أصغر هو حلو أو عسل. وكذلك قول من يقول فلان يطوف بالليل فهو سارق. وقد يقع الغلط من جهة العقل لا من جهة الحس كمن يظن أن كل كائن له مبدأ يظن أن كل ما له مبدأ كائن مثلا يظن أن كل حار محموم لما رأى أن كل محموم حارًا.
As for that which is from the aspect of logical consequences (lawāzim), the cause in it is the illusion of the converse. I mean by 'consequences' every predicate upon the whole, essential or accidental, and every necessary concomitant of the condition in conditionals. The error occurs in it by the illusion of the converse: that it is brought to mind that the antecedent is also a necessary concomitant of its consequent. Most of that is from sense perception, like one who sees something small and sweet and supposes that each one is a necessary concomitant of the whole; so he supposes that every small flowing thing is sweet or honey. Likewise the saying of one who says: 'So-and-so walks at night, so he is a thief.' And the error may occur from the intellect, not from sense perception, like one who supposes that everything that comes to be has a beginning, and supposes that everything that has a beginning comes to be—for example, he supposes that everything hot is feverish because he saw that everything feverish is hot.
Source pages p. 588

Commentary
This passage discusses fallacies arising from logical consequences (al-lawāzim). The author explains that the cause is the illusion of the converse: thinking that if A is a necessary concomitant of B, then B is also a necessary concomitant of A. He distinguishes between sensory-based errors (e.g., assuming that because something is small and sweet, everything small and flowing is sweet like honey) and intellectual errors (e.g., assuming that because everything coming to be has a beginning, everything with a beginning comes to be; or because every feverish person is hot, every hot person is feverish). The fallacy lies in reversing the relationship.

Key Terms
لوازم
إيهام العكس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Every mammal is an animal, so every animal is a mammal
B) Every feverish person is hot, so every hot person is feverish
C) Every human is mortal, so Socrates is mortal
D) Every intoxicant is forbidden, so wine is forbidden

Key Takeaway
The fallacy of the illusion of the converse arises when a necessary concomitant relationship is reversed, assuming mutual necessity.

The Fallacy of Begging the Question (Muṣādara): A Definition
Author briefly defines fallacy of begging the question as inability to distinguish identity from difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of begging the question by detecting when a premise assumes the conclusion rather than providing independent support.

Core Idea
Begging the question occurs when you cannot tell the same from the different.

In this lesson, we examine the logical fallacy known as begging the question (muṣādara). The author gives a pithy definition: it is the inability to distinguish between identity and difference. This means that a person who commits this fallacy presents an argument where the premise is essentially identical to the conclusion—they have begged the question rather than proved it. Understanding this definition helps you spot circular reasoning in theological and legal debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fallacy's name
The Arabic term is muṣādara, meaning 'begging the question'. Note that it is a fallacy of assumption, not of form.
This fallacy is also known as petitio principii.
2
Deconstruct the definition
The author says it is 'inability to distinguish between 'it is it' (huwa huwa) and 'the other' (al-ghayr)'. So the fallacy occurs when the person treats what is actually identical to the conclusion as if it were a separate, supporting premise.
3
Apply the test
When you encounter an argument, ask: Is the premise essentially the same as the conclusion? If yes, the argument begs the question.
4
Distinguish from other fallacies
Begging the question is different from false premises or ambiguous terms; it is specifically about identity and difference.
Other fallacies like equivocation involve ambiguity, not identity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الذي بالمصادرة فقد علمتَ فتحققت أنه من العجز عن التفرقة بين هو هو والغير.
As for that which is by begging the question (muṣādara), you have known and verified that it is from the inability to distinguish between 'it is it' and 'the other'.
Source pages p. 589

Commentary
This very brief passage identifies the fallacy of begging the question (muṣādara). The author states that it arises from the inability to distinguish between identity and difference—that is, assuming what is to be proven or failing to see that the premises are equivalent to the conclusion. The phrase 'it is it' (huwa huwa) refers to identity, and 'the other' (al-ghayr) refers to distinctness. The fallacy occurs when the argument relies on a premise that is essentially the same as the conclusion.

Key Terms
مصادرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the root cause of the fallacy of begging the question?
A) Using false premises
B) Inability to distinguish between identity and difference
C) Ambiguous terms
D) Ignoring modal conditions

Key Takeaway
Begging the question is not just circular reasoning; it is the failure to see that the premise is identical to the conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the following argument from a fiqh debate: 'Riba is forbidden because it involves excess, and excess is forbidden.' Does this argument beg the question? Explain why or why not using the definition from this lesson.

Sophistry and Fallacies
intermediate
5 min
Fallacy from Taking Non-Cause as Cause
Author describes fallacy of assuming something is a cause when it is not, with a syllogism about soul and life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of taking non-cause as cause and distinguish between a syllogism being productive and being relevant to the intended conclusion.

Core Idea
A syllogism can be valid yet fail to prove what it intended.

This lesson examines the fallacy of 'taking what is not a cause as a cause' (waḍ' mā laysa bi 'illatin 'illah). The fallacy occurs when a syllogism produces a conclusion, but that conclusion is not the one the arguer intended. The author illustrates this with an example about the soul and life, showing that even if the syllogism is logically productive, it does not prove the desired point. Understanding this fallacy helps you critically evaluate arguments that may appear valid but actually miss the mark.

Guided Path
1
Identify the arguer's intended conclusion
The arguer wants to prove that the soul and life are not the same thing. Find the final statement that expresses this goal.
The intended conclusion is 'the soul and life are not the same thing'.
2
Extract the premises of the syllogism
Break down the argument into its component statements: 'If existence is absolute, then existence is opposite to corruption'; 'Death is corruption'; 'Life is existence in what lives'.
Note the conditional structure starting with 'if'.
3
Determine the actual conclusion drawn
The arguer says 'this is absurd' and then asserts the intended conclusion. But the actual logical conclusion from the premises is not stated explicitly; instead, the arguer leaps to the desired claim.
The syllogism does not actually lead to the claim about soul and life.
4
Analyze why the syllogism is productive but irrelevant
The syllogism yields a conclusion (e.g., 'life is existence in what lives'), but that conclusion does not address the identity of soul and life. The reasoning may be valid, but it does not serve the purpose.
Productivity (intāj) means the syllogism produces a conclusion; relevance is a separate matter.
5
Recognize the fallacy
The fallacy lies in assuming that because the syllogism is valid, its conclusion must be the intended one. The arguer has taken a premise that is not actually a cause (or reason) for the intended claim.
This fallacy is common in theological and legal debates where an argument proves something true but not the point at issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الذي من وضع ما ليس بعلة علة فذلك في الأقيسة الخلقية كمن يريد أن يبين أن النفس والحيوة ليسا شيئًا واحدا فيقول إن كان الكون مطلقا فكون ما مقابل لفساد ما؛ والموت فساد فالحياة كون فيما يحيا يكون؛ وهذا محال فليس النفس والحياة شيئًا واحدا. فهذا المحال إن كان مما قيل فيلزمه، وإن لم تكن النفس والحياة شيئًا واحدا. وهنا فإن القياس منتج لكن لا للمطلوب.
As for that which is from taking what is not a cause as a cause, that occurs in constructive syllogisms like one who wants to show that the soul and life are not the same thing, so he says: If existence is absolute, then existence of something is opposite to corruption of something; death is corruption, so life is existence in that which lives; this is absurd, therefore the soul and life are not the same. So this absurdity, if it was said, then it follows necessarily, even if the soul and life are not the same. Here the syllogism is productive but not for what is required.
Source pages p. 589

Commentary
This passage discusses the fallacy of taking what is not a cause as a cause (waḍ' mā laysa bi 'illatin 'illah). The author gives an example from hypothetical syllogisms (al-aqīsah al-khuluqiyyah). Someone tries to prove that the soul and life are not identical by constructing an argument: 'If existence is absolute, then the existence of something is opposite to the corruption of something; death is corruption; therefore life is existence in what lives; this is absurd; therefore the soul and life are not identical.' The author points out that even if the conclusion (that they are not identical) is true, the syllogism does not prove it correctly—it is productive (intāj) but not for the required conclusion. The fallacy is attributing causality to a premise that does not actually support the intended conclusion.

Key Terms
وضع ما ليس بعلة علة
قياس منتج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author identify as the problem in the example about soul and life?
A) The syllogism is invalid
B) The conclusion is false
C) The syllogism is productive but not for the required conclusion
D) The terms are ambiguous

Key Takeaway
A syllogism can be productive (producing a conclusion) yet fail to prove the intended claim, which is the fallacy of taking non-cause as cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the intended conclusion and the actual conclusion in the following argument: 'Every created thing has a beginning. The world is created. Therefore the world has a beginning. Therefore the world is not eternal.' Is there a fallacy of taking non-cause as cause? Explain.
?
Construct a valid syllogism about a ruling in fiqh (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is haram; khamr is an intoxicant; therefore khamr is haram'). Then modify it so that it commits the fallacy of taking non-cause as cause (e.g., changing the middle term to something that does not connect).

Sophistry and Fallacies
beginner
5 min
Fallacy from Combining Multiple Questions into One
Author describes fallacy of combining multiple questions into one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when multiple distinct issues are illegitimately combined into one question and explain why a single answer is impossible.

Core Idea
Combining distinct questions forces false dichotomy or contradiction.

In debate and reasoning, a common sophistry is to bundle several distinct issues into a single question, demanding a simple yes or no. The author explains this trick, showing that when rulings differ, no single answer can be truthful. This lesson equips you to recognize and defend against such traps.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question as presented
Look at the question being asked. Is it phrased as a single query expecting one answer? For example, 'Is this good or evil?' when the object contains both.
2
Check for distinct issues
Does the question actually cover multiple distinct matters with different rulings (ahkam)? In the example, goods and evils have opposite rulings.
3
Recognize the impossibility of one answer
Because the rulings differ, no single answer can be true for all parts. The author says 'their rulings are different and cannot bear a single answer.'
4
Note the sophist's trap
The sophist uses this to corner the respondent: if the respondent hesitates, they are called incapable; if they answer, they contradict themselves.
5
Apply the defense
When faced with such a question, break it down into separate issues and refuse to give a single answer. Demand separate questions for each distinct matter.
This preserves logical consistency and exposes the fallacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الذي من جمع المسائل في مسألة واحدة ليلتمس عنها جواب واحد؛ وأحكامها مختلفة لا تحتمل جوابًا واحدًا فيقع الغلط فيما فينتج منه المحال. وإذا اختلفت المسائل في المحمول والموضوع كل وقوع الشبهة في ذلك فلم يتروّج على المجيب، ولم يذعن لجواب واحد. وبالجملة فقد جمعوا أشياء مختلفة في السؤال فيستدلون على سبيل التعميم؛ فإن توقف المجيب نسبوه إلى العجز والتحير، وإن أجاب فأدوه إلى التناقض. وله أمثلة كثيرة منها ما إذا أخذت جملة واحدة من خيرات وشرور مثلا فقيل هل هي خير أم شر هل هي بيض أو سود وعلى هذا؛ فهذه السبعة هي الأقسام التي من جهة المعنى.
As for that which is from combining multiple questions into one question to seek a single answer—while their rulings are different and cannot bear a single answer—so error occurs in it and leads to absurdity. And when the questions differ in predicate and subject, each occurrence of doubt about that, then it does not become apparent to the respondent, and he does not submit to a single answer. In summary, they collect different things in the question and then infer in a generalizing manner; if the respondent pauses, they attribute it to inability and confusion, and if he answers, they lead him to contradiction. It has many examples, including if one takes a single group of good things and evil things, for example, and says: 'Is it good or evil? Is it white or black?', and so on. These seven are the divisions from the aspect of meaning.
Source pages p. 589

Commentary
This final passage describes a fallacy where multiple distinct issues are combined into a single question to force a single answer, knowing that different rulings apply. The author explains that because the predicates and subjects differ, the respondent cannot give one answer without contradiction. The trick is to generalize in the question so that hesitation implies incompetence and a direct answer leads to inconsistency. An example is asking about a mixed set of goods and evils: 'Is it good or evil?' The passage concludes that these seven (the previous sections plus this one) constitute the divisions of fallacies from the aspect of meaning.

Key Terms
جمع المسائل
أحكام مختلفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author identify as the key feature of the fallacy of combining multiple questions?
A) The questions are ambiguous
B) The questions have different rulings that cannot be answered with one answer
C) The questions are too long
D) The questions are rhetorical

Key Takeaway
Combining multiple distinct issues into a single question is a sophistical trick that prevents a truthful single answer, forcing contradiction or confusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a real example from Islamic jurisprudence where a single question combines multiple distinct rulings (e.g., 'Is this contract valid or invalid?' when it has mixed elements). Explain why it is fallacious and how to properly split the question.
?
In a debate about the Quran, consider the question 'Is the Quran eternal or created?' Identify if this combines multiple issues (e.g., essence vs. speech) and how a defender should respond.
